THE RED PAGE.
" Castro’s Last Sacrament."
WHAT is the balance of gain or loss to the
purchaser of this book who can recall
having encountered many of the stories
in the columns of The Bulletin ? From
readers' whose opinions really count in that deci-
sion the judgment will be so unanimous as to en-
title those who don’t count —except in mere
numbers—to some degree of sympathy. The
objection of the latter must confine itself to a loss
of freshness, a point which one frankly acknow-
ledges they are justified in advancing. Mr. Dor-
rington’s merits are of . a very pronounced kind,
but their very emphasis compels you to feel at
once the force of them, and leaves little that is
new to be gathered on a second perusal. His
chief element is force—intensity in style and
vigorous characterisation; but with subtlety he
does not concern himself. lam far from saying
that the book cannot be read a second time. It
has its own decided claims to that distinction.
But it will be read solely for the sake of renewing
old impressions. Some compensation will arise in
the fact that the impressions will become deeper.
But one feels that the reader possessed with a
forgetful memory gets his compensation on a scale
that is monstrously unfair. His claim on literature
is paid as often as he cares to present it. The
gain in getting these stories in book form is
that it gives us a distinct idea of the author’s
height—of his place in literature. Their collec-
tive impression is vivid and lasting. Of the
excellence of Mr. Dorrington’s stories as they
appeared in The Bulletin no reader could . be'
ignorant; •but, separated as they were by- fairly ,
long intervals, one neglected to sum' the valud of
his work.' There need be no -hesitation now
in asserting that collectively it makes the most
brilliant book of short stories written in Aus-
tralia.
The stories are not of one pattern. They repre-
sent a wide variety in matter and atmosphere,
treated with the thoroughness of one who kilows
his trade. It is this fact of the author’s extra-
ordinary range that , grows to be insistent as one
reads. It has been printed that Mr. Dorrington
has wandered far for his knowledge of life, and
lias drawn on his experience for some of his
stories. Yet the absolutely impersonal quality of
the book is - very noticeable. That is to say, the
author employs his art to such effect that you do
not feel he is dealing with things he has seen, but
with things be knows. Who but the man; who
knows could give with such conviction the
Egyptian atmosphere—as supplied to tourists —
that surrounds “ A Blind Man’s Holiday.” “ Come
in out of the desert, Messieurs,” runs the formula
of the “ oasis in the desert of morality, ” and the
words grip you like a rice. The reason is because
they are so thoroughly in character, they add to
and depend upon the atmosphere of the situation,
and the exaggeration of the invitation responds to
the exaggerations that surround the particular
phase of life to which the “ oasis ” belongs.
It is superfluous to enumerate the merits of this
book as judged from the conventional standard.
It has a wide knowledge of human nature, a
forcible and artistic method of employing that
knowledge, and it has all the other numerous un-
exalted qualities that are common to so many
books. Praise of that kind seldom climaxes in
anything more than that a book is readable. But
Mr. Dorringtoh’s stories have other claims to
attention. They demand to be considered from
the standpoint of what is best in literary art. To
adequately criticise the book solely in that aspect
would require numerous quotations and a more
detailed analysis of the stories than can be given,
here. Their variety is insistent, but in vigorous
vitality of characterisation they present an envi-
able sameness. Black men, yellow or white, are
drawn with equal facility and artistic complete-
ness. The following (wo extracts from “The
Curing of Little Jan Tromp ” are but small ex-
amples, but will tend to show with Vhat living
human figures Mr. Dorrington deals :
The pagoda under the blue-jungled spur showed a
glimmer of light. It was getting dark, and Tommy the
cook was singing below to Typhoo Shang; Typhoo
responded with thirty-six middling verses about a girl
named Soo Lee and a number of tame peacocks. The cook
gathered himself from the bunk and said that he ob-
jected to the introduction of poultry into songs, and
wound up by calling the Emperor of China a “ cow.”
I peeped below and saw Shang feeling for his knife sor-
rowfully, and pondering the six feet of cabin that
separated him from the cook. Shang was keener than a
Malay to smell an insult. His arm went out lazily, hut
there was murder dozing in his little black eyes.
“ There’ll be no scrapping here,” I said, sharply.
“And no bloomin’ mess to clean up,” said Horace,
flipping Shang in the eye with a banana. Shang smiled
sweetly ; the knife vanished up his sleeve like a trick.
1 Lillie passenger, him bully leper,” said Typhoo.
4 What’s that ? ” said I, leaning over. “ Say it again.”
“Lillie Jan Tlomp, him leper. Me gottee bluller in
Lillie Bluck-slee, him leper too. Hide him under cabbage.
Jan Tlomp leper.”
I called to a Dago hand. “ Send the passenger to me.”
Three minutes later Mohali was standing on the bridge.
He offered me a cigar ; I put it aside. ,
“You’ve played it pretty low on me,” I observed.
“But I’m damned if I’ll jig lepers up and down the
’Pelago ; it’s an outrage on my clean schooner, sir.”
He stepped back, holding the rail, and the jewels lit up
his fingers as if he’d just taken them from a kettle of
phosphorus.
“You speak of little Jan Tromp.”
“ Of the leper,” said I, “ that you covered with a pan-
ther’s skin. But ashore he goes, first rock in the Malacca.
I’d put him over now only I fear to pollute the Almighty’s
sea. I’m sorry, sir, but I’m not running a lazaret for the
benefit of my health.”
“ Mere Caucasian prejudice,” he remarked, firing up.
“ A rose, by any other name, would smell as sweet,” I
rapped out, remembering my schooling.”
The above give more than a hint of Kipling,
and similarity in style and atmosphere with the
work of that author is present in many of the
stories. But Ido not find it produces the irrita-
tion and antagonism that the work of a mere
imitator usually inspires. That I take to be
owing to the faot that the work can be compared
on equality with Kipling’s. If anyone doubts
this he need go no farther than the first paragraph
of the first story for matter to use for com-
parison. It is not contended that Kipling is
not the greater author. The palpitating human
note that invests his best work renders his posi-
tion unassailable. Other authors whose work
these stories recall are Stephen Crane, R. L.
Stevenson and Bret Harte. The latter two are
present in only a faint degree, but it was perhaps
inevitable that a writer whose best work centres
in the vitality of his characters should show some
similarity to Stephen Crane. There is material
for comparison in the first part of “The
Hunchback’s Syllogism.” That I regard as some
of the best work in the book, though it has not the
undercurrent of subtlety so frequently present in
the Arnerican author. But the tangled web of
ettuition surging in people who cannot express
themselves is brilliantly done.
' In his word pictures, Mr. Dorrington is entirely
himself. It is not that he sees deeply, but that
lie. reproduces vividly with a wondrous economy
of words. He invests with a high value that
which in other hands would be entirely common-
place.
A mackerel cloud dappled the purple silence above
close packed meadows.
The quick night blotted out the bush. Someone with
a lantern was singing in the gully below. The southern
express thundered across the bridged valley, smearing
the darkness with scarves of flame.
The current that flung him across jagged ironstone
reefs swept the woman high amongst weeds and sea-shells
under the lee of the island.
A thin red stream leaped across her mind’s black chaos,
opening into a million estuaries that swam east and west
amid down-swirling stars.
As evidence of the excellence of Mr. Dorring-
ton’s skill take his handling of a difficult scene
A silence came that stung- him like whips, and sharp
upon it an explosion that seemed to test the limits of
sound. The blind man quaked, and clutched his fore-
head. Suddenly a gleaming cen tre-piece seemed to shiver
the black roof above him, spilling a myriad stars about
the empty darkness. One luminous disc shot down to
his forehead ; he screamed, threw up his guard, but the
flame struck him where the peckings had been.
“ Oh God 1 I can see ! ”
“ What 1 ” said Velarney. “No ! . . but it is possible,”
he muttered. ,
Drummond leaped upwards, but Yelarney caught the
frail body and held it like a vice.
“ Be still, thou jumping jack, and . . . if it is really
so . . . be glad !”
Drummdnd clutched the strong wrists that pitined him
against the parapet. -
“Let go. I can see— see —do you hear? I’ve been shut
up in darkness for ten years. I won’t move a limb, I
promise you. Let’s look up, old man ; .even through ,
your fingers I see stars ; look at ’em ! look at ’em '! Was
it the water—or you ? The doctors told me the nerve .
had been simply paralysed by that cursed explosion—a
shock might ...”
He fought to catch a glimpse of sky, but Velarney
locked him mercilessly to the roof.
“ Never mind the stars ! Poets call them stars ; they
are merely tears, frozen tears of the unhappy blest. Be
still.! ”
Velarney shook him angrily, but the hand that caressed
his face thrilled him like a woman’s touch.
“ 1 pan Stffef-how strange—l never knew you were like
this—it is not your conjuring, is it? We’ve been pals,
Velarney, and I’ve helped you a bit. Don’t take away
the light! eh? you shan’t! my God, you shan’t 1 ”
. You will do well to remember that the above
situation is abnormal, that the author has no
beaten track to follow, but has had to depend
wholly upon himself. _ And, further, it must be
recognised that the incident required a certain
elevatio.n above mere facts to make it convincing
as literature. It is extremely improbable that a
•verbatim report of the dialogue of an actual occur-
rence would be convincing. Here the author has
taken the facts of the situation, rejected some and
polished the remainder. Even so, parts of the dia-
logue remain hard—so great is the resistance of the
situation, to literary treatment. But that little
whisper of, “ I never knew you were like this,”
vivifies the scene. It could have come only from
one with a delicate perception of what is real in
literature.
But Mr. Dorrington has faults, and of these the
most prominent is his too frequent use of the
superlative note, his readiness to deal in exaggera-
tions. In “La Revanche” you have as central
incident a Frenchman setting out to murder a
German for singing “Die Wacht am Rhein.” In
“The Mother of an Invention” the chief charac-
ter boasts that her son “Never put a finger on me
in his life,” and a few pages further on the son
takes her by the throat and flings her from him—
purely evidence of his manhood. A woman draws
this heroic picture of her husband : “He carried
me out of a building once—they said that the
shearers had fired it —and the lead spilled from
the open roof on t<s his naked hands, and he
laughed—my God ! he laughed—because he held
me in his arms.”
‘Similar blemishes are not uncommon, but are
perhaps almost inevitable in the work of an
author determined on extracting the last ounce of
vitality out of his characters. It is this tendency
that has led Mr. Dorrington into the error of
taking the central figure of “The Mother of an
Invention ” out of her legitimate atmosphere. She
•is presented as a woman who has been reared in
lower-class poverty with an horizon of limitless
hard work. She is ungrammatical and speaks in
the vernacular of her class, but. there her fidelity
to her surroundings ends. For the rest you see
her sparring for literary effect, choosing telling
phrases with a nice distinction. It is in this story
that Mr. Dorrington, usually indifferent to the
ordinary tricks of the trade of fiction in building a
story, leaves too much of the framework visible.
In other stories he ignores, not always with the
best effect, that very elementary rule of the craft
of not taking the reader too much into your con-
fidence. In “The Sign of the Cross” the reader
knows in the first page of the wife’s infidelity,
instead of getting it as part of the climax. To
reserve it for that would strengthen the effect,
but would necessitate an altered construction. I
do not plead very strongly for that, as the story
is a very neat little piece of work as it stands.
As it is not intended that I should exhaust my
impressions regarding the book, this notice may
close here, without, among other things, any
examples of the excellences of Mr. Dorrington’s.
humor. Nor shall I offer any explanation, of how
the author manages to get so much out of his
characters, while dealing with them for the most
part as they present themselves to the outside
world, not as they appear to themselves. It
remains only to be said that, regarded purely as
literature, tne book represents a higher achieve-
ment than anything we have had in Australia.
But, if the stories be judged by a standard of per-
fection, there is, despite their many excellencies,
always a little to of. Yet how few
books are worthy of being judged by such a
standard! That “Castro’s Last Sacrament”
belongs to the limited category is surely a suffi-
ciently emphatic guarantee of its quality.
Q.
JOHN REAY WATSON.
Three of the tribe of third-rate story-books
wander in for notice from George Robertson Pro-
prietary, booksellers, Sydney. They can be read
without much pleasure, and forgotten without any
regret. “The Heart’s Highway,” by Mary E.
Wilkins (John Murray) is one of the more or less
historical romances that have such a vogue in
America, and are praised as masterpieces of fiction
in the American reviews. A large semi-literate
population buys them because they are “ just
out,” and nobly puffed, and there is apparently
nothing very much better to buy. Then the next
“ masterpiece appears, and they die, and bury
themselves in the heap. A certain amount of
skill and labour goes to the making of them, but
they want Life and the Breath of Genius; and
these things are rare. “Rose Island,” by
Clark Russell (Longmans) is good-enough sea-
stuff, but not as good as it might be and has
been. A shade of artificiality has sallowed
Russell’s sea-phrasing nowadays ; he has been
told so often that 'bis descriptions of waves and
ships are the real thing that he remembers his
lauded skill as he writes—and writes with more
obvious effort. Better than buying “Rose
Island” is to read “The Wreck of the
Grosvenor”again. Frank Bullen’s ‘‘The Men
of the Merchant Service ” (Macmillan) is the best
of the trio, and it can only be called a story-book
by courtesy. It aims to throw, light on the con-
ditions, qualifications, and duties of officers and
crew on a merchant sailer; and does throw.
Bullen writes readably, and his personal reminis-
. cences are interesting—frequently* trustworthy.
B. can make a lie look so truthful, or a truth look
so lieful, that one is never quitp sure whether to
add a grain of salt or a sack. His slobbered
piety, too, is so unctuous that The Spectator and
The British Weekly , which 1 like, their piety thick
and slab, cannot find words enough to praise him.
But indeed he has seen most of the wonders of the
deep, and colouring with imagination and saucing
with good emotional rhetoric, he makes those won-
ders sell large editions. Nearly everybody in-
terested in ships and sailors will be interested in
his new hook.
■ [Hm ! those three volumes don’t size up to much.
Booksellers and publishers rarely rush to provide
this page with “ review copies”; and tills particu-
lar example of b.are justice isn’t likely to encour-
age them. But there are already too-many re-
viewers busily tempering the wind of criticism to
the born duffer and the cheap puffer. (Mem. for
the unintelligent: Haven’t said or implied that
Wilkins or Russell or Bullen is a duffer or a
puffer ; though B. does puff ; and why shouldn’t
he ?.- This is no age-for the modest literary violet:
you have to be a brazen sunflower if you wislvto
he seen. All the same ——]
Writes “ lgnotus ”
Cannot sympathise, with your denunciation of Kipling,
but wish he would be more careful. His ode to the Com-
monwealth is disfigured by allusion to the “ notched
Kaikouras.” They are no more in the Commonwealth
than is Fusiyania or Table Mountain. I note these
schoolboy errors everywhere now. The Laureate,' fen the
S’African war, says it is time to' “ cicatkise thri sear.”
Surely,,if he only thought just a minute, he would re-
member that a scar' is a wound • which has' been
cicatrised. A sear is a ' cicatrix. ' But, then, if he
had thought, I suppose he would not have pub-
lished the stuff at all. As to Marie Corelli, ‘ in
“ The Master Christian/’ with her description of a ring
set in “ rose brilliants,” one does not wonder. One tells
one’s little sister to remember that a rose has a pointed
top and is generally of small value; a brilliant has a flat
top. You will say I am too microscopic, that I am looking
for defects. I reply, No. When a knife won’t cut’ l use a-;'
lens, I see the edge notched or blunt. Similarly, when a
poem or novel won’t hold me, when I get a blurred pic-
ture, I look closely and find the cause, Small
inaccuracies make the result unconvincing: I will give
you another instance. In “Red Pottage” there is a
lot of good work. But' the whole thing is based on
■an ignorance of usage which shocks'me. A husband
takes liis friend aside, and proposes they shall draw lots
who shall kill himself. The friend at once accepts the
situation, thereby at once confessing that he has been
guilty. No#, according to accepted usage, he. should
have first denied the charge, .for the woman’s sake. A
man who is charged with theft, may confess, if he likes ;
he is a fool if he does, but it is his own funeral. A man .
charged with over-intimacy with a married woman, on no'
evidence, is in honour bpund to lie. • Unless he at once
denies it, he. is an abominable cur, and forfeits our respect
instantly. In the same book a child swallows a half-
penny, and has to be held upside-down and thumped
until the coin drops out. But, if swallowed, you could
not recover it that way ; and* if lodged in the windpipe,
the child would itqt be able to speak, and say it Was
moving. These little things make the book, to me, un-
real.
A Brunswick (Vic.) doctor reports that a con-
finement-patient, when paying her account* asked
if he'gave any coupons. Our professional dignity !
N.S.W. Premier Lyne: “ Marie Corelli is high
above any writer of the present day that I know
of.” How like clings to like ! Corelli’s status as
a writer is precisely Lyne’s status as a politician
—Arcades ambo ! •
A Terrible Thing has happened. Somebody
has disinterred the fact that Richard le Gallienne
grew to manhood (if ever) as Dick Gallon, and
was known to the Liverpool youth of his epoch as
“Four Quarts.” The ladylike poet has been
forced to “ explain ” that his grandfather’s name
was Le Gallienne; that his father called himself
Gallienne ; and that Gallienne was corrupted to
Gallon. When he became a man and a poet (if
ever) he turned from the measurable to the
modish. Yet the dove-coteries,remain uneasy. >
The search for an ideal trio of “the time and
the place and the loved one all together ” brought
nothing worth print. Efforts were made by
Cynic; Lover; Eagleliawk ; E. W.D. ; 'Nowra;
Arab. G. ; R.H.A..; K.T. ; A.D. ; Ahau; J.M. ;
—and most of. them merely desecrated one’s
imaginary shrine. Thus .
Let it come—now. We are together -ALTOGETHER !
... I drink thirstily the hot lemm, . upheaved by the
mighty convulsion within her generous, glorious bosom.
... A soft murmur, something akin to pain, escapes
between the feasting lips. Then ......
And so oh—any man to any street-walker.

“Alex. M.” : J?e that “cockney rhyme”
(“S.-T.S.” B. 6/10/’00): —
I should like, as . a close observer in; this respect, to
point out that, while many Scottish and Irish persons
over-roll the “ r,” as in “ corrnerr,” and the cockney, with
his “ cawnah,” eliminates it entirely—between these ex-
tremes there is a tertium quid', the inability to appreciate
which I. first 'observed in the case of fairly-cuitivated
English people who could perceive no sound-difference
whatever between: “ father” and “'farther.” The ranker
kind of cockney, indeed, not only eliminates the r-sound in
some places but interpolates it superfluously in others;
so that he was going to the “ cawnah ” aforesaid “ to meet
Maurd.” But, "outside such gross vulgarism as this, there
are very many reasonably-refined English people who are
cockneys to the extent that—like“S.T.S.”—they would
phonetically render “morn’’and “dawn” by identical
symbols (bar the first letter) and would think “or”
adequately represented by “ aw.” Let us experiment
orally. In ending the word “ cawn ” (Corn) the tongue-
tip is touched quickly and lightly against the spot just
above the upper incisors, and as quickly withdrawn ;
while in “ corrn ” the tongue-tip does not reach this “ n ”
spot until, by an arching movement of the body of the
tongue, accompanied by a forcing of air along the mouth-
roof, the tip has been sharply “ trilled ” for an infinitesi-
mal portion of time. But, in “ corn ” normal, the tongue
is rapidly retracted and raised near the root, while the
tip very slightly touches the mouth-roof rather further
back than in either of the other cases, the air being at
the same time lightly forced over the tip with just that
suspicion of vibration which Prof. Saintsbury could not
understand that the trained ear should fail to recognise.

The Roman newspaper, Giorno, which is Gabriele
d’Ahnunzio’s oracle, announces that November, 1900, will
see the first three books of his new work, “ Laudi del
Oielo, del Mare, della Terra e degli Eroi.” The first book
contains a long poem, a kipd of “ lyrical presentation of
the poet’s life,” and the second odes in praise of Michael
Angelo, Leonardo, the old sea republics and the cities of
Italy. The third book will be in honor of the
and contains two poems, “Glauco” and “ L’Oleandro.”
D’Annunzio has also been working for a long time at a
new no'vel, the second of the “Lily” series. It is. to
appear in December. And besides all this he is just
-putting the finishing touches to a new drama, “Fran-
cesca da Rimini,”, and,,has already made arrangements
with companies who are to play it in Europe and America.
Voila ! the exuberance of the Titan and the Overman.
But, as Sir Joshua Reynolds used to exclaim, -‘‘The
quality of it! damme, what’s the quality, man 1 ”

“ H.” : I have been reading G. F. Train’s
“Young America Abroad.” This book, composed
of hastily-dashed-off letters to a Boston paper,
includes a series from Melbourne, where Train
lived for 33 months, beginning in 1853. He paid
£l5 a week for board and lodging at the Prince of
Wales Hotel, the Menzies’ or Scott’s of the day.
This hotel was in Flinders-lane, standing back
from the pathway, opposite where the Cathedral
is now. Train makes a droll mistake, gravely
saying that Lord Melbourne came up the Yarra
and founded the city.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Sunset Gate.
In truth, the half-lit life I told you of,
Its very shadows are a sweetness to discover;
Love dies if all be clear as day to love, —
Noon and the. high-road ill befit the lover.
It is composed of interwoven dreams,
A deeper deep suggests, a higher height discloses ;
An avenue it is of varied gleams,
Bordered on either hand by startling roses.
One needs a noiseless tread and gentle arm
To walk such secret ways and capture all the
prizes,
An eye for subtlety and secret charm,
An ear alert for musical surprises.
You noticed at the close of yesterday
How all your wooers—heart and mind intent on
bringing
Love tribute to you—had no word to say
Of that loud sunset and the small bird singing.
But then, just there, we touched a kindred
thought,
A glance, a flash that kindled to a revelation ;
And you were mine, and mine alone, and nought
Could bring to nought the moment’s swift elation.
It pleasured you to find the moment bring
A mind receptive to the golden hour’s revealing ;
And I ?—-well, couple close a slave and king
(The slave for me) and you may guess my feeling.
Recurs to me an unused garden gate,
From casual passers-by with leafy tresses hidden,
That once I chanced upon alone and late,
And entered with a sudden step unbidden—
And found a haunting grace in every grove
And winding way, with merry lights and fancies
winging
About the place—the gate to which, O Love,
Was that loud sunset and the small bird singing.
N.s.w.
RODERIC QUINN.
Straws on the Stream.
The sunlight, through the willows,
Laid patterns quaintly shaped
Across the clear, cool water
That flowed, now shadow-draped,
Now flashing ringing ripples
Like waves of thought or speech
That imitate some master
Whose height they cannot reach.
And on the bridge she loitered—
A lily framed in green—
The light on amber tresses
Throwing a golden sheen ;
And lazily, in silence,
As in a pleasant dream
We watched two spinning grass-blades
Sweep down the running stream.
Passing the bridge together
Onward they leapt and sped,
Now one to rearward lagging,
Then rushing far ahead !
One circling in an eddy,
The other for a space
Delaying in slack waters,
Then skimming on apace.
So, never far dissevered,
Yet never side by side,
They took the rapids wildly
And swung the corners wide ;
Till in a pool where brightly
There gleamed the royal sun
They inward swerved and gaily
Were borne along as one.
I turned to her and whispered,
“ Those straws are you and I.
For years I’ve travelled lonely,
And, sweetheart, you know why !
On Life’s broad, rapid river
We are as grass-blades thrown.
Speak ! shall we cling together, •
Or must I drift alone ? ”
With eyes devoid of laughter,
Yet full of Love’s grave smile,
She answered, “ Long and weary
Is every lonely mile ;
And hark ! I hear the ripples
And willows sing this strain,
‘ Drift on, drift on together !
Why must you part again ? ’ ”
Though shadows on the water
Were beautiful and clear,
Though softly crooned the river
I did not see or hear
Aught save her eyes’ shy glances,
Her heart’s fast, happy beat.
Close, close I held and kissed her
And Life and Love were sweet.
M.L.

Yankee Doodle at the Front!
An incident in the Boer war as related by a returned Officer.
A regiment of our Australian infantry -was travelling (as depicted in
the smaller photograph) to Belmont. At Graspan we were
entertained by the
garrison of Gordon.
Highlanders sta-
tioned there, and
when taking our
departure by rail
were accorded the
customary musical
send - off by the
garrison band,
which, on this occa-
sion, consisted of
two drummers and
a piper. As our
train moved off I
heard the cry on
all sides from our
boys —‘Yankee Doodle!!’ ‘Yankee Doodle!.!’ and there in the distance
was an almost exact representation of the Yankee Doodle tobacco brand
so familiar to' all Australians. The band after seeing us aboard were
returning back along the road to camp, and as we passed they sturdily
marched and played, whilst we, with the familiar tableau before us
reminding us so of home, cheered wildly. I instantly took out my kodak,
and, although the train was moving fast, secured a fairly good picture,
with the idea that perhaps my friends (the Dixsons) in Australia would like
to have it. On many a weary march afterwards, when smoking the army
regulation tobacco, did we long for just one pipe of the old far-off favorite.”

NOTICE.
By sending direct to The Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
■week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and all Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.
Literary communications to be addressed to J. F.
Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns m length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, DECEMBER 15, 1900.
A Policy for the Commonwealth :
The Differential Tariff Idea.
_ _
Even at this early stage of Federal politics
there is a great upheaval of more or less
Jinuo politicians demanding that the Com-
monwealth shall give special trade advantages
to Britain as against the outside world in
general. The proposal has taken no definite
shape as yet, and the details require filling
in, but the central idea is that the Common-
wealth should charge lower duties on British
than on non-British goods, and by thus—to
the extent of the difference in the duty—
shielding the British manufacturer against
the foreigner afford that same British manu-
facturer Protection in Australia which he
doesn’t get in his own country. The spec-
tacle of Australia going out of its way to
afford Protection to a Freetrade country,
and the spectacle of a Freetrade country
grabbing with both hands at the benefits of
a Protective tariff while professing to have
long since seen and abjured the errors of
Protection, may look just a trifle humorous,
but that is another story. If Britain
chooses to look humorous it is Britain’s own
affair.
* * * *
Regarded as a purely business proposition
there appears to be no reason worth men-
tioning why Australia should not admit
British products at lower duties than the
products of other countries—provided Britain
is prepared to reciprocate. But Britain
must reciprocate, or it won’t be a fair deal,
If Australia fosters Britain’s products by
giving them a special advantage in its mar-
kets over the products of France, Germany,
&c. then Britain may justly be called upon
to respond by giving Australian products an
equal advantage in its markets over
the products of France, Germany, and
other countries. As matters stand at
present the benefits and advantages are
all in favor of Britain. Australia buys
more of Britain’s goods than Britain buys of
Australia’s. In 1899 the States of the
Commonwealth bought about £21,000,000
worth of imports, other than gold, from
Britain, and Britain bought about
£17,000,000 or £18,000,000 worth of goods,
other than gold, from the Commonwealth.
On paper it certainly looks as if Britain
bought more largely from Australia than
Australia does from Britain, but the several
millions worth of gold which Australia sends
annually to Britain (or to other people on
Britain’s account) towards paying interest
on public and private debts, and as rents to
absentees, isn’t a purchase made by Britain
from this country, nor is it commerce in any
reasonable sense of the term. Each of the
Australian States admits Britain’s goods on
precisely the same terms as it does those
of all other countries. Britain admits Aus-
tralian goods on the same terms as those
of other States, and levies the same heavy
tax on Australian wines and Victorian
brandy as it does on French and Spanish
wines or brandy. And each is, and
always has been, guided by purely selfish
motives. Five of the Commonwealth States
are Protective because they believe Protec-
tion pays them best. Britain and N.S.W.
are Revenue-Tariff States because they be-
lieve that Re veil ue-Tariffisin pays them best,
and for no other reason. There isn’t a
penn’orth of sentiment in the case on either
side. Foreign-trade advocates publish sta-
tistics, just as Protectionist ones do, to prove
that, by their policy, they are personally
getting ahead of their trade rivals—are per-
sonally grasping the other fellow’s money
for their own sordid pockets, and over-
reaching and ruining the other fellow for
their own sordid benefit. Foreign-traders
certainly allege that theirs is a policy of
brotherly love—but their statistics never
attempt to prove anything else than that
their policy is the best policy for grabbing
the brother’s wealth and leaving the brother
in the lurch.
* * ¥ ¥
It is necessary to point out these things in
order to put the matter on its right basis—
the purely business one. The fact that
Australia has always been a far better cus-
tomer to Britain than Britain has been to
Australia, and that the obligation has been
wholly on Britain’s side, has never evoked
the least sign of consideration in trade
matters from Britain towards this country.
Australia has met its obligations to its prin-
cipal creditor with scrupulous exactness—
and its wool is admitted to England on no
better terms than that of Argentina, which
cheerfully defaulted and left the British
bondholder on the rocks. It has shown
itself on various occasions frenziedly loyal to
Britain, and its wines and wheat receive not
one pennyworth more consideration than
those of Britain’s worst enemies—Russia and
France. It has cost Britain nothing in active
defence; and it is treated not in the least
more kindly than France, which cost Britain
£600,000,000 in war expenses. Britain has
spent enormous sums indefenceof Turkey and
Portugal—the bill for one day’s campaigning
in aid of either of these lands amounted
to more than the defence of the Common-
wealth States cost Britain in a century. And
Turkey and Portugal gave their benefactor
no aid in return, and promptly swindled her
out of much loan money to boot; yet her
consideration for them in trade matters is
quite as tender as her consideration for these
States. The U.S., which fought her in two
wars, andbrokeher empire asunder, and which
sells much to Britain and buys hardly any-
thing, gets exactly the same treatment as we
do. And it was Britain which inaugurated
the policy of taxing the products of her
colonies, and which put things on this un-
friendly basis. Under all these circum-
stances it would be interesting to know what
Britain’s share of the proposed differential
tariff system is to be.
* • * *
Obviously if it is to be a matter of senti-
ment the sentiment should be mutual. If it
is to be a matter of business the business
advantages should be mutual. If the Aus-
tralian Commonwealth is willing to charge,
say, 5 per cent, more duty on the manu-
factured goods it buys from other countries
than on those it buys from Britain, Britain
should be prepared to reciprocate by charg-
ing 5 per cent, more on the grain, butter,
wine, wool and frozen and preserved meats
of other countries than it charges on those
of Australia. If Australia charges Britain’s
manufactures 20 per cent, all round while it
charges those of other countries 25 per cent,
it is only a fair return that Britain should
charge 5 per cent, on foreign wool, butter,
meat, grain, &c., while letting Australia’s
in gratis, and should give Australia a 5 per
cent, advantage on her wine and brandy
tariff. Even at these rates experience affords
every reason to believe that Australia will
buy rather more from Britain than Britain
buys from Australia, and so long as it does
that the terms are good enough as a basis
for any fair dealing.
* * * *
But if the concession is to be on one side
only—and judging from the language used
by some of its advocates that appears to be
actually contemplated—then it should, on
the basis of figures, be a one-sided con-
cession granted by Britain to Australia, not
a one-sided concession granted the other
way about. Apart from the export of cash
which goes towards paying interest on this
country’s British debts, and which is not in
any sense an exchange of commodities, this
country buys more from Britain than Britain
buys from it in return. Therefore if one
party is to give a special advantage it should
be Britain—with a view to encouraging the
further purchase of Australian commodities
in the United Kingdom in order to make the
trade even. But as all the discussion so far
is about the special terms which Australia
is to grant to Britain, at least let Britain
give something in return, if it is only
to avoid making the business a bur-
lesque. If Australia gives special assist-
ance to Britain by giving British products
5 or 10 per cent. Protection over the
products of other countries it is bestowing
on the British manufacturer the blessing of
Protection in Australia, which is
ridiculous enough without; any other ele-
ments entering into the case to make it
worse. To protect the Briton abroad when
he isn't protected at home —to protect
a man who says Protection is a foolish
and accursed thing—to show him up to the
world as a hypocrite who grabs with both
hands at the advantages of a system which,
he is always understood to allege, has no
advantages —to profess to be kinder to him
than he is to himself—all these are remark-
able proceedings. And if, on top of all this,
Australia gives Britain this special aid and
advantage without getting any equivalent,
then, in addition, it puts its massive parent in
the absurd situation of receiving pauper re-
lief from this young dependency. And that
is still more ridiculous.
* * * *
Why the subject of a differential tariff
should have risen so violently just now,
more than at any other time, is diffi-
cult to explain. The fact that Australia
has just spent a good deal of money over a
British war is no visible reason why it should
feel called on at this special moment to
make other sacrifices for Britain’s bene-
fit. If Britain was desirous to offer some
special assistance of the kind to Australia
the situation would be more understandable.
The time is inopportune in another sense ;
Australia doesn’t know for certain yet how
it will draft a Federal tariff that will make
ends meet even without granting special
concessions to anybody, and before it talks
of sacrificing customs revenue, even for
Britain’s benefit, it should find out with some
certainty what revenue there will be to sacri-
fice. This country has to be just before
it is generous, and the necessity of having
enough money to pay the British creditor is
far more conspicuous than the need to make
a superfluous gift to the British manufacturer.
Further, if Australia does commit the
amusing folly of giving Britain this special
trade advantage without getting any similar
benefit in return, it is at least to be hoped
that Britain can supply some means of
adequately distinguishing between its own
goods and those of foreigners that have merely
passed through England’s open ports on
their way hither. And it would be well to
sit down in a cool place and reflect seriously
as to whether this special advantage is to be
bestowed on the United Kingdom only, or
on all the British possessions. If it is to be
bestowed on all the countries that make up
the British Empire—the Empire wherein
Britain itself is only a speck both as to area
and population —are all these countries to
come in on the same terms as Britain ? Is
Canada, for instance, to get a special advant-
age in this market without giving one in
return, and if so what has Canada done for
this country to deserve such consideration ?
And if it is proposed to give Britain the
advantage of a differential tariff without any
direct return, and merely by way of ex-
pressing this country’s natural love for its
parent, is it to be thereby understood that
Britain has no natural love for its descend-
ant ?
• * * *
Also, if the concession is intended as some
return for Britain’s protection, which isn’t
sentiment but pure business, it would
be well to have that matter also put on an
understandable basis. As matters stand,
Australia has supplied Britain with a stu-
pendous amount of money. A very large
number of needy Britons made their pile out
here in the golden days, and took it home to
enrich the United Kingdom, and this place
has paid exorbitant interest to an immense
number of British investors, and done much
for the mother-land in the way of supplying
a field for investment. It has paid much
more than it has received for the active
defence of the empire, and it has
done more for British shippers and manu-
facturers than any other population of its
size on this earth. If it is held that there is
still a balance owing for the passive protec-
tion afforded by Britain, then if the pro-
posed tariff concession is granted let it be
at least understood whether it is to square
the debt, or partly square it, or whether it
is a gift and Britain is to go on de-
manding extra naval contributions from
this country, just as if no such concession
had been made. If it is all a matter of
business, let the account be properly drawn
out and audited and receipted. And if it is
sentiment, at least let the sentiment be in-
telligible. Hitherto Britain has given as
good terms, in commercial matters, to the
hostile Frenchman and Russian, and the
bloodthirsty and fraudulent Turk, as she
has to Australia. If the differential-tariff
idea is to be wholly one-sided, then these
people will, upon the whole, in their deal-
ings with Britain, be receiving better terms
than Australia does. Perhaps there is no
reason why Australia should objeet to this
arrangement, but it should at all events
understand that there is such an arrange-
ment.
A Cheap and Handy State Constitution.
When the Commonwealth Government is fairly
established, the six States—so it is already freely
admitted —require something new in the way of
Constitutions. Pomp and circumstance are not
essential ; the Federal Parliament can furnish
any pomp that,may be needed, and in sufficient
quantities to supply the wants of the whole
Commonwealth. The great essential is a cheap,
useful, working constitution. It isn’t necessary
that the State Legislature should consist of two
Houses if one will do the work equally well.
There is nothing specially sacred about two
Houses, or one, or seven, or any other number;
it is merely a question of getting the work done
efficiently, and if it can be best done with 147
Houses of Parliament then there should be 147.
If Australia went by its own bitter experience it
would take the first opportunity to be done with
the bi-cameral system for good. In its history half
the time of the Assembly has generally been
occupied in fighting the Council, and the only
times and localities that have shown much results
in the way of peace and progress have been those
rare places and occasions when the Upper House
was so effectually suppressed, or so completely in
unison with its neighbor, that it ceased to have
any mind of its own. At these times it has been
really a case of government by one Chamber, and
it is a solemn fact that government by one Cham-
ber is the only kind that has been anything like a
success in Australia —not that it lias been much of
a success.
*** . * ■
Probably the reason why it wasn’t more of a
success was that it was government by the wrong
kind of Chamber. The essentials of suc-
cessful Parliamentary Government appear to
be these: There should be a certain number
of representatives chosen by the people—
the people in the widest sense of the word—
voting by constituencies. And there should also be
a certain number of representatives chosen by the
people—the same people—with the whole State
voting as one constituency. The first crowd is
wanted in order to make sure that each section of
the country will have a delegate who knows its
special features, its interests and its requirements,
and who will demand for it a fair share of con-
sideration and cash. The second crowd is
wanted quite as urgently to make sure that the
interests which affect the country at large will not
be lost sight of in the exclusive consideration of
the interests of Big Scrub or Boppy Mountain. It
is the interest of N.S. W. in general to see that
the burden of the public debt is not heaped too
high, while it is the business of the local repre-
sentative to let the burden heap up as it may so
long as Big Scrub gets its own railway out of
the next loan, and he dare not protest lest
the obvious retort should be that, in deference to
his desire for economy, the proposed Big Scrub line
has been abandoned. It is the interest of N.S.W.
at large to see that new lines are built where they
will pay best and do the most good to the most
people ; while it is the business of the purely local
man to look after getting his own line, whether
it will pay for axle-grease or not. And when
there are no men to look after the interests of
N.S.W. in general, and 125 men to look after the
interests of 125 fractions of it, and to grind each
other’s little parochial axes in exchange for assist-
ance in the grinding of their own, the interests of
N.S.W. in general have a bad time.
* * * *
The elective Upper House, as it exists in Vic-
toria, Tasmania, S.A., and Westralia, does not re-
present the State at large, for it is elected, like
the Assembly, by constituencies. The nominee
one, as it exists in N.S.W. and Queensland,
doesn’t represent the State at all; it merely
represents the various Ministries—mostly dead
and gone and forgotten —which created it.
A great part of these two Upper Houses
represents only Parkes, who is dead, and M‘ll-
wraith, who is dead also. The Federal Senate
comes nearer to the ideal Upper House than any
other. But after all there seems no reason why
the State Legislatures should burden themselves
with the pomp and glory of two Houses when one
would do the work equally well. Five out of the
seven Canadian provinces have only one House of
local Legislature, and nothing has happened there
to show that government by one House is not
perfectly feasible. No one has dreamed of divid-
ing the London County Council, or any other
municipal body which looks after the concerns of
a community as large as, or larger than, N.S.W.,
into two Houses. Why the bi-cameral system
should be a fetish, or the uni-cameral system be
regarded with such profound dread, passes com-
prehension. Both have failed and both have suc-
ceeded according to the character of the people
who ran the show; but no country which failed
to govern itself decently with one Chamber has
governed itself any better merely through estab-
lishing two.
* • • *
Therefore The Bulletin desires to suggest, for
the consideration of State politicians, that the
future State Legislatures consist of one Chamber
only—half or perhaps two-thirds of its members
elected by separate constituencies as at present,
the other half or one-third elected by the State as
a whole. Such a Chamber would represent both
interests, the parochial and the national. It
would include such men as Barton and Wise,
who can get an overwhelming vote from
the State as a whole because they are regarded
as best qualified to look after the interests of the
State as a whole, but who can only get a pre-
carious footing in any constituency because each
constituency thinks them too big and intelligent
and far-seeing and generally patriotic to sink to
the level of the local culvert, and to yell per-
sistently for a new gate-post at the local lock-up.
And it would also include such apparently
unavoidable evils as Pumpkin Smith, who looks
manfully after the culvert and the gatepost, and
nothing else. It wouldn’t be a case of one
House looking solely at the parochial aspect
of things, and the other looking solely at
the national aspect, and colliding violently
in consequence; the one House would em-
body both aspects. Nor would it be at all like
the existing State Legislatures, where one House
looks at the parochial aspect of things, and the
other looks mainly at its own interests and
those of the propertied class it sprang from.
There would be no deadlocks in such a
Legislature. The Ministry would possess the
confidence of the whole State Parliament. There
would be no more cases of a Liberal Ministry and
a big Liberal majority in the Assembly having its
work persistently undone by a Tory majority in
the Council. There would be one set of officials
the less to pay, and a va.-,t mass of dreary conver-
sation the less to record. And out of the savings
it might be possible to so increase the pay of the
legislators as to give the country a chance of
getting really good men. The present pay of an
M.L.A., when all necessary outgoings are de-
ducted, is about that of a very moderate clerk,
and the country gets about the kind of intellect it
pays for.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Mr. “Justice” Hodges Once More.
In recently awarding punishments, at Melb.
Criminal Sessions, Mr. Justice Hodges reached a
height of absurdity that even the stolid dailies
were constrained to notice After sentencing
Howard Harrison, who killed his paramour at
Brunswick (he almost cut her head off), to two
years’ hard labor, his Honor apparently repented
of his leniency, and ladled out penalties hot and
strong. First came a lad in knickerbockers whom
Judge Hodges considered would have been fitly
punished-- for indecent assault—by a parental
thrashing, but, as the lad’s father was dead, the
Judge ordered that 25 strokes of the birch be
administered by the common hangman-and-
nagellator, with a month’s imprisonment to follow
Hodges satirically observing that this correction
would probably not be as efficacious as the
parental chastisement. Possibly not; but it
will certainly be much more so in
helping to degrade the recipient and pre-
pare him for a career of confirmed criminality.
Next, two youths, of 18 and 11), for shop-breaking,
got 18 and 12 months respectively; Mr. Hodges
telling the elder prisoner (with a previous con-
viction) that his sentence had been increased
because he (the Judge) believed he had committed
perjury while trying to exonerate his companion !
This was practically punishing a man for an
assumed crime of which he had never been con-
victed. A more monstrous abuse of justice is
scarcely conceivable! Mr. Hodges himself,
however, was staggered when a man whom he
sentenced to 18 months for indecent assault said :
“ Would your Honor make it two years, and
allow me to get some money and go out of the
country altogether when I have served the sen-
tence ? ” After considering the matter, his Honor
did “make it two”—thus making the sentence
equal to that of the practical murderer before
mentioned. It was Judge Hodges, by the way,
who last September sentenced a boy to 9 years
for indecent assault—subsequently amending the
period to 9 months !
—4.
Bursting of the Federal Capital
Conspiracy.
Attorney-General Wise, of N.S.W., recently
started shedding some new and unexpected light
upon the Federal problem. He obtained from
Mr. Alexander Oliver, President of the N.S.W.
Land Appeal Court, and published in the daily
papers, a two-column opinion re the alleged
theories of some alleged people who are stated to
have said or thought or dreamt that the Common-
wealth Constitution empowered the Common-
wealth Parliament to select a site for the capital
in N.S. W., and take possession of it without
asking the consent of N.S.W.’s State Parliament..
The Bulletin never heard of these people, but
that is a mere detail. The two-colunm opinion
sets forth, to begin with, that the Federal Parlia-
ment has to select the site, and then seek to
obtain the surrender of it from the N.S.W. State
Parliament, and that it can’t lawfully ignore
the latter body, or look at it with a disrespectful
eye. This is comforting to the purely parochial
crowd, so far as it goes, but it doesn’t go far.
For the tail-end of the opinion is to the effect
that the Federal Parliament is really boss
of the situation, after all, and can, under the
ordinary powers of land resumption, take and pay
for any site of 100 or 5000 square miles, or more,
provided it is over 100 miles from Sydney, even if
N.S.W.’s State Legislature refuses to part
with the same. That is a very bad smash
for the cheeky parochial party of which the
Sydney Telegraph is the representative—the
party which claims that N.S. W. State Parliament
has the sole right of selection, and that the
Federal Parliament and five out of the six States,
and nearly two-thirds of the Commonwealth popu-
lation, shall have no voice in the matter. The
situation is something like this :
(1) The Federal Legislature is to select a site in N.S.W.,
at least 100 miles from Sydney, and consisting of 100
square miles or any larger area, for the capital of all
Australia. There is no date fixed for making the selec-
tion.
(2) Then the N.S.W. Parliament may either agree, and
hand over the selected site, or refuse to hand it over.
(3) The Federal Parliament sits in Melbourne till the
site is selected and sufficiently prepared, so that the
Legislature can remove there.
(4) The Federal Legislature can’t make the N.S.W.
Legislature agree to hand over any site the latter isn’t
willing to hand over. Nor can the N.S.W. Legislature
compel the Federal Legislature to accept any site the
N.S.W. one is willing to hand over.
(5) If the'N.S.W. Legislature refuses to hand over the
site which the Federal Legislature selects, the latter may
cave in and c.loose another site. Or it may simply say :
“ We will take this site and no other, and the Federal
Parliament will go on sitting in Melbourne until you cave
in and surrender the site we have chosen. The Federal
Parliament will go on meeting in Melbourne for 500 years
if need be, till you do cave in.”
But—and this is where the Daily Telegraph
party falls in very badly—is seems there is another
alternative. Mr. Oliver’s opinion is that, though
the Federal Parliament must ask the State Par-
liament of N.S.W. to hand over the site which it
(the Federal Parliament) has selected, and though
it can’t make the N.S.W. institution agree to its
selection, it can, in the event of refusal, take the
site it has chosen without the permission of the
State Parliament after all. Instead of simply
saying that it will go on sitting in Melbourne till
the State Parliament comes over to its views it
may go in for a process of compulsory resumption.
Says Mr. Oliver:
If no agreement can be arrived at between the State
and the Federal Parliament, or if the State Parliament
declines to make any offer of territory, a reserve power is
vested in the Federal Parliament in the last resource of
legislating for the purpose of compulsorily appropriating
on just terms a suitable site for the seat of government.
Truly this a downfall. The Daily Telegraph
party selected Orange-Canoblas as the site for the
Federal city so that all the trade might go to
Sydney. It assured the public that N.S.W. is
sole boss of the situation because the N.S.W. State
Parliament must hand over a site before any
capital can be built, and by refusing to hand over
any site save Orange-Canoblas, it could compel
the Federal Parliament to put the capital there
and nowhere else. In case the Federal Parlia-
ment refused, and a deadlock ensued, it was even
fool enough to talk of getting the Supreme Court
to order the Federal Parliament to proceed at once
with the selection of a site (regardless of the fact
that the Constitution fixes no period within which
the choice must be made) so that the Common-
wealth Legislature would have to take the only
site offering—which was to be Orange-Canoblas.
And now it would appear that the Commonwealth
Legislature can select, say, 5000 square miles at
Bombala or Armidale, and ask the N.S.W. Parlia-
ment for the site—-and if the N.S.W. Parliament
refuses, can either take its chosen site all the same
or can just sit down where it is till the local Legis-
lature at Sydney changes its mind. After this,
the Daily Telegraph had better give up posing
as a source of public information.
A Thousand Years’ Lease.
Tasmania has just sat up in its sleep to find that
another Private Enterprise job has been worked
on a green and confiding island. Tasmania has
already been had almost every time it put its
faith in syndicates—the Main Line Railway, the
Great Western Railway, the Van Diemen’s Land
Co. and many other like shows are cases in point—
but this time it has been taken in after such a
wildly ridiculous fashion t at even Tasmania is
mad. The Van Diemen’s Land Co., which is the
beginning of the trouble, is a British curse and
incubus that dates back to 1825. It has an
immense area of land in North-western Tas-
mania, with over 60 miles of water-frontage,
and there it has mouldered and lingered, and
blocked settlement and progress by its con-
servative policy, for 75 years—and no Tas-
manian Government has had the courage or
patriotism to strike it with a heavy graduated
land-tax and absentee-tax, so as to make it turn
its land to better account or else sellout. This
co. built the Emu Bay and Mount Bischoff rail-
way, and afterwards leased it to the Emu Bay
Railway Co. It is a very profitable mineral-
carrying line; in Tasmania the Government
invariably gives to private co.s the right to con-
struct railways in mining districts where there is
a heavy traffic, and makes the ruinous lines in
thinly-peopled agricultural regions itself. The
port of Burnie is on the V.D.L.. Co.’s property,
and the Government started to build a line
thither, regardless of the fact that the co. owned
the foreshore ; so the Government line stops about
a mile from the port. All goods have to be carried
for that last mile at the V.D.L. Co.’s own terms.
Burnie was made a useful port —a port to which it
was worth while carrying a Government railway—
by the expenditure of £45,000 of Government
money on a breakwater. When the Government
agreed to build a breakwater, and thus improve
the V.D.L. Co.’s property at Burnie, the agree-
ment was that the co. should, in return for this
outlay, hand over 3J acres of land, to give the
Government access by railway to its own break-
water. But though the State built the break-
water the co. didn’t hand over the land—so the
only people who can get access to the State-
made harbour of Burnie are the V.D.L. Co.
But the worst trouble is to come, for it now
transpires that the Fysh Government in 188!)
leased the breakwater to -the V.D.L. Co. for
1000 years for a single "payment of £lOOO,
and threw in a 1000 years lease of a bit of
Government land which might have given it access
to its own property. It locked itself in, so to
speak, and threw away the key. So the V.D.L.
Co. has the sole right to carry its railway along
the breakwater to the wharves, and erect cranes
and goods-sheds there, and load and unload ships
there —in fact, it owns the harbour of Burnie,
body and soul. The State certainly reserves the
right to run trains on the co.’s breakwater line,
provided it doesn’t annoy or inconvenience the co.
thereby, and providing it pays an unspecified sum
(to be fixed by expensive arbitration) for the privi-
lege ; but in practice it always would annoy or
inconvenience the co. Besides the State can’t
get to the breakwater in any case. Instead of
handing over 31 acres as agreed upon, to give
the Government access to its own pro-
perty, the co. has kept the land; it hasn’t
handed over anything, and it has a lease of
the State’s property for ten centuries for about id.
per cent, per annum on its cost. This incredible
agreement was apparently not sanctioned by Par-
liament, or by anybody in particular. The
Minister who signed it is understood to say that
he signed the document without reading it—that
he only read the endorsement on the back and
the endorsement led him to believe that he was
signing something quite different—something
about a stray dog regulation, it may be. But no
one seems to be explaining with any vigor whether
the Minister had previously agreed to this appal-
ling job—whether he unconsciously signed a paper
that he intended to sign anyhow; or whether he
unconsciously signed a document that neither he
nor any other Minister had ever heard of or
dreamt of, and which had been drawn up by some
outrageous process of fake, and smuggled among
the Ministerial documents by some person
who ought to be in gaol. That is the really
important point, and about it there appears to
be a great scarcity of information. And why
the atrocious bargain was kept a secret for 11
years is also unexplained. Now the Lewis
Government hesitates, in a small sort of way,
and thinks of trying to call in question the
1000 years’ lease of the breakwater wharfage
rights, and the V.D.L. Co. refuses to hand
over the long-promised 3§ acres till it gets the
lease fully confirmed and acknowledged—the
Government apparently can either have access,
but no wharf or a wharf but no access. And if
the Government confirms the 1000 years’ lease it
now transpires that the 31 acres which the co. is
willing to hand over is to a groat extent under
water and quite useless for railway purposes—a
good deal of it is said to be sea-bottom. In addi-
tion to all this the V.D.L. Co. agreed, 28 years
ago, to hand over to the Government a wharfage
site, so that the Government might build a wharf
at Burnie and further improve the co.’s property.
The site has never yet been handed over, and it
now transpires that it is a site on dry land, with
the co.’s land between it and the water. And
there is talk of compromising by paying
the co. £205,000 for a small" bit of rail-
way valued at £151,000, the balance being to pay
the co. for letting the State have a look at its own
breakwater. And if Tasmania isn’t by this time
tolerably weary of its V.D.L. Co. it certainly
ought to be.
A FAINT moaning sound on the surface of Wes-
tralian politics suggests the possibility of an at-
tempt to start a new boom by giving concessions
on a large scale to private land-grant railway-
builders. . Westralia, within a few years, has
raised £20,000,000 worth of gold and borrowed
£10,000,000 in addition, and it has become so
demoralised by its artificial boom that it finds it
hard to settle down to plain, ordinary conditions
again. In its biggest boom year it borrowed
almost enough to maintain its whole population
without any serious necessity for work. And now
that loans are becoming hard to get, a few
eminent wild-catters would like to create a private
railway boom in the hope of keeping the great
public drunk going a little longer. It would
require a ruinous sacrifice of the public estate
to start anything of the kind, and when it
was over Westralia, with its supply of loans
giving out, its gold supply probably diminish-
ing, its land revenue depleted through sacri-
ficing its best assets as bribes to land-grant
railway syndicates, the profits from its State
railways on the down grade, the inflow of private
capital almost stopped, and a stupendous outflow
of money for interest, would be left in a lamentable
situation. Westralia has already tried private
land-grant railways on a larger’ scale than any of
the other States of Australia; it had to prop' up
its private railways with State money ; and it has
been driven to a policy of repurchasingits syndicate
lines through the utter failure of private manage-
ment. But even while the State is negotiating
for the repurchase of the last big private railway
failure, a considerable section of its politicians is
quite willing to start the discredited policy afresh
if it can thereby keep the unwholesome public
drunk going just a little longer, and stave off the
day when it will have to face the situation in real
earnest. The policy of most Australian politi-
cians is to keep things going, by any disastrous
expedient that may come along, for their time,
and leave their children to pay the bill. And yet
this country lives in a chronic state of wonder-
ment because the new Australian generation is
not more respectful to its parents. In a general
way the new Australian generation is a great deal
too respectful to its parents, considering what the
parents have done to this country.

Melbourne Argus breaks out again in the old
place:
Until it is made clear that, behind the woman suffrage
proposal, there is a majority of either the women or the
men, the Council will be justified in refusing- to sanction
it.
What a useful old gag it is ! Victoria’s House of
Landlords refused to have a Referendum to find
out if the majority was in favor of Woman Suffrage.
So the people signified as best they could by elect-
ing 70 avowed Woman Suffragists out of 95
Assembly members. And the Upper House
paper replies that this isn’t sufficient proof, be-
cause this huge majority might have been elected
on some other grounds. Nothing is con-
clusive proof as to what the people want
but the Referendum, and the Council won’t
sanction a Referendum to find out what the
people want, because it holds there is no evidence
that the people want a Referendum. So the
animal comes out at the same hole it went in at,
and goes in again by the same hole it came out of,
and then keeps on going in and coming out ad in-
finitum.

Sydney’s first municipal election under an adult
and one-person-one-vote system is over, and the
dirty old Council has disappeared. Fifteen old
members are missing. Eight were defeated, six
never stood up to give the people a chance of
beating them, and one is dead. The nine members
of the old Council who have come back are mostly
reformers who, in the bad old days, were smothered
by the filth and incompetence of their colleagues.
That great practical joker, Matthew Harris,
the B.A. who talks about “yous,” has been
beaten by Andy Kelly and W. T. Henson.
It is a fearful thing for a knight and
B.A. to go down before a man in a soft
hat and with a name like Andy Kelly. The
men elected have a big opportunity before them.
They have as many large problems to deal with
as ever faced a municipal council, and the hardest
of them all is to persuade a weak-kneed com-
munity that an increase of rates is absolutely neces-
sary to begin with. With the present revenue, any
attempt to cope with the urgent questions of the
day merely means getting further and further
into the financial bog, and the heaping up of a
load of interest on loans and overdrafts that will
hang like a millstone around the necks of the citi-
zens for generations, and clog every effort to make
Sydney a pleasant place to live in. And judging
by the speeches of most of the councillors before
election, increasing the rates is the one thing most
of them shrink from. Most of them were for
raising money on debentures, or borrowing in
some shape or form, or getting cash from any-
where but the pockets of the rate-payers. And
unless the new Council has grit enough to get
right down to the policy of making income cover
expenditure, it is only a question of time till it
meets the same fate as the unclean old Council it
has just ejected into oblivion.
Picture of N.S.W. Leg. Assembly tor N.S.W.
electors to study, taken from Sydney Telegraph
of 5th Dec. : —
In regard to the transaction of its business, this has
been one of the most remarkable of all sessions of Parlia-
ment. The intelligent stranger who strolled at inter-
vals into the House to observe its procedure and to study
its men would have said that the Chamber was never
under any sort of control at all. He would have noticed
that two or three members, quite unofficial members,
took unrestricted charge of the House, dictated to the
Govt., drove the Premier into acquiescence with their
demands however preposterous, constantly paralysed
business, brow-beat parties, laughed at the Speaker,
flouted the Chairman, indulged in coarse language that
men would not repeat in mixed society, made
speeches such as no newspaper, anxious for its
reputation, would publish at all. He would have
remarked, too, that these members, who carried
licence of debate to the limits of toleration, tried,
and frequently succeeded, in making tyrannical use of
the forms of the House to defeat and hamper and bull-
doze other members. He would have heard the word
“ liar ” used as a common expression that rarely met with
notice, and less often with disapprobation. He would
have become so accustomed to the ejaculations, “ For
God’s sake,” and “ Great God,” and “I wish to God,”
that he would think they were part of the ordinary
language of the Chamber. He would have seen
the Opposition with a nominal leader, who was
seldom to be found in the House ; Ministers who, with
the exception of the Premier and two or three other
members of the Govt., rarely troubled to attend in their
places. He would have seen the Opposition so hopelessly
divided that its strength as a united body could never be
tested.
He would have seen railway and harbor proposals
rushed through the House at 2 and 3 and 4 a.m., when
not a quorum was present. He would have seen hours
wasted on trivial points of order, and discussion on bills
involving important changes in legislation, so important
that they were virtually a change in the Constitution,
severely and angrily deprecated. He would have seen
bills introduced at one sitting and passed without Copies
of them having been prepared for circulation among the
members who had to consider them. He would have
wondered why, even if the bills could not be printed, the
Minister could still find no time or inclination to explain
what was in them. And he would have concluded his
experience by witnessing the last possible stage of all-
notice being given of a bill that it was proposed to intro-
duce the same night and rush through all its stages.
These things might have amused the intelligent stranger!
If he thought they were not any improvement on vvhat
he had been accustomed to elsewhere, he would at least
have the novelty of a fresh experience and the lessons to
be derived from new methods. If it seemed strange to
him that the governing authorities of the House could not
control a few active, irrepressible, self-advertising spirits,
that the Govt, had meekly to do their bidding, that the
rules of the House were made use of to forward the per-
sonal interests and convenience of a few, it would have
shown that his education in up-to-date politics was still
in arrear.
The recent counting-out of Lyne’s Marriage Bill
(having for its object the suppression of matri-
monial agencies) in N.S.W. Leg. Council is a
well-earned slap in the face for the insolent cleri-
cal trade-union. The Bill, in fact, was an impu-
dent attempt to put back the hands of the world’s
clock and secure to the churches that monopoly of
the marriage-business which is fast slipping out
of their hands. The framers of the Bill over-
stepped themselves in their haste to protect what
it pays them to call “morality.” Even the House
of Fossils couldn’t help seeing the absurdity of
placing the superintendence of doctrine and
morals in the hands of an Attorney-General and
Registrar-General, both of whom might be Jews,
Agnostics, or anything heterodox. Nothing
can more clearly prove the humorless dull-
ness of the clerical mind than the way the
parsons of all sects, so long as they belonged
to a “regular church,” fell over each other in their
frantic hurry to put themselves under the heel of
a State Department which, for the time being,
might be expected to obliterate the outsiders.
The whole tendency of the Bill was to throw
obstacles in the way of lawful marriage. What
does it matter where, when, or how the ceremony
is performed, so long as it is lawful, and without
compulsion of either party ? If marriage is a good
thing it should surely be made as easy as possible.
Vio. Chief Sec. Peacock’s return to office was
promptly signalised by a repetition of the in-
famous practice of gaoling the sick poor. On
Tuesday week sundry subservient Coburg J.’sP.
visited Pentridge female penitentiary, and, having
diagnosed a case of illness as St. Vitus’ dance,
promptly ordered twelve months imprisonment as
a cure. The unfortunate subject had already
served one similar term for the same offence—
that of having no competence and not being im-
mediately able to work —and though she pleaded
hard to be sent to a benevolent asylum or a hos-
pital, anywhere but a gaol, the beaks treated her
as a habitual criminal and a confirmed scoundrel,
and gave her another year. Is there any other
country on earth—civilised or savage—which thus
dishonors its name? Already the province pre-
pares to spend thousands over the Commonwealth
celebrations, and the Yorks, and the returned
contingenters—while a sick woman rots in gaol
for two years, for the crime of being poor.
Pekth (W. A.) City Council has lately been asking
the Government for authority to supply electric
light and power to the city, and to give the rate-
payers the benefit of the profit derivable from the
enterprise. Perth Tramway Co. demands autho-
rity to do the same work and to divide all the
profits among the (largely-foreign) shareholders.
And the awful Government of Big John Forrest
is enthusiastically helping a Bill through Parlia-
ment to give this most valuable monopoly gratis
to the Tramway Co. —this despite the indignant
protests of Perth Council. An even more shady-
looking measure is the Hampton Plains Syndicate
Railway Bill, which is also being helped along by
the Forrest push for all it is worth. The
Forrests have persistently refused to build the
Kalgoorlie-Esperanee Bay line, which would
give the goldfields a short road to the sea,
on the ground that it would destroy the
trade of the hideously long railway from Kal-
goorlie to Perth. The Esperanee line would
pay the State splendidly, for almost all the gold-
fields trade would go over it, but it would pay by
taking business from the other State line, and
would shift the goldfields base of supplies from
Perth to Adelaide, so it has always been blocked
as a Government enterprise. But the bill now
before Parliament gives the Hampton Plains
Syndicate authority to build the first section of a
line pointing straight towards Esperanee, and
everybody has a distinct idea that the other sec-
tions will naturally follow. The wrecking of the
profits of the Kalgoorlie-Perth State line by a
State line to Esperanee isn’t to be thought o’.
But the same damage done to the Perth State kne
for the benefit of a private syndicate railway is
regarded as rather commendable than otherwise.
At present, however, the private line is only
to go part of the way to Esperanee; it is
to end nowhere in particular, and its out-
ward and ostensible purpose is to carry a
shrub or two to the goldfields under
the name of firewood. But many influential
persons are understood to be interested, and the
extension to Esperanee is probably quite safe
as soon as the co. finds that better firewood can be
obtained a little further on. Big John Forrest
has intimated that he is about to resign the high
billet in which he has borrowed so manfully for
ten years, and his last days promise to smell even
worse than his first. This is saying a good deal,
but the circumstances appear to fully justify it.
Let all men who love Liberty read Richard
Harding Davis’ powerful sketch, “ The Last
Days of Pretoria,” in Scribner’s for October.
Davis, it may be mentioned, is not a Fenian, nor
a German, nor a Frenchman, but an American
of English blood. Here are the concluding lines :
The hot, barren kopjes with their stunted cacti, the
splashing water-falls, and the twisting white river that
raced the train, all filled me with regret. They had never
looked more beautiful or more to bg desired, or more as
the sftene set for a country men would choose to call
home. The sight of the men to whom it really was
home, who were fighting for it, and who were to continue
to fight for it, stirred me with pride in them. I saw them
for the last time even as I was steaming awav from them
to another continent, to other interests and older friends.
They were jogging patiently through the high grass on
our right, and spreading out fan-wise over the red kopjes
that lay between them and Irene, where the sultry air
was shaken with the heavy vibrations of hot-throated
guns. They trotted forward alone or in pairs, each an
independent fighting-man with his rifle and blanket
swung across his shoulders, with his canvas water-bottle,
rusty coffee-pot and bundle of green fodder dang-
ling from his saddle. I knew as the train
carried us away from the sight of them that rio
soldier in pipe-ola.v, gauntlets, and gold lace would ever
again mean to me what these burghers meant—these
long-bearded, strong-eyed Boers with their drooping
cavalier hats, their bristling bands of cartridges, their
upright seat in the saddle, and the rifle rising- above them
like the lance of the crusaders They were the last of the
crusaders. They rode out to fight for a cause as old as
the days of Pharaoh and the children of Israel, against
an enemy ten times as mighty as was Washington’s in his
war for independence. As I see ir, it has been a Holy
War, this war of the burgher crusader, and his motive’s
are as fine as any that ever called a “ minute man ” from
his farm or sent a knight of the Cross to die for it in
Palestine. Still, in spite of his cause, the Boer is losing,
and in time his end may come, and he may fall. But
when lie falls he will not fall alone ; with him'will end a
great principle, the principle for w-hieh our forefathers
fought—the right of self-government, the principle of
md pendence.
iiiiif is the principle, alas, which Australian men
and money have helped to strangle. Instead of
rejoicing we should weep tears of blood.
