From letter received by a Sydney resident from
his brother (a lieutenant Ist Bombay Lancers,
now at Matao, midway between Pekin and
Tientsin).:--
Had a scrap yesterday with 300 Boxers and floored over
40 of them. I hate the work, for these men are only
tools in the hands of men our diplomatic (?) fools allow to
pass scot free, Call the Boxer rank-and-file “ inhuman ?”
Heroes, every man—according to their lights. Give ’em
.a white skin and a mediaeval name, and we’d rank ’em
with Cromwell s supporters. The missionaries brought
about the trouble, and now must we risk our useless
lives to retrieve the country’s honor and inter alia bar-
rack for their indemnity. Inhuman? Yes, of course,
they are. They expect no quarter, and get none. The
Russians called a lighter with 500 of the Talal Tug and
Lighter Co.’s men (returning from coaling men-of-war) to
land on banks of Pei Ho and butchered the lot in cold
blood . Many of these men I knew when I was quill-
driving- for the same company, And, talk about loot!
Every man in. the Kaiser's “No-quarter” army would go
away a millionaire—could he keep away from rum.
I’m fast losing my prejudices in favour of Germans.
During the bombardment of Tientsin the only non-com-
pliance with orders came from the gentlemen from the
Rhine. Japs have provedgrit y from the first. Hart (Sir
Robert- Hart, Inspector-General of Customs) or Hillier
could do better than the so-called diplomatists we pay- to
be outwitted by Russia. Old Li openly propitiates the
bear, and we know what that means. My knowledge of
Russian, Manchu, and Jap stands me in good stead. Why

SUNDRY SHOWS.

That old favorite, “ Faust,” is proving the
more popular of the alternating operas at Sydney
Royal. As a whole, the new production is evenly
unexciting, although backed up with some stirring
scenery and choruses. Charlie Tilbury’s clean-
faced Mephisto sings with a round, unworn bass,
and acts in a quiet, ordinary, graceful kind of way
just as a Devil of long and gentlemanly experi-
ence would be sure to do. Lilian Coomber, as
Marguerite, is well-equipped so far as looks go,
but her clear little soprano is colorless and youth-
ful, and often ineffective. Probably the “ Jewel
Song ” gets a more dramatic and dignified treat-
ment from half a dozen local sopranos who will
never get the chance to warble behind grease-
paint. As for tenor Arens, his Faust music is
feelingly sung and with evident determination to
do his best, but the gent in brown would never
lady-kill anyone a day older than Marguerite
with his intense “ I lof thee.” His voice is strong
and robust, but not of fine quality. Lawrence
Mooney’s ringing baritone is flanked with some
good acting in his death-scene. Siebel falls to
Mary Lynne, she of the veiled contralto and the
well-displayed legs. It is these extremities which
win her a patient hearing. The great want in the
new co. is dramatic ability, and yet, even as
voices, the voices of the principals are all consis-
tently mediocre. These remarks refer to the cast
as it was on the first night. “ Faust ”is also to
be seen at intervals with Cecile Lorraine as Mar-
guerite and Salvi as the demon, and again with
Madame Slapoffski as the tempted maiden, so
there is no lack of variety in Marguerite. “ Car-
men ” commences on Saturday.
#* * *
Her Majesty’s, Sydney, is closed till 26th
December, when “Australis, or the City of
Zero,” by Bernard Espinasse and J. C. William-
son, will be presented with large magnificence.
Nance O’Neil departed after a week of one-night
revivals, which were interesting in their way, but
nothing to be compared to the glorious “Mac-
beth.” To everybody with a capacity for wild
enjoyment, “Macbeth,” thanks toM‘Kee Rankin
as the Scotch king, will remain the brightest spot
in the Nance O’Neil record. It was fresh, daring,
and original. The back view of the vast hot
usurper laboring up the great stone stairway of
the Castle of Inverness to kill Duncan will be
remembered by this writer when he has even for-
gotten the Commonwealth celebrations and the
Boer-hunting contingent. And if Macbeth had
given up the struggle when half-way up the stair,
and called to Duncan to come doon and be killed
on the ground floor, his joy would have been
complete.
** * *
The Criterion version of the ‘ ‘ Three
Musketeers ” is somewhat livelier than some
others Sydney has seen. It includes the public
execution of Miladi pursued by the Red Heads-
man, also Miladi’s subjugation of Felton in her
role of persecuted Puritan maiden. Also it
includes other troubles of that much-hanged and
unappreciated woman. There was evidently some
excitement in being a married woman in those
days, when the gallant French nobleman
occasionally suspended his wife on a tree in a
public and ostentatious manner, and possibly tied
a rock to her feet to prevent her flapping
dismally in the wind. Even the King seems to
have had the headsman always waiting in
tlse next room in case he might come
in useful for the Queen. The result was
probably that Queens walked a more careful chalk-
line than they do now, or looked as if they did.
Dampier is brighter than Rignold as the French
hayseed who came to make his fortune in Paris.
Also, he is like an Australian, inasmuch as when
he loses his horse and his lady-love at the same
time he goes to look for the horse first. But
Dampier is unnecessarily ’umble in manner when
he accidentally knocks against a captain under
the impression that he is merely a man. Miss
Lily Dampier makes Miladi a rather misunder-
stood individual instead of a walking iniquity,
and the gallery resents the fatal abbreviation of
her by the man with the axe. But she is
surprisingly good as the Puritan damsel
who was “ wronged.” Besides Buller as
a hungry servant, there is a squat red
Cardinal and a yellow - legged King who is
reminded by various people that his surname is
“ The Just ” whenever they want to make him do
anything that is Unjust. He is most aggressively
Just in the scene where he orders the Queen to be
searched and personally overhauled by a male
minion in quest of incriminating documents. One
wonders if the great and good monarchs of that
period really had their wives searched by male
captains and such-like, to see if they had com-
promising or defamatory letters hidden down
their backs.
John F. Sheridan duly removed his bags
and chattels to Sydney Lyceum, where he did a
surprisingly brief revival of old “Fun on the
Bristol.” Even the good old joke of the heaving
stage vessel which took a mixed company a very
long way for a dollar is wearing out a
little. John is now doing “The Shaughraun,”
and doing it remarkably well, but no deep dis-
simulation can conceal the fact that it is also a
play that is past its first youth. The advt. says
that “The Shaughraun” will live for ever, but
this paper is half inclined to think that it is
dying already. It represents a state of things that
is becoming old forgotten history. The Irish
nation has mostly emigrated. The Saxon op-
pressor can now walk such long distances in
empty Clare and Kerry without finding anyone to
actively oppress that he has eased-off a good deal
to save the cost of boots. The informer has
largely ceased to inform for the want of people to
inform on or to carry the information to. The
peasantry that once was hanged for the wearing
of the green is doing police duty in New York,
and the Celtic Irishman will soon be everywhere
except in Ireland, and the Saxon Irishman
will be left there to inform on himself. Where-
fore the “ Shaughraun ” will soon be out of date,
for they don’t have shaughrauns in New York,
where the O’Donnells and O’Murphys are going
nowadays.
* * * *
The small lost-looking theatre down in
Castlereagh-street, Sydney, bursts into represen-
tation every now and then in an erratic manner,
breaking out on a Tuesday, taking a rest on
Wednesday, and then resuming its duties on the
day following. The Royal Standard’s recent out-
burst was “ Dr. Bill.” It is noticeable that when
the tragic drama, like “East Lynne” and
“Uncle Tom” gets into grey hairs it invariably
becomes comic, whereas with farces of the “Dr.
Bill” order the process is frequently reversed.
The Standard’s “Dr. Bill” was mainly upheld
by a very serious hero with flickering Cinemato-
graphe eyebrows and a stodgy father-in-law.
“Dr. Bill” is the drama of the Locked Door
Joke made in France, and was vaguely under-
stood, when it first dawned on the Saxon intelli-
gence, to be dubiously respectable. However,
the wickedness has been gradually peeling off it
during the last few years. At the Royal Standard
there weren’t enough doors to be locked, so most
of the people had to content themselves with re-
tiring to the wings and pretending that they were
locked. Altogether, it was a repentant kind of
drama, and might almost have been written by
Rev. George Walters.
* * * *
At Sydney Tivoli Frank Leon, England's
“ Only Champion Dancer,” dances with every
muscle, and generally without music, except the
pounding of his fast and furious feet. Lizzie
Kirk, returned after an absence of three years,
owns a Scotch Presbyterian name, which seems to
overawe the audience during her worn-out witti-
cisms on the lydy who ransacks the whole shop to
match something that she doesn’t want matched.
Later on she brightens up by being partly remin-
iscent of Irene Franklin. The stage is now con-
siderably darkened when Lottie Collins makes
her appearance as a coon, perhaps because Lottie’s
lower limbs are not in keeping with the exuber-
ance and round curves of her expressive face.

“ Banjo ” Paterson twanged his round,
unvarnished tale in the dredgeful city of Dunedin
(M.L.) for three nights, from 21st Nov., to good
biz. On 24th “Banjo” and Donald Macdonald,
brother correspondents for the same papers, gave
a joint entertainment under the much-travelled
Smythe, and met for the first time on a Dunedin
stage. Mac., by the quality of his defect— i.e. ,
his stereotyped, recitation-lilce yarns—uninten-
tionally scored off his poet-rival. He was telling
his “argument” yarn, and said, as per stereo-
type, “He was that sort of man, that when you
catch him young, sometimes becomes a poet ”
but the audience roared, and Banjo blushed.
* * * *
On its first night (Dec. 8) the “ Pinafore ”
revival drew a moderate attendance at Her
Majesty’s, Melbourne. The present edition of the
Gilbert-Sullivan nauticality is attractive enough,
though the public begins to think that it has seen
‘ ‘ Pinafore ” already somewhere. Wallace Brown-
low’s Captain Corcoran looks and sounds all that
an operatic Son of the Sea ought to be. Kenning-
ham makes an apple-cheeked and tuneful Ralph
Rackstraw, and, as usual, works hard all the time.
Bathurst is convincing as the “ Bos’n’s mate,” and
displays the beauty of his voice in “We tars!”
The joke of the evening is Bos’n Hugh Ward’s
hornpipe. In fact, all the planets on the present
“Pinafore” firmament are men, while, at best,
the ladies only twinkle, twinkle like little tremb-
ling stars. Alma Skinner, who pipes “ Off to the
Rio Grande ” very prettily, looks a gentle, wistful
little midshipmite. Walking the deck, she
might easily be a small-boy ghost of Kings-
ton, or a midget edition of Melbourne’s own Pro-
fessor Harrison Moore. Carrie Moore’s appearance
as Josephine conveys the correct idea of a little
maid from school, all ready to plunge into love
with a common sailor. Miss Fy vie Dench’s Little
Buttercup is a superior, humorless person, a
teacher or sich, masquerading as a bumboat woman.
Cecil Englelieart, dark-haired and rosy-cheeked,
is a glowing Hebe in pink. Other pleasing items
are a ballet and a gushing crowd of sisters, cousins
and aunts. Lauri as the Great Admiral turns on
rather a mild tap of Porter. On Saturday (Dec.
15), “ Pinafore ” is to give place to “ Florodora ”
—its first Australian appearance. An old friend
who is not old, Miss Grace Palotta, is to be in the
cast.
* * * Hf
Bland Holt’s “Absent-Minded Beggar” has
left behind him his home at the Royal, and on Satur-
day (Dec. Bth) a large crowd rollod up to welcome
“ The Cotton King.” The revived play dishes up
the usual haw-haw villain (one Wheeler), whose
machinations, schemes and skeleton keys work the
ruin of Baker, as usual. Baker is the most ruined
and persecuted man on the Australian stage.
Being hounded from bad to worse, he is thrust
into an American lunatic asylum at last, and
escapes thence in quaint broad-brimmed hat and
Silver King tailorings, just in time to rescue his
faithful Frances Boss, in becoming brown-
holland clothes, from being squashed by a
descending lift. Baker goes home that night
without revealing his identity to his dear
one, and evidently spends the interval in per-
sonal decoration, for next day he calls at his
mother-in-law’s elect, groomed, starched, and
polished, and steps to Miss Boss’s palpitating
side, raising his glossy top-hat, with the aplomb
that marks the tile of Yere de Vere. Bland Holt
is a North Country edition of his usual stage self
—a cheeky rough-and-ready squire of distressed
humanity, and Mrs. Bland is a female Samson,
who becomes a Hallelujah Lass, and is rightly
considered a remarkably fine woman by the Sal-
vation Army captain (Charles Brown). The rest
of the co. is much as usual, and the play may be
summed up as being like the co. “ Cotton King”
belongs to a class of plays that are very much
alike; the trouble is that it is even more alike
than many other plays.
* * He *
At Melbourne Bijou Miss Abbott, the
Georgia Magnet, doubles up rash males, who pit
mere mannishness against the patent Abbott
knack. Small, perky faddists attack their
“subject” seriously, striving and straining to
master her resistance. Lumbering patriarchs
become butter-fingered and bumble-footed in their
dread of toe-trampling or shin-kicking so small an
adversary, while showy lady-killers brave out
their failure to “shift” the alleged phenomenon
by ogling and tossing kisses in her direction. Such
a small thing as a clean shave rubbed the shine off
a traditional Strong Man. Possibly an unsympa-
thetic, gimlet-eyed she-committee might succeed
in loosening the grip of a Magnetic Woman.
Daisy Harcourt’s coster items catch on, with their
references to the singer’s khaki underwear and
her sister’s twins. When this is printed, how-
ever, Geo. D’Albert, who earned only moderate
applause by the literal sweat of his comely brow,
will be off the Bijou bill, and “ Clive,” clever
conjuror and pretty youth, will also be on the eve
of flight.
#* * *
The “Scarlet Feather,” which is not long
for Melbourne, drew only two crack audiences
(one of them on opening night) to the Princess
throughout its first week and a half. During the
same period at Her Majesty’s the “Old Guard”
prospered, so the slump in the house of the
red hen - ornament was evidently not solely
due to the return of the coinless khaki
one with the failing mind. Neither can the
trouble be laid to the account of “our Nellie,”
with her delightful voice, delicious figure and
dazzling dresses. Experts describe the Stewart
contour in page attire as too beautiful for this
world. With or without glasses it’s difficult to
detect a fraction too much either of beef or bone
in the whole of the stage-boy’s dainty carcase,
though sticklers hint at a tendency to massiveness
across the back of the shoulders. But apart from
Nellie, who is the plum, the rest of the pie is
mostly paste. Tapley, singing soulfully “ Angels
of Heaven Defend Thee,” seems almost handsome
by comparison with the tame Belvwa of the long
body and short legs. Seguin, the imported
funny man, is perhaps hampered by the
dearth of laughter m the libretto, but
Lauri in Seguin’s shoes would fake up glee galore,
notably in that variety turn where, embosomed in
his harem, the frisky pill-man sings “ There are
only a hundred girls in the world for me.”
Soprano Emmie Owen, with a pretty voice, and
inoffensive, rather dainty, appearance, wears the
same frock throughout. Maud Thornton is a saucy
village belle and acts prettily and pertly, but her
voice is so weak that hearers call to mind the
rumor that the “ Scarlet Feather ” was designedly
gut on the boards as a one-woman show. Mrs.
alhaem, the comic lady, makes no attempt to
sing, but by way of compensation looks like the
weirdest frump extant.
The Fates perpetrated a ghastly joke in
Wellington (M.L.) recently. A coal-hulk keeper
named James Wilson had gone ashore one even-
ing, and after a couple of days had passed without
his turning up, his wife saw in the paper that
one J. Wilson had been run-in for being drunk,
&c. She sent ashore an ill-spared sovereign and
bailed him out, but that night her husband’s body
was found in the harbour. Poor wife ! She had
bailed out the wrong man.
Dan O’Connor, M.L.A., Australia’s only
orator, has broken out once more. Sez he, in a
letter to Sydney E. News :
The Persons who oppose the rights of woman sufferage
—are the Tomb Stones of a Vile and putrid past; they
represent the Lust and Brutality of Paganism.
The Teaching of the Lord Jesus Christ Emancipated
Women and Promoted her to the Exalted position which
she holds to-day and dignifies by her Grace and beauty
and Culture. The Church is the work of the Almighty
God—So is the State.
The Church has done her work nobly—Now the State
has the privilege of doing Justice to God’s Noblest Crea-
ture —a Woman.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The Westralian 3| per cent, loan of £BBO,OOO,
put on the market in London at par, only brought offers
of about £450,000 in all. The underwriters took up the
remainder. Westralia gets the money, of course, but,
after paying the heavy expense of underwriting the loan
and all the ordinary charges, it probably gets only about
£97 for each £lOO ; so the interest-rate is £3 12s. 2d., and
the loss on repayment will make it considerably more.
As already mentioned, it is almost certain that the W.A.
Govt, could have got the money much more cheaply in
Sydney and Melbourne—but it couldn’t get out of its old
rut, and it couldn’t realise that j ust now its credit in London
is almost as dead as Cain’s brother. Westralia last June
owed £63 Bs. per inhabitant. It will soon have the most
awful debt in the world. It produces much gold, but,
thanks to what it used to call the “ splendid influx of
foreign capital,” the gold belongs to people in England
and elsewhere—almost everywhere save in W.A. The
disease of foreign capital has fastened on W.A., and eaten
it up badly, and it has really no prospects of its own that
are decent security for a big loan just now.

The reason for showing the N.S.W. revenue with and
without the rents from the resumed area is that these
rents are the receipts from property taken over by Govt,
but not paid for, nor has the payment been provided
for, and the expenditure estimates for the year do
not include provision for the year's interest on the
money which must be borrowed to pay for the pro-
perty. Therefore these rents are really in the nature
of a trust fund, and to include them in the year’s revenue
when the corresponding outgoings are not provided for
in the year’s expenditure is a very dubious proceeding.
Even the improvements being made in the resumed area
are not charged against the £43,000 odd already received
as rent from the unpaid-for property—they go into the
permanent debt, just as the cleaning of the resumed area
was charged to the floating debt.
The following are the respective increases of
revenue when brought to a common basis of so much per
inhabitant per annum. The N.S.W. figures do not in-
clude the rents from the resumed area. The calculations
are based on Ooghlan’s estimates of population as at 31st

Taking Premier Lyne’s own figures the N.S.W.
revenue, as shown above, for the five months to 30th
Nov., 1900, is £242,575 more than for the corresponding
period of 1899. The ordinary expenditure has in-
creased by £202,286, so on paper the N.S.W. Treasury
is £40,289 richer than it was this time last year.
Yet while revenue for the five months is £4,134,588,
expenditure is £4,178,865, sq the deficit is already
£44,277. And the expenditure of loan money
is swelling at an appalling rate. For the five
months to 30th Nov., 1899, N.S.W. spent £792,359 of
borrowed cash. For the five months just ended it has
spent £993,838—an increase of £201,479. And Lyne is
charging ordinary expenditure to loans quite as vigor-
ously as Reid did.
Queensland is in a much worse state than even
N.S.W. Its revenue for Che five months to 30th Nov.,
1900, is £126,154 less than for the corresponding period of
last year, while its ordinary expenditure is £183,251 more.
Therefore Queensland is £309,405 worse off than 12
months ago. At 30th Nov., 1899, it had a surplus of
£830,554 ; just now its surplus is only £521,149. Queens-

The Pioneer Tin Mining Co., of Bradshaw’s
Greek, Tasmania, have ordered from the Austral Otis
Eng. Co., South Melbourne, a large dredging plant for the
treatment of their tin deposits. The Plant is a very com-
plete one, and includes vertical compound engine 400 h.p.,
suitable boilers carrying 1501 b. steam pressure, and one of
Otis newest design 15in. gravel pump. The plant is to be
completed and set to work within six months.

Since the start of the Broken Hill Proprietary’s
new 5000-ton mill, most of the Barrier mines have been
engaged in remodelling their concentrating plants on up-
to-date lines, and wisely show a preference for machines
which have proved most successful on the difficult fines
and slimes. The machines that can claim this distinction
are the Luhng ” Yanners, as supplied by Messrs W &
J. Lempnere, of Melbourne. The South’s new mill will
include a dozen or so, and the Junction Co. has also de-
cided to erect a number of Luhrig Vanners as an impor-
tant adjunct to its large plant. Other mines would do
well to follow this good example.*

in the share market and partial failure of the sulphide
plant roused F. L. Gardner to make some injudicious
declarations m London. Frank said that the GB P was
simply a jeweller’s warehouse. He was ready to defy
the “ bears,” advised holders to stick to their shares and
if they were not prepared to do that he was ready to’take
the lot for £lO a share. Also published that he wagered
100,000 francs in Paris that the mine would turn out 250
tons of ore per day for the next seven years, of the aver-
age of ljoz. ; he offered to increase the wager to £lOO 000
sterling, stakes to be deposited in the Bank of England
There is a lot more of the same sort of hogwash. Writer
can’t believe it of Gardner, who, though foolish in some
things, is not such an ass as his buttering newspaper the
British Australasian, is making him out to be. ’

W estralia’s expenditure account for last month
isn t in yet, but for four months to 31st Oct., 1900
revenue showed a surplus of about £3713 over ordi-
nary expenditure. At the end of Oct., 1899, the
surplus was £102,286, so W.A.’s Treasury is
£98,573 worse off than it was 12 months ago. The great
increase of £85,913 in the first four months’ revenue was
more than counterbalanced by the increase of £184,486 in
the expenditure. Loan expenditure is unknown, but
plainly the war bill and the interest on the perpetually,
increasing debt are coming home to roost with a ven-
geance. Last year Westralia had a surplus large enough
to pay off the old deficit in full, but it only got it bv levy-
ing extra taxation. This year it looks as if the' extra
taxation would just about keep things square. Next year,
on present appearances, some more fresh taxation will be
needed.
Victoria, S.A., and Tasmania, as usual, furnish
no information about the public outgoings.

-f'he Victorian State Land Bank completed
its fourth year on 30th June, 1900. Results so far are as
follows :
Working Expenses in
expenses in proportion to
proportion to average capital
" oth gross earnings employed Total
,une Loss. per cent. per cent, reserves

Profit.
1898 1,608 23 17 11 1 210 £146
1899 5,551 14 17 2 13 7 6,697
1900 6,846 15 6 4 13 10 12,643
Taking everything into account this is very good pro-
gress. The reserve fund is increasing at a good rate. The
total amount now out on mortgage is £906,146, and of
this only £413 is represented by properties in possession.
The payments in arrear (principal and interest) are £B3 in
all, and the losses on sale of foreclosed properties have
been £46 up to date. Hitherto the Victorian State
Land Bank has borrowed money at 3 per cent.,
but the rate was too low to attract lenders, and it is to
be raised in future borrowings to 3£ per cent. This will
reduce the margin of profit somewhat, but in all proba-
bility the gradual reduction of expenses and the income
derived from investment of the increasing reserve fund
will make good the difference. Even if all the present
funds had been borrowed at 3£ per cent, instead of 3 per
cent., the bank would still have a margin of profit on
its existing lending rate of U per cent. It is quite possi-
ble, when its reserve fund grows much larger, and the
income from it increases, and expenses have been re-
duced, that it will be possible to borrow at 3J per cent,
and lend at 4J or even 4, but that event is some distance
off yet.

Alderman Thomas Hughes lately made an in-
teresting statement of how the awful Corporation of
Sydney had conducted its finances, simply because sheer
cowardice prevented the aldermen levying sufficient rates
to cover ordinary expenditure :
Year’s Total
_ Deficit. Deficit.
At 31st Dec., 1894 .. ... jgig qqq
1895 ~ .. £22',957 41,957
1896 36,577 .. 78,534
1897 36,526 .. 116,060
1898 (after taking £B9OO from
sinking funds and dis-
charging many em-
ployes).. 4,300 .. 119,360
1899 (works stopped and city surplus
allowed to go to wreck) .. 2,750 .. 116,610
deficit
1900 (estimated) 47,376 .. 163,986
This year 2d., in the £ was added to the rates (raised
from Is. 4d. to Is. 6d.), but there was so much
expenditure to pay interest on overdraft and loans
and to repair damage that had accumulated
through the stoppage of work, that the biggest deficit
up to date is looming. Now it is reckoned that it will be
necessary to float another loan to pay off the overdraft
and that another 3d. in the £ rates will be required to
cover interest and sinking fund. That will make an in-
crease of sd. in the £ (Is. 4d. to Is. 9d.) in two years.
And that is merely to make good back shortages, and
leaves no surplus for keeping the city in better condition
If the extra sd. had been put on in 1894 there would
have been no overdraft, and enough income to keep
Sydney m reasonable repair. But the dry-rot of the
policy of Let-it-Slide and Put-it-off-till-To-Morrow is in all
Australian finance.

New deposits have increased during last year by £261,589

The solid and prosperous Derwent and Tamar
Fire and Marine Assurance Co. of Tasmania, now in its
62nd year, publishes its fifth annual balance-sheet, which
covers the 12 months to 31st October, 1900. It is only
the fifth published balance-sheet, because for 57 years
the Derwent and Tamar didn’t lay its accounts before the
Eublic. The results during these last five years have
een as follows:
Total Re-
Dividend and serves at

The paid-up capital is £50,000, so the reserves (reserve
fund £50,000 and undivided profit £10,711) are very solid
by comparison, as Australian ideas go. The Derwent and
Tamar was struck heavily in the memorable Melbourne
fire, but it is steadily recovering from that bad shock. It
is a solid and well-managed concern with a small, but
apparently safe, business, and capital and re-
serves are equal to more than 4J years’ premiums,
which is a degree of solidity not often attained
in this country. Its investments yield apparently
about 4 per cent, all round (though, as cash on current
account isn’t distinguished from money at interest, it is
difficult to say), and seem reasonably solid. Yet the fact
that, out of £117,000, no less than £73,000 is invested in
mortgages, and only £4OOO in state securities, looks
rather dubious policy. In bad times, it is the mortgage
business over which almost every co. is let in.

* • • •
Owing to grave doubts as to the depth of silt in
the Broken Hill Water Supply’s reservoir(and therefore also
as to the quantity of water) N.S.W. Works Department
is sending up an expert to make a hydrographic survey.
The Co.’s engineer says there is 12 months’ supply for the
B.H. mines and town ; others put it at from four to eight
months. If the latter is the correct estimate the outlook
is rather gloomy. So far this year Broken Hill has had
under 7in. of rain—and yet they locally call it a good
season.
• * * *
B.H. Junction claims for the half-year a profit
of £706. On the same figures other co’s (and probably
this one too in different circumstances) might have
claimed a good deal more. For the £706 profit is arrived
at by deducting depreciation, not for the half-year, but for
a whole year, and by also deducting the expenses of the
recent reconstruction of the co. If the depreciation
charge is cut in halves and the reconstruction expenses
charged to capital account instead of to working account,
the profit on the half-year’s operations amount to £4300.
But the mine did not fairly earn that much, because the
charge of 6 per cent, for a half-year for the wear-and-tear
of plant is not enough. Nothing much short of 20 per
cent, a year will cover it. The co. has just got £25,000
of new capital, and after paying off £lO,OOO borrowed three
years ago, it has £17,600 cash and £11,700 good debts with
which to pay for the plant, etc., now being provided.
Much of the old plant is poor—a thing of shreds and
patches—and the re-arrangement ought to permit of all-
round cheaper and better work. And the Junction can-
not afford to give away much. Its milling sulphides (and
it has practically nothing else) are worth only 9'950z.
silver per ton, 13'35 per cent, lead, and 10’36 per cent,
zinc, whereas two years ago the ore averaged 10 - 720 z.
silver and 18 per cent, lead; and of the present diminished
metal contents, so refractory is the stuff, only 34'68 per
cent, of the silver and 56'07 per cent, of the lead are re-
covered. In other words, the co. can save only 3£oz.
silver per ton, and 7 per cent. lead. Nor do the surround-
ings lend themselves to cheap work. Last half-year
mining cost 15s. Bd. a ton of ore against 13s. 4Jd. in the
preceding half. As to that it is explained that the ore in
this mine takes a lot of getting-at, which is true ;
also, it is likely to continue true, but more so.
Further, the mine takes a lot of propping-up after
the ore is taken out; that is a condition which
is going to continue. Altogether, though improved a
trifle, the prospects are poor, even with metals at their
present high prices ; with a slump in them, nothing but a
most substantial improvement in the processes of sulphide
t«eatment could keep the mine going. In fact there isn’t
another mine on the B.H. line in a worse position to face
a slump.
* * * *
The big Sulphide Corporation, owning the B.H.
Central, follows Block 10, Block 14, and others with an
experimental plant designed to concentrate the zinc
“ tailings” by the agency of magnets. Much of the plant
has been imported after the plans for it had been approved
by English “ chemical engineers ” —experts of whom it is
complained there is a striking dearth in Australia.
* * * *
Sulphide Corporation, which owns the B.H.
Central mine and the Cockle Creek (Newcastle) works,
pays a div. of 7J per cent, all round —the second for the
year. Also it carries forward £52,500, as against £lB,OOO
brought forward. There are 550,000 preference (£1) and
650,000 ordinary (155.) shares ; so the div. means a distri-
bution of £72,187 10s. That, the former 7i per cent, and
the undivided balance represent a profit of about £lBO,OOO
for the year—which is not so good as was expected. The
Co. has just secured, as assistant manager, Mr. James
Hebbard, for six years Govt, inspector of mines at Broken
Hill, and before that manager, first of the B.H. Junction,
and then of the Tom’s Lewis Ponds. Hebbard began work
as a boy in a Bendigo mine 20 or 25 vears ago, made good
use of the Bendigo School of Minesj and has kept a level
head most of the time since. He is as good an authority
as there is in Australia on mine-timbering. The State, of
course, expected him to work for just about a third of
what the private co. assesses his services at for a start.
With the help of active metal-markets, a chair-
man of directors, a Melbourne board, an Adelaide board,
a managing director, a general manager, a mine manager,
a business manager, a secretary, an actieg-secretary, and
an Adelaide secretary, Block 14 made last half-year
nearly enough to pay its £15,000 in divs. Nearly
enough—it had to take £1666 from somewhere or other ;
or, not to put too fine a point on it, it increased its debts
by that much and a bit more. What would have hap-
pened if it had lost one of its managers, or if lead had not
steadily advanced to £3 a ton higher than it was last
year, present writer doesn’t know. What it costs the co.
in management he doesn’t know either. In fact, the
Block 14 people have a wonderful gift of silence. Still
they are slowly losing it. This half-year, for instance,
they actually tell shareholders what their sulphide
ore assays on an average (it is 9'soz. silver,
143 per cent, lead, and 10 T per cent, zinc),
and they also announce that the extraction at the
mill equals 69'5 per cent, of the lead and 53'2 per cent,
of the silver. True, they don’t tell yet what zinc is left
in the concentrates ; and so the figures are not a great
deal of use, after all. Nor do they say what were the
carbonates.
* * * *
The information which is dropped into the cracks
between these large masses of silence is, however, not so
very satisfactory that one would like a great deal of it
The ore handled was 75,377 tons—7o,oo6 tons of sulphides
and 5371 tons carbonates. This is 14,000 tons more than
was handled in the preceding half ; and the half’s output
was a record up to then. The gross profit for this 75 377
tons was £21,732. For this write off £7659 for deprecia-
tion of machinery and plant—none too much considering
the heavy wear and tear on mill machinery and the
remarkable rapidity with which mining plant seems
to become obsolete. This gives a true profit of £14,073
assuming that the allowance for depreciation is sufficient
—equalling a true profit of 3s. 9d. per ton all round in-
cluding carbonates. A ton of the latter is probably worth
to the co. at present four or five tons of sulphides : so the
profit from the sulphides would probably not be more
than 3s. a con. This isn’t a margin to get excited about
especially considering the very high price which has ruled
for lead lately.
Of course this does not mean that if lead fell £2
a ton (equal to about 4s. 4d. per ton of Block 14 ore)
Block 14 could not pay divs. What would happen if that
fall occurred, and supposing l no better ore-dressing appli-
ances to be available, would be that the co. would have to
abandon its policy of trying to work all the ore, and
would have to confine itself to only the richer portion. A
rise in working costs has naturally the same effect: so
that when rail and ocean freights recently went up, and
with them the price of coke and coal, the co. had to
shut down one of its smelting furnaces. From
the poorest of these ores the co.’s profit at the best of
times did not reach Is. a ton j but the average cost of
mining, milling, roasting, and smelting them has been
21s. a ton for labor only. Other expenses amount to £1
ss. a ton—for timber, coke, coal, railage, &c. The co.
gets the sprat; “ expenses’’get the mackerel. And the
co. did not even get the sprat lately, and so knocked off
treating the lowest-grade stuff ; hence, in order that the
mackerel might not be lost, the S.A. Govt, and Silverton
Tram Go. reduced the freight on this poorest stuff, in the
hope of bringing it again within the “ pay limit.” But
the smelter is still idle ; the co. says the sprat is still out-
side its reach, unless it can get cheaper water as well as
cheaper carriage. '
Long Tunnel, Vie.: “After figuring as a
regular div. payer for a great many years to the total
tune of over a million, this mine has had to return to the
call-list; £1 call per share on 2400 shares is payable this
week. The co. has no excuse for not having, during its
20 years prosperity, established a reserve fund that would
have enabled it to at least 20 months’ adversity.
Ivanhoe Junction : “ Crosscutting has been
stopped from the 200 ft level; work at present being con-
centrated on sinking the main shaft.” But why sink a
main shaft in such a hopeless position? So far none of
the Ivanhoe or Golden Horseshoe lodes show any sign of
going into the Ivanhoe Junction except at a depth of four
or five thousand feet unless they suddenly run very flat.
Boulder North Extended main shaft down
560 ft. Probably the shaft will be further deepened to the
1000 ft level." But why go to all this expense? Wouldn’t
it be better to wait until the Boulder itself had found
payable gold in the lease which takes in ground north of
the Ivanhoe ? After that comes the Central and West
Boulder and the Boulder No 1, on each of which much
money has been spent and nothing got. After these we
have the Boulder N. Extended, Lake View and Boulder
Junction, and other cats which serve no purpose but
that of wasting money.
• • • •
North Kalgurli: Late development works at-
tended with more encouraging results than for a long
time past. This mine has been another horrible disap-
pointment Because of its position—next the Kalgurli
and adjoining the Associated Northern block—it at one
time had a valuation of nearly half-a-million ; to-day its
market valuation is under £lOO,OOO. Its real value is nil.
It has spent upwards of £lOO,OOO in good mining work, is
ably managed and only wants gold to make it a success.
Again going into reconstruction. Has 25,000 shares un-
issued, but these are of not much use seeing that ordinary
shares are much under par. It will probably spend
another £20,000, and if nothing is found it will then
probably give up what ghost there is.
* * * 9
The Lady Loch, Coolgardie, after two recon-
structions, is having a fairly good time. Crushed for one
year 8431 tons for 64460 z., which gave a profit on mining
and milling of £12,291. Of this expenses in London ana
Westraha absorbed £4,235. When £1,668 is allowed for
depreciation profits are reduced to a little over £6OOO,
which will be probably applied to increasing the orushing
power, erecting cyanide plant, &c.

BRANDY
Last month Broken Hill added another to
the countless host of the tragedies of industry,
although the ordinary fool-jury found, of course,
the ordinary fool-verdict of “ suicide while of un-
sound mind.” A Swiss miner, Manni, though
only 57, had been worn out by “miners’ com-
plaint” contracted in the deep poison workings of
Bendigo ; and after losing one job after another
at the B.H. mines he was told that it was no use
applying any more —he was “ too far gone.” Then
—poor man—he went home, gave his last pay to
his wife, and passed himself out. But Marini’s
case is of most public interest because it draws
attention to the fact that neither the Miners’
Accident Relief Bill lately passed, nor Lyiie’s
Old Age Pensions Bill, will meet the cases of pre-
maturely-worn-out miners, of whom there are
hundreds, especially around the deep mines of
Victoria and the silver-lead mines and works of
N.S.W.
* * • *
During October 29 accidents, three of
them-fatal, occurred amongst N.S.W. railway and
tramway employes. In same month 16 members
of the travelling public met with accidents, two
of them fatal. Thus though the number of
passengers by tram and rail greatly exceeds that
of the railway enipioyes, the number of accidents
was 2to 1 higher among the latter. This explains
t ;e anxiety of the Commissioners to evade respon-
sibility in the case of Campbell, lately decided
against the man, which decision puts the Commis-
sioners in a degree beyond the provisions of the
Employers’ Liability Act.
A couple' of sapient J’s.P. at Caulfield
(Melb.) t’other day indicted a £2 fine on two full-
grown fiends who had deliberately poured kerosene
over a, dog and then set fire to the poor animal.
Had they simply stolen the kerosene or the dog
they would probably have got three or six, or
twelve months apiece. The most surprising thing
about Melbourne is that no J.P. has ever yet been
lynched.
* * * *
It lias not yet been noted what a blistering
was received by the more or less influential
women who had the indecency to sit in court
throughout the trial of Jimmy Governor, In his
statement to the jury the aboriginal, while setting
forth the indignities allegedly suffered by him at
the hands of Mrs. Mawbev and Miss Kerz,
naively added some remarks which were calculated
to make any decent woman pray for the earth to
open and swallow her up.
* * * *
A bench of Coburg (Vic.) magistrates re-
cently sent to Yarra Bend asylum a man who had
been arrested at the gaol gates as insane. He
was then being discharged from prison, having
just completed a sentence of three months, with
two floggings of 10 lashes each, for indecency.
Prima facie evidence that either the Victorian
system manufactures lunatics or that Victoria
gaols and flogs its insane.
Justice without mercy ! A 17-year-old lad,
at Newcastle (N.S.W;), has been sentenced to a
month’s gaol for stealing bags, worth Is. Cd. But
the bags were ti e property of the mighty A.A.
Co.
All the Sydney undertakers’ carriages
having been hired for Ist January to convey dis-
tinguished visitors in the Commonwealth proces-
sion, people are requested to abstain from dying
on 30th and 31st December.
• * • •
Trooper See—whose decision to re-enter
the ranks of the Sydney Lancers, when public
opinion was so strong against his accepting a
lieutenancy in the regiment, was so applauded a
few weeks back —has quietly re-appeared as an
officer. Together with Lyne, Jun., he suddenly
took command of a troop on parade in officer’s full
uniform, sword and all, much to the disgust of the
troopers, who had imagined that the previous out-
cry would have stopped the consummation of a
Eolitical job. If the politicians of the State
ave any more of their relatives and friends who
desire commissions in the mounted regiments the
result will be something like revolt. These posi-
tions have for years been held before the members
of the Volunteer corps as probable prizes, and
when a man who, at considerable annual personal
expense, has striven hard to qualify for a commis-
sion in his regiment finds it presented to an untried
applicant with a political pull, he can hardly be
blamed for chucking up the whole thing in dis-
gust.
Once again: Do the workers of Australia in-
tend the command of the military forces to be in
the hands of a privileged class ? Do they intend
that those forces shall, in the case of industrial
conflict, be ever at the beck and call of men of
fashion and influence, and partisans with consti-
tuted-authority-no-matter-how-constituted ? Are
they content to serve in the ranks while the pay
and the gauds and the glory and the advertise-
ment and the stepping-stones to. place are
monopolised by the sons of the rich and the
politically powerful? Is the man who has the
cheek to say ‘ ‘ Haw ” always necessarily to be
boss ? Is the Australian army to be commanded
by skill and merit alone, or are our men to
be led to slaughter by such vapid nincompoops
(however brave) as the multitude of Imperial
officers who have been the laughing-stocks of
Australians in iS’Africa ? The yarn that officers
must be men of “ social position” is poisonous rot.
“I made my marshals out of mud,” said Napoleon.
* • *
The fairly-certain K.C.M.G.-ship for the
Mayors of Australian cities during the Common-
wealth celebrations year has made the struggle for
the Mayoralties and other alleged honors almost
pathetic. Gillott, of Melbourne, was David
Syme’s nominee in the Turner Govt., and his
fears of defeat at the E. Melb. Parliamentary
election, as he was untried, made him do ridicul-
ous things in the way of choking off likely candi-
dates in opposition to him. Boulder Bill
Brookman, as Mayor of Perth, is sure of his title,
and so now is Ware, of Adelaide, who has a
column about his titled ancestors printed in the
local Register. Gillott has not paraded any
ancestors, but he gets to his handle all the same.

Sydney Morning Herald (which isn’t
built like the worldly Melb. Argus) fought very
shy of particulars about the sensational Coning-
ham divorce-case. As for the Stirling (Melb.)
case, Granny—unlike the ribald Sydney D.T.
(which had the whole thing wired over and
served up hot) —avoided all reference to sundry
sprightly passages about curling-tongs and night-
dresses and giddy things like that, and virtuously
declined to record how many beds were in a room,
or how a Chinese witness, on being shown a photo,
of the re -pondent, ecstatically smacked his pagan
lips and rapturously exclaimed —“ Welly ni’ young
wifee ! ” All which, from the boarding-school
point of view, may be magnificent—but it’s “not
war ! ” The news-reading public is not exclusively
composed of the elderly, unmarried daughters of
ascetic clergymen.

RETURNED INVALID WARRIORS.
What we expected to see. What we do see.
quiet skill and eloquence by barrister Skerrett,

* * * *
Major Cameron, the returned Tasmanian,
was the one man who lifted up his voice at the
recent swashbucklers’ feast arid festivals in Mel-
bourne with the desire to talk horse-sense. At
the first feed to the officers he deprecated much of
the sickening treacle with which the Australians
were being smeared by Price and home-staying
Imperial bounders like Madden and M‘Eacharn.
“ For God’s sake don’t spoil the men with gush !”
was the burden of his plaint, and his plain home
talk for greasy flatterers hit so hard that most of
the “prominent persons” present smiled sadly
and wanly into vacancy, and seemed to wish that
something most unpleasant would suddenly hap-
pen to the man who was damming back their
oratorical sewage.
* * * *
“G.J.M.” supplies a convincing proof
that the plague-scare has fizzled out in Mel-
bourne :
Recently saw half-a-dozen rats lying in front of the
Richmond Town-hall. Evidently had been purchased by
the council, which thus stood a good chance of being
double-banked by any passing Weary Willie. And if
those rats were infected, they certainly gave the plague
a grand chance of spreading, for every wandering cur
mauled them about, and inquiring small boys took them
up and examined them minutely.
The opening of the Hobson’s Bay yacht-
ing season lias become quite a fashionable function
since the bulky Uncle Brassey lent the bright red
light of his countenance to the sport, and on a re-
cent Saturday the Bay was gay with yachts, bunt-
ing, and the liveliest youths of half-a-dozen
waterside suburbs. The most cheerful incident of
the day resulted from the overturning of a small,
ewe-necked, ten-ribbed canoe containing two
dirty, irresponsible 12-year-old beachcombers from
Port Melbourne. The kids rose to the occasion,
bestrode their wreck, and threw out frantic signals
of distress. The most gorgeous yacht of the
sivarra, manned by the most exquisite salts in all
3t. Kiida, fell out of the manoeuvres, and after
fetching-up alongside the castaways with con-
siderable difficulty, found its good offices repu-
diated. “We don’ wanter be rescued, mister,”
said the perkier of the two; “butforGor’ sake
give us a dry cigarette.”

Mr. John Osborne* sec. of N.S.W. Public
Service Association, writes to The Bulletin,
with tears in his ink, to point out this paper’s
grievous error in stating (l/12/’OO) that
At last meeting of the council a long and earnest dis-
cussion as to whether it was more advisable to sweep out
the Govt, offices with tea-leaves or sawdust ended after a
close fight in a victory for the sawdust party.
The point, protesteth Mr. Osborne, “ was not tea-

sweeping, and the discussion did not occupy more
than seven or eight minutes.” It is, indeed, com-
forting to know that the P.S.A. can thus promptly
deal with momentous issues. The Bulletin
regrets having approached in so cavalier a. spirit
a matter “at the bottom of which,” according to
Mr. 0., “are Life and Death ! ”

A HEAVY WARRIOR.
RETURNED CONTINGRNTER : “ O chase me girl[?]
I’m chock-full of bullets!”
That yarn (reprinted by Sydney D.T.
from a Yankee paper) about the alleged recent
discovery by a Parisienne of a uranium-compound
which, while giving a light like that of day, emits
no heat and practically lasts for ever, is doubtless
a hoax. If it isn’t, why did not the cable-liar
long ago tell us of an invention which would dis-
count coal-gas 50 per cent, and break-up all the
electric-light companies—this instead of maunder-
ing in the ample space at his command about
the doings of that eternal bore, “Sir Langdon
Bonython? ”
* » * *
Charming outflow of slush from Rev.
Roseby at the recent Sydney Sunday-Closing
meeting
Why, he asked, had the Lord’s day Act of Charles 11.
remained unaltered upon the Statute' Book? It was be-
cause they felt that they still needed it, and could not
improve upon it, otherwise the intelligence and conscience
of the English people would long ago have struck that
Act off.
Does Dr. Roseby really not know that the Act he
admires so much was passed to compel people of
his own particular sect to attend the parish
church, which they abhorred ? Dr. Roseby’s
knowledge of history evidently doesn’t run to the
fact that, when that Act was passed, people of his
way of thinking could not hold civic office unless
they attended the parish church once every Sun-
day and took the sacrament in the same place at
least once a year. Which do Roseby and 00.
value most—liberty of conscience or the right to
bind their neighbours’ consciences?
* * * *
Fred. J. Broomfield on Vic. Artists’
Society’s annual exhibition at Melbourne :
There is nothing that smites a man from Sydney in this
dead City of the Horse so much as the'absolute in-
difference, amounting almost to scorn, of everything
artistic and literary ; “ but,” says Marshall-Hall, “ admit-
ting all this, and more also, are we not great on music ?”
The Victorian Artists’ 1900 Exhibition is held in a room
in which most men would be ashamed to receive a
common, ordinary guest. It is a sort of an 11 x 8 affair,
and the exhibits number 76. Nerli, in Bulletin, l/12/’OO,
speaks, pathetically of two societies in Sydney cutting
each others’ throats. In Melb. this doesn’t happen.
There is really no society, and the simulacrum of such
society as the place boasts would not worry whose throat
was cut, throats of the members included, for they would
but drip milk-and-water 1 Broadly speaking, Art in
Melbourne is lower than Dante’s seventh-circle. A lot of
rubbish (mark well ! out of seventy-six exhibits) repre-
sents— Nothing ! McCubbin is there, and Tranthim-
Fryer, and J. Mather with a portfolio of water-color
drawings. And this trinity simply saves the show from
absolute wreck. The exhibition is predominantly
feminine—missy, with plenty of the reduced photo.,
which Mile, calls a miniature, rioting feebly on a plush-
shield.

Melbourne municipalities already squabble
over the landing-place of the “dook.” The
M'ShieD favoured Port Melb. ; with the advent of
Turner St. Kilda bobs up. Brassey and Deeming
came that way, while Hopetoun landed at the
Port. Why not give Footscray a show ? Or why
not build a light-draught junk and bring the
Yorks up Reilly-street drain, disembarking just
below Mark Foy’s ?
Colonel Gordon
recently ordered a
march-out of the
S.A. army on
Sunday after-
noon, prepara-
tory to a sham-
fight next day.
Pious Adelaide
took offence at once, the
headmasters of both Colleges
forbidding their cadets to take part. Those
who did turn up were marched out by the
judicious Gordon to the strains of ‘'Onward,
Christian Soldiers,” which, of course, made every-
thing all right. A number of the boys left behind
joined the camp at 1 a.m. on Monday.

Melbourne is evidently convalescing of its
Jingo-fever. A man was recently caught de-
facing the specimen illustrations on news-agents’
hoardings by slashing a penknife across “ Bobs’ ”
phiz and writing insulting remarks on Kitchener’s
evil countenance. Yet he wasn t even charged
with sedition or high treason—simply with de-
facing property valued at Is., and P.M. Panton
let him go with a mere enjoinder to “get some
exercise and cool down.” Now what would have
happened to that man last May ?
* * • *
A very big crowd turned out in Melbourne
to welcome “Fire low” Price and the young men
he led home from the war, and for an hour-and-a-
half fat Colonel Tom, looking in the face remark-
ably like Widow O’Brien with a shabby khaki
moustache, and sitting his horse like a feather-
bed, was a greater and more glorious warrior than
Bobs,” Kitchener, and Red vers Buller all rolled
into one. The crowd whooped him up all along
the line, and clung to his ’onest, ’orny hand, and
hugged his martial legs with patriotic enthusiasm
inflamed by beer, and a popular impression that
Price had saved the Empire by hurling his shape-
less bulk down a steep place on top of Kruger and
the Boers was evident throughout the length and
breadth of the procession. But Tom did not sit
it out ; he succumbed to various ailments and
pressure of circumstances in Collins-st. on the
return journey, and was removed from his horse
and put to bed by sympathetic friends.
* * * *
As a pageant the procession was a chaotic
failure. The crowd simply walked over the in-
funated police at the outset, and took possession
of the thin brown line, of heroes, and after that
the grand march consisted of scraps of squads
battling along a road packed with squealing fe-
males and red, raucous men. And how the sex
did squeal! Listening for these enthusiastic,
deep-chested, British cheers that were going to be
faithfully reported in next morning’s paper, one
heard only trie squalling, yelling, and yapping of
women > an( l the abiding visual expression
left by the spectacle is that of a large, crimson,
more or less dishevelled female fighting
to get an affectionate “ half-Nelson ” on the near-
est coffee-coloured swaddie. The immense matron
was generally backed up by two or more giggling
damsels, with hats awry and hair all over the shop,
who helped themselves after the older battler had
done hugging and kissing the miserable victim.
The dus f y soldiers got very sick of the hugger-
mugger before they had worried their way through
a quarter-of-a-mile of it, and they were not always
too polite in inviting fair enthusiasts to “take,
oh, take those lips away,” nor were they particu-
larly gentle in putting aside proffered cheeks.
Returning to the barracks after Princes Bridge
was passed, all attempts to keep order were aban-
doned. Respectable citizens hoisted soldiers on
to their shoulders and, with the khakied legs em-
bracing their necks, puffed and sweated through
dust and heat with their bellowing burdens. Girls
of the factory-rat type and style simply swarmed
over the Tommies, and in one instance a howling
push of blushless damsels made a great effort to
carry a fat Tasmanian, the struggle ending in the
lot going down in a bunch, with the plunging
Boer-hunter on ton.
Two or three returned nurses driven in an open
landau by a liveried menial comprised a dramatic
feature of the Melbourne procession to the honor
and glory of T. Price. The nurses were in full
Red-cross fig, and three sick swaddies sat by their
sides, looking haggard and sentimental. The
nurses assumed the devoted-sister pose quite suc-
cessfully, and had the happiness of being the most
conspicuous item after “good old Price.” But
there were irreverent wretches who yelled, “Was
that all you captured, miss?” and inquired
if they were “going back for any more.”
With a view to improving the occasion, the Melb.
W.C.T.U. seized upon “Bobs’” message re the
iniquity of feeding returned warriors on beer, and
they prepared great posters appealing to the
uhlio to forbear. These were hung on all the
leal hoardings at considerable expense in the
nope of converting the welcome into a teetotal
function. A moving spectacle was arranged by
four boozy youths in African khaki grouped in a
limp, beery tableau right under one of the flaring
posters aforesaid, singing “Shons of the Queen”
and “Sholdiers of the Shea.”
* * * •
The most prominent decoration in Mel-
bourne, on the day of the last returned-soldiers’
“procession,” was a huge banner extended across
Collins-street with the device, “ Maote virtute
P.ueri.” This was possibly intended as an adapta-
tion of Virgil’s “Macte nova virtute puer”
( ‘‘ Be honoured, lad, for your early valour”), but
the adaptor apparently did not know that in
Latin a plural noun requires an adjective in the
same number. The Age said that this ungram-
matical atrocity was ‘‘ an old Latin greeting
happily thought out.” After all, ignorance is the
essential qualification for a Jingo.

In a Richmond River (N.S.W.) court
t’other day defendant grimaced at plaintiff in the
box while the P.M.’s attention was fixed on some
papers. Plaintiff replied by putting his thumb to
his nose and twiddling his fingers. Not to be
outdone, defendant put up both hands in an ex-
tended fashion from his nasal organ. The alert
policeman actually noticed these things and, on
the lunars being reported, the P.M. let the pair
off with a caution.

A Wagga (N.S.W.) trooper—the ( gentle*
' man proudly gives his name in full—‘Writing from
B’ Africa to Wagga Advertiser, after describing
how the Church of England service was read over
the graves of a couple of the writer’s comrades by
the R.C. chaplain, Father Patrick, calmly
proceeds:
From grave to gay and that in a thrice (sic). An hour
afterwards we were burning down the farm houses in the
vicinity of the last scene. At one farm the woman was
given ten minutes to get her bedding out. An ancient
organ had been dragged out to the front of the house,
and as I rode up to direct the party under me, I found
one of our nigger waggon drivers comfortably seated at
the organ singing to his own accompaniment, “ Home,
Sweet Home,” while another sable imp was mischievously
lighting another fire underneath it. Such is an incident
of life on the veldt. These methods may be questioned,
but it is the only way to effectively punish treachery and
deceit. For the same reason the other night, when
ordered out at midnight, we proceeded in deadly silence,
with bayonets fixed, with instructions not to shoot, bub
to use bayonet and butt —and take no prisoners. We
tala no more notice of shrieks for mercy than we would
of the cawing of a crow. You won’t be able to under-
stand these things over there, but they are the correct
thing here, and the only thing to impress Boer natures.
How proud Australia should be of it all !
And here you have it again—this time from a
valorous and modest Gippslander (who also gives
his name !) :
We took possession of the Knights railway-station, and
made prisoners of the stationmaster and porter, and I,
being a corporal, was placed in charge of these prisoners,
with an escort of two men, with instructions to follow
the raiding party. Our men went off, and I followed
with the two prisoners. . . . Just then I felt a stinging
sensation in my shoulder, but I would not give in, but
gave them several shots as we rode along. I found after-
wards the wretches had sniped me from a mullock heap
on my left rear. I was going to shoot the prisoners, but
to 1113' surprise I found I had not a shot left in my bando-
lier or pouch, so I and my men had to ride for our lives.
... You may bet the Sale boys upheld the credit of the
town.
Once more: A Groperland trooper to the West
Australian (italics Bulletin’s) :
. . . A curious incident arose out of the capture of
another Boer. Colonel De Lisle was with one of the
Colonial African officers of the Border Horse, when one
of our scouts brought in a six-foot prisoner, with a long
beard. The Colonel suggested his riding back and getting
an escort to take the prisoner to the Colonial Division.
The other officer said, “ Never mind, I’ll take him.” With
that he spurred his horse, caught the Boer by the whiskers,
and galloped off.
And —— ?. And ? And now the daily press
is publishing cables setting forth how humane the
colonials always were, according to Lord Roberts !
It is only necessary to read what they proudly
write about themselves. “ Gentlemen Every-
where ! ”
• * * m
It is a genial custom in certain Australian
towns to dog any couple that may be taking an
evening ramble. Recently at Mt. Morgan an
irate swam, before taking his girl for a stroll,
armed himself with a gun, with which he succeeded
in potting a too inquisitive person who followed
him. He has now to stand his trial.

The fervent damsels who hugged the
returned warriors in Melb. the other day, as the
latter struggled through the overwhelming crowd,
were no doubt carried away by their enthusiasm —
not for hugging but for valor. But the thousands
of smartly dressed girls who didn’t want to hug
the soldiers respected their own beauty and the
P.D. corset which adorned them. To the graceful
girl in the P.D., every day is a day of triumphant
conquest, as every occasion graced by female
beauty is a triumph for the P.D.*
Simplest—Most Durable—Best.
