A WOMANS LETTER.
Sydney, December 11, 1900.
My Dear Myee.—
Nance O’Neil! Somehow the name suggests
hard work. Somebody says washing and ironing,
seeing brawny arms in the “ O’Neil,” and a will
to use them in the “Nance.” And truly, what a
worker ! Last week’s programme is unprece-
dented. She was Lady Macbeth on Friday,
Camille on Saturday, Lady Teazle on Monday,
La To.sea on Tuesday, Hedda Gabler on Wednes-
day afternoon, Magda at night, Queen Elizabeth
on Thursday, Fedora on Friday, Camille again on
Saturday afternoon, and at night, Magda, and
Hedda, and The Jewess, and Lady Teazle, and a
new part as a Tough Girl all combined. Possibly
there isn’t another young actress in the world
getting through such a rapid succession of heavy
emotional, exhausting star parts.
At the “ Hedda Gabler” Matinee a houseful of
women—including Madame Agnes Jansen—pon-
dered on the unlikeness of Hedda to Me. Once
upon a time it was the thing to gauge characters
by the ways of Other Women, but nowadays Me
is the only woman any stage woman is to be
com pared to. The Me Craze threatens to knock
out great dramatists’ reputations. Would Me
have married a man I didn’t love ? Oh, no!
Would Me have given a man a pistol, and urged
him to die beautifully ? Oh, no! Would Me
have burned his great work, and been rude to the
aunt of my husband, and suicided with a loud re-
port in the next room, and three people sitting on
the other side of the curtain and listening. Oh,
no ! Would Me have worn a dead-white wrapper
with a face like the second day at sea, and not a
sign of fringe. Oh, no, no, no !
Nance moves with more restraint and less back-
sliding than of yore through the part. Also, she
looks quite her worst in the first act, which argues
great things for an actress under 30—and not
under compulsion—who dares look her worst in
Act 1. Unnecessarily, too, for in this act Hedda’s
husband keeps on remarking how beautiful Hedda
is—and as no woman ever looked that in a fat,
green face, with pale hair scraped off her forehead,
and a dead-white gown, one can only assume
that Nance is not aware how she slays her
beauty. Joseph Carne, as Mr. Hedda, disguises
splendidly under a red beard, and is much easier
in his acting ; Josephine Thynne sits up all night
in a pink striped evening dress with a big black
velvet bow, in a gold wig ; and Mrs. Henry
Bracey well becomes the new bonnet of George
Tesman’s aunt.
That bonnet makes me thoughtful. Why
are male writers so unjust to their women ?
That incident of Hedda and the bonnet. How
dare Ibsen draw it ? Beautiful Hedda in a silk
gown knows it is the kind aunt’s new little bit of
millinery, bought to please her. Seeing it on a
chair, she spitefully speaks of it as some
old thing the maid left lying about. I call that
wanton cruelty—on the part of the author.
It reminds me of Thackeray, and a bit of odious
injustice ht does Becky. Hounding her down, he
loses his head and gives way to insult. Do you
remember Becky and Amelia at the ball, Becky
rushed with partners and swamped with admira-
tion, and little, pale Amelia sitting alone, a
mournful wallflower? Thack. marches radiant
Becky up to the wallflower and makes her utter a
most crushing comment on the cut of Amelia’s
frock. No, no! With apologies to Thackeray
and Ibsen there’s a limit even to the nasty woman.
Spiteful remarks about clothes are always kept for
times of provocation, not thrown away unneces-
sarily. Your nasty woman doesn’t fire her dead-
liest weapon off in time of peace ; that is to say,
when she is being admired herself.
Saturday night at Her Majesty’s, and the new
order of things, the woman’s house. A few men,
of course. But one has only to watch Nance take
her “ curtains,” only to note the smile she casts
up almost painfully at her house, to know that
smile isn’t masculine gender—engendered, I
mean. They say that the best of everything is
done for men. I don’t know. Nance gives
one the impression that her audience is all
common gender as far as she is concerned. She
was lavishly applauded, flowers and call after call,
and the house broke up after half-past 11 with a
farewell speech—the tremolo stop in that big
golden voice well out —from Nance, Kingston and
Rankin.
At the opera! Magic sound! A box at
Faust ! No low-born heroine but tasted life out
of a box at Faust. Is it not so ? And this
romantic city crowded it all the week, preferring
Parlate d’Amor to poor old worn Ah Che.
Women liked to see the clean-shaven devil with
the nice smile, knowing that black moustache and
chin-tufts are out of fashion with their men.
Everyone admires the pretty little youthful air of
fair Lilian Coomber, the round-cheeked Mar-
guerite. Her singing is also young—and fair.
Of course the tenor has not the lean and hungry
look unknown to tenors. Perhaps his likeness
to a well-known artist is the kindest comment on
his looks permissable. Miss Jansen, the dramatic
contralto, is the only member of the new warblers
to make a decided hit. She does not appear in
“Faust,” but after her gypsy in “ Trovatore” one
looks forward to seeing her again. Her reputa-
tion as a six-barrelled linguist gains from a glimpse
of her intellectual brown-eyed face.
By the way, I hear that one lady in the cast
so fetched the local critics with her ankles that
they burst into enthusiastic praise of her voice—
which is not —well, it’s a contralto, but not nearly
on a par with those ankles.
Speaking of art, friends of Mr. B. E. Minns
read with interest that Colonel Rhodes has given
him a commission to paint a portrait. Yet B. E.
M. never set up here as a portrait-painter. Also
Percy Spence is coming to draw our Common-
wealth Show for the Graphic.
Later developments of the Commonwealth re-
port that everyone who can manage it is going to
get away till the shows are over. Society finds
nothing in its line provided. In fact, women
generally are turning up their noses, and only the
country people keep their interast at bouing-
point. In Sydney, it cools perceptibly every day.
However, the crowds will be great, and I am
asked to mention that splendid seats at half-a-
guinea are available in Mr. Julius Caro’s grounds,
“ Melrose,” Queen-street, Woollahra, the pro-
ceeds to go to the Children’s Hospital.
As for those steps that the emaciated Hopetoun
has to “toddle” up—to use his own expression—
at Mrs. Macquarie’s Chair, they seem to me like
a veiled invitation to “walk into my parlor” said
the Premier to the fly, for the Premier of this
State will take the place . But I musn’t go
on in this way.
Have you heard of the two new engagements—
Mr. Justice Stephen and Miss Huthwaite, Mr.
Patterson and Miss Aline Fowler ?
Not a society function has been the ecclesiastical
divorce case, but women who wouldn’t dream of
going into the Court have crowded like flies round
a photographer’s frame in King-street, where
Dr. O’Haran’s smiling portrait stands out from
the middle of a galaxy of photos, of well-known
Sydney women.
By the way, on dit that a member of the legal
profession is qualifying for a billet as clown in
Fitzgerald’s circus.
Speaking of judges, I see that Mr. Justice Owen
is one of the judges of the ode competition. His
poetical attainments rest on those verses he wrote
(when at the Bar) some fifteen years ago, on the
Anderson-Fairfax divorce-case —I mean libel-suit
—about somebody’s stocking, wasn’t it ? They
were considered very funny, as everything is
funny in court. The other judges are Mr. Du
Faur, and Mr. Alex. Oliver of the Land Appeal
Court, but rumor is darkly silent concerning any
poetry in their past. Mr. Nugent Robertson lent
an air of reality to this weird board for a while,
but now I hear he has resigned on account of tho
ode he has himself written for the competition,
and the poetry will just have to be judged by
equity and kilometres.
Last Monday Mr. Hawtrev welcomed his new
English company—come to play “A Message
from Mars ” at the Palace—in a most successful
way. Invitation cards to afternoon tea at The
Australia brought a few hundred guests, theatrical
and society. It was a big puzzle to find who was
not of the co. Every pretty girl in Sydney seems
included. Another puzzler is, what are they all
going to do? I believe some are even under-
studies to understudies, but you couldn’t read that
in their faces. They all looked engaged and im-
portant. Among the Sydney girls were Roxy
Barton, in white with a floppy black hat; Winnie
Austin, in a black and white muslin and blue
toque ; Madge Hope, Laura Hanley, Ida Wilshire,
Vera Eyeton, and Lena Brasch.
Miss Ruby Ray, the new English star, a dark,
graceful young beauty, was frocked simply in
crush-strawberry cotton and black hat. Fair-
haired Mrs. Herbert Ross was in cream and a
blue trimmed hat. Herbert Ross looks a comedian,
a loosely-knit little man in a loosely-knit mous-
tache. Their clean-cut beauty-man, Mr. Holding,
is a combination of Correze Thursby and our
own late delicately-chiselled Earl. Miss Alma
Allen, in black tailor-made, is an English-looking
figure, tall and plump, with fair hair dressed on
her neck. So far the huge company gambols
about like one happy family.
As showing how the wheels work. The wife of
the Sunday-closing N.S.W. Premier presented
the wife of the Lieut.-Governor, on the latter
lady’s departure Europe-wards by express the
other Sunday evening, with a basket of flowers.
As absolutely fresh blossoms were insisted on, the
employes of a city firm were kept at work all the
Sabbath collecting and making up the flowers.
Yet to sell flowers in Sydney on Sunday is a penal
offence.
Admirers have strolled round Mr. Fox’s pic-
tures all the week, but the red ticket hasn’t
flourished about the frames. And yet such a one-
man show is rarely (or never) seen here. I hardly
know where to begin. “ The Lady in Black” is a
familiar title, since Our Gallery contains another
of that name, and attracts one first of all.. A tall,
laughing, rounded girl with red-gold hair, and a
skin that you want to touch, so deliciously does it
round off the canvas. That’s a feature of Mr.
Fox’s portraits. They do not lean back,
lie back, or stand back in the canvas. They
come out, the flesh curves out, humanly and
artfully. You must have noticed that in his child
portrait in our Gallery, and you will observe the
same characteristic here again and again. “My
Cousin ” is a graceful female in pink ; but I like
better a little girl called “Zephyr,” with
adorable red hair and a brainy face. Mr.
Fox pays his women the compliment of put-
ting a meaning behind their prettiness. Also,
there are some tree-scapes here that go straight to
one’s heart—big green pictures with a human note
in the gum-trees, and a fine poetic feeling pervad-
ing them all.
But what is the matter with local purses that
Art inevitably snaps them tighter ? It can’t be
shrinkage, considering the wonderful goose-and-
turkey-yarns floating around, and the fabulous
prices Sydney is going to give for ducks. No.
Fashion smiles another way. She doesn’t look
towards pictures. The good old days, when nice,
fat men strolled round art-shows, and wrote cheques,
and had big pictures by local artists on their
walls, threaten a severe collapse.
Yet all the time, is there a bride who wouldn’t
thank you more for one well-signed picture than
for eleven butter-dishes? Pictures are furniture,
everything is furniture to a bride. But even on
that unexalted plane nobody gives them to her.
and the eleven butter-dishes knock each other
spitefully alongside the seventeen toast-racks, eat-
ing their hearts out among the cobwebs of the
back pantry.
I wouldn’t dream of recommending it as a
bridal present, but look at Tom Roberts’ portrait
of Henry Parkes. That was one of two or
three good portraits of the old man. Thirty
guineas was wanted. No buyer. Perhaps an
artist makes a mistake in setting out all his odds
and ends at a show of this kind. But even the
best pictures at both these one-man shows didn’t
elicit one truly respectable bid.
A friend writing from London says Lady Car-
rington does not look a day older than when she
left here, although her hair is quite grey. It is
very becoming to her. At the time my corres-
pondent wrote there was nothing talked of but
the Elections. “ The knowledge,” she says,
“ that women have of politics and the interest
they take in all public questions would astonish
some of our Upper House members who are
opposed to Woman Suffrage. It is quite usual
for wives and daughters to be beside their hus-
bands and fathers on the platform, and in one
instance, where the candidate was taken ill just
after he had begun to speak, his wife continued
and finished his speech, and subsequently carried
the election.” The same correspondent writes:
“Ellen Terry, appearing with Irving in ‘Olivia,’
looked a sad burlesque. We wouldn’t stand such
a thing in Australia. A stout, elderly lady play-
ing the r6le of a beautiful girl.”
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“ There will be a Mrs. Smiler Peacock after Ist
Jan. next, the wedding of the Man who Laughs
having been fixed for that momentous day. The cere-
mony will take place at Warrnambool, and there
is likely to be a political gathering. The Speaker
will probably be there to see that the question
is put in proper form ; but the fact that the Com-
monwealth celebrations will be taking place in
Sydney will make the wedding comparatively
quiet.
“ Tom Tatchell, co-respondent in the well-
advertised Stirling divorce-case, was for years one
of Victoria’s best tennis-players. His is the
usual bandboxy masher style of bank clerk beauty
so indigenous to Australian country towns. His
uncle represented Dunolly in Vic. Assembly for
some time. The petitioner, Dr. Stirling, is one
of Melbourne’s ablest operating surgeons, and is
a scion of the big drapery house of that name.
“In ‘The Scarlet Feather’ Nellie Stewart’s
clothes, in their way, are quite delicious. With
her first little, brown, glove-fit suit, goes a red
cap, casting a becoming glow over her cheeks,
which she is almost too careful not to over-color.
This first rig-out of Pippo’s, by the way, is giving
great offence in some quarters. The wearer looks
as if she had been poured into the brown riding-
breeches, and though there’s a knapsack
at the back, there’s no basque, sash or
sich in front to blur the outlines objected to.
Anyhow, that small suit is keeping some easily-
shocked people away from the theatre. Friends
have come home from ‘ The Scarlet Feather’ and
assured absentees that Nellie must have been
measured inch by inch for her clothes. Some
alarmists even raise a scare by surmising that
casts have been taken of the daring dresser by
female plaster-moulders, and then the models
handed over to theatrical tailors.
“ The page’s next wear is a Court suit of black
and white in which he looks larger, staider and
paler and trots out some of his jewels. Lastly
‘ Pippo,’ who is an actress in disguise, appears in
a loose robe of lace over pink, achieving a flesh-
tint effect and following all the curves of the
lady’s uncorseted figure. The lace gleams all over
with the most wonderful spangles, ‘moonbeams,’,
‘fireflies,’ ‘ blue devils.’ Round the corsage a jet
background doubly enhances the blaze of jewels.
A broad belt of diamonds round the dazzler’s
neck hangs down in one end in front, her hair is
latticed with brilliants and crested with plumes,
and a girdle of enormous turquoises completes the
toilette. Then there’s a lovely cloak, pale-blue,
shimmering with jewels and edged with fluted
pink chiffon.
“The latest feminine craze in Melbourne is to
wear the hat jabbed over the eyes & la Nellie
Stewart.
“It has not so far been noted in print that the
success of the recent Melbourne grand opera sea-
son was almost entirely a cheap-admission success.
The rich, who are supposed to have a peculiar
possession of culchaw, simply stayed away.
Being merely, for the most part, as Higinbotham
called them, ‘ wealthy lower orders,’ they prefer
leg-shows, short-skirted exhibitions, entertain-
ments which lie light on the chest after a too-
heavy dinner. The gallery folks, mostly people
whs work for a living but haven’t too much cash,
pulled the season through. The dress-circle
was consistently ill-patronised, and the gal-
lery invariably crammed with an audience,
most of whom had paid extra early-door fees,
understood the music, and were able to enjoy it
to the full. In the circumstances, it is surely bad
policy not to make the gallery more comfortable.
The cushioned, roomy, low seats ought to be there,
and the high, hard, carpeted benohes in the dress
circle.
“Mrs. W. S. Lyster, professionally known in
the old days as Miss Georgina Hodson, was a fre-
quent visitor at Melb. Princess’s when Musgrove’s
grand-opera co. held the floor. Mrs. Lyster is
not quite so young, but almost as sprightly, as
when she eloped with her William from the N. Y.
theatre, where she was the rage as Pocahontas, in
the burlesque. The story goes that the fair eloper
went off before the manager could provide a sub-
stitute, so the comedians, Walcot and Brougham,
had to rattle through the piece without any
Pocahontas.
“ The selection of one Franklin Peterson to the
Melb. University Chair of Music, vice that wicked
man Marshall Hall, has a strong Presbyterian
smell that argues all sorts of things. Though 39
years of age Mr. Peterson’s Mus. Bac., Oxon,
dates as late as 1892, and his list of qualifications
reads like those of the late despised Tomlinson.
He has a brother who is principal of a Canadian
University; another now dead, was a prof, of
Sanskrit at Bombay. As for Mr. Peterson him-
self, he seems to have gained fame by becoming
organist and choir-master to the ‘Palmerston
Place church,’ whatever that may be; and as a
final testimonial the public are told that he has
been teacher of pianoforte at the Edinburgh
Ladies’ College ! Marshall-Hall will now start a
conservatorium of his own, and there is little
doubt it will flourish exceedingly.
“ Widow O’Brien’s only chick in real life, Bertie
Sheridan by name, is a most vivacious youngster,
Stayed with his dad and mother at the Grand
Hotel, Melb., recently, and was given to amusing
the guests greatly by his anti-military views and
his smart mimicry. ‘ Would you like to go
to the war, Bertie ? ’ one admirer asked. ‘ Not
much ; I’m not taking any war, thanks. I’d
rather go on the comic-opera stage.’ Used to
give one little scene which always took his
audience immensely. Bertie being the relative
of a S’African contingenter receives a letter,
seizes the missive, tears it open with trembling
fingers. ‘Ha! it concerns my darling Tom at the
front. Thank Gawd, he is shot.’ Staggers to the
nearest seat and bursts into sobs of relief.
“ A certain Melb. solicitor got away from all his
liabilities long ago owing to the land-boom and
possibly another woman. His wife lately applied
for a divorce, on the ground of desertion. She
told the Judge that, in February, 1889, her hus-
band misappropriated some trust - money and
bolted to South America. The last she saw of
him was when he came home one afternoon with
‘ a quantity of gold,’ some of which she stitched
into his clothing or otherwise helped to conceal
about his absconding person. Then he caught
train for Adelaide, and left her to pine in grass-
widowhood all these years. Up to ’9B she corres-
ponded with him occasionally, her letters being
wifely petitions for coin or for a new home in the
land of his adoption, whilst hubby’s replies were
friendly expressions of regret that he could do
nothing to assist her. The Judge reserved his
decision, but gave it as a first opinion that there
can be no ‘ desertion ’ in the case of a solicitor who
bolts with the full approval and connivance of his
better-half. So the next family-lawyer who wants
to clear out will find his wife a less unsuspicious
helpmate than the poor trusting woman afore-
mentioned was in days of yore.
“Vic. Art Society this year held its poor little
exhibition right in the city. Just before the open-
ing, the controlling Govt, department sent word
that the show could not be held, as the exhibition
room had not sufficient egress in case of a panic
inside. After some parley the artists arranged to
alter one door, and paint ‘ Escape Door ’ on
another. The first Saturday they advertised a
piano recital. Half-an-hour after the advertised
time of the recital there were in the room a lady
and gentleman, the secretary, and the pianiste ;
after a painful pause, there remained the sec. and
the pianiste only. Very soon it was suggested to
turn the doors round, in case there should be a
panic in the streets.
“The amount paid by Vic. National Gallery
for the new picture ‘ Horses Bathing in the Sea ’
by Miss Kemp-Welch, a London painter, was
£7OO. The amount paid (also just lately) for ‘A
Bush Symphony ’ by Ford Patterson, a local
painter, was £2OO. Of course you can hardly
compare two works so different in subject and
treatment. The horse picture is a frank, bright,
vigorous, unimaginative, pleasant statement of a
natural scene —a rather fine-and-large colored
photograph. The bush picture is full of thought
and feeling, atmosphere and tone. It is a painted
poem ; it doesn’t suggest the camera in a single
inch of it. Present writer would have thought
£7OO for Patterson’s and £2OO for Miss Kemp-
Welch’s picture more reasonable. One would not
grumble much at the price paid for the London
lady’s enjoyable work, which may be worth it,
and will be very popular, but one would contend
that if she is sufficiently paid, then Ford Patterson
is grossly underpaid. Is it any wonder that good
Australian artists have to go to England to earn
bread, to say nothing of butter ? Let any
Melbourne person who cares for art make the
comparison and judge whether this comment is
not justified.
Yours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

One of the dangers of Sydney during the holi-
day season will be Che burglar and sneak-thief. The
Federation festivities will attract all the rascaldom of
Australasia, and plate and purses will be constantly
menaced. Those who are leaving Sydney, and visitors
to Sydney, therefore should deposit their plate, cash
and valuables in the Sydney Safe Deposit for security
Access can be had to them at any time during business
hours. The vaults are situated behind Paling’s, off
George-street, and are always open for inspection. They
are in themselves a sight worth seeing.*

4 "Vosa”: Colonel Proctor, a Fijian planter
in "the early seventies, was
bit of a character. He lost
a leg at the battle of
Chancellorsville, fighting
for the Confederates
when a youngster of 20.
The substitute was so
fine a piece of workman-
ship that the Colonel
danced at a Melb. ball
without anyone discovering
his deficiency. Whin he
first landed in Fiji a score
of natives scrambled on to
the deck of the steamer to
gratify their curiosity. Proctor
gave one of them sixpence to
pull the boot off his cork leg,
having first loosened the
fastenings. The Fijian put
forth his strength as request-
ed, and fell head over heels
with the whole limb in his hands.
Springing to his feet, the terrified
darkie leapt into the sea, accom-
f anied by all his companions. In
'iji the Colonel, getting tired
of travelling with a screw-driver
and oil-can in his pocket, dis-
carded the artificial leg and
went about with a crutch.
But le was wonderfully
active. On the occasion of an
accident to a boat’s crew of
H.M. schooner Alacrity, off the
coast of Vitu Levu, he swam
half-a-mile in the shark-infested
sea to their assistance,
and subsequently re-
ceived a medal from
the Royal Humane
Society for saving life.
His cotton - growing
speculations on the Ba
river came to an abrupt
end in consequence of the
discovery that his foreign laborers had been
kidnapped (unknown to him at the time) in
the notorious slave-brig Carl. All the “boys”
had to be surrendered to the British authorities.
For several years after this Proctor was captain
of a South Sea trading vessel. Getting into a row
with a Frenchman at the New Hebrides, he was
chained by the waist to a cocoanut tree for a week.
He was able to roam 15 yards from the tree, and
his own account was that he felt like a monkey on
a barrel-organ. As he knew nothing of naviga-
tion he travelled by dead-reckoning. Never
heard what eventually became of him. The
finale must have been something picturesque.
“ Potato ” Young, ex-Speaker of N.S.W.
Assembly, objects to the name of Mt. Kosciusko,
on the ground that it is Russian. And, after all,
when you come to think of it, it is really impos-
sible for a man to be any good unless his name is
Bull, or Brown, or Young.

Cabled that “before his death, Sir Arthur
Sullivan ” —original family name Solomon, by the
way—“composed a new setting for the ‘Te
Deum,’ for use after the proclamation of peace in
South Africa.” The cause of the composer’s
demise is so far unstated. Mail news will prob-
ably reveal it as a stroke of lightning or a shower
of brimstone. God surely can’t let blasphemy be
stretched too far.
J. B. Burton, Victoria’s new Mines
Minister, was 10 or 12 years ago general president
of the Miners’ Association ; that was in the days
when the one association covered Vic., N.S.W.,
Tasmania, S.A., and part of Queensland. Burton
even as a union boss was a silent man. W. G
Spence, now Labor M.P. in N.S.W., but then
miners’ secretary at Creswick and general secre-
tary of the association, had to do the talking for
the two —and did it without turning a hair.
Spence gradually drifted away from the Vic.
miners because they did not care to mix unionism
and politics ; Burton was more in sympathy with
the keeping of the two separate. Yet Burton
gets the higher political reward.
BEYOND IMITATION.
ONE OF THEM: “ There’s one thing there’s no
mistaking—and that’s a really fine figure.”
Millionaire Tyson once remarked that he
would much like to see Queen Victoria ; but when
shown the sort of dress in which he would have to
appear at court he said: “I would not put that
on if they gave me all England.” By the way,
the stories told her about Tyson made Lady
Carrington feel much interested in the old man,
whom she several times tried to meet, but all her
little traps failed.
A certain “doctor’’who
practised in Sydney some years
ago as an “eye-specialist” pro-
fessed also to have an infallible
cure for snake-poisoning, and
arranged to read a paper on the
subject before the Royal
Society. The late Gerard
Krefft, Curator of the Austra-
lian Museum at the -time, at-
tended the meeting with two
pets a brown - banded (or
“tiger”) snake and a black
one. The “ doctor” read his
paper and expatiated at length
on the efficacy of his antidote
(which, by the way, was simply
the old treatment of ligature,
incision, sucking, and internal
and external use of liquid
ammonia). He summed-up:
“ Gentlemen, I have so much
faith in my treatment that if
the most venomous snake
known could be now produced
before all here assembled, I
should have no hesitation in
allowing it to bite me.” Where-
upon Krefft rose and said:
“Doctor, I have in this bag a
brown-banded snake, and in
this other a black snake —both
in good biting-order, and I shall
be pleased to apply to your finger
whichever you may select! ” With which
he advanced towards the lecturer, who promptly
retreated, calling excitedly upon those present to
protect him from the madman. The meeting
roared with delight and the great snake-authority
beat a hasty retreat per back door, and was never
again heard of in that capacity.
How rich Tyson’s conversation was in
purely Australian phrase and allusion. To an
estate-agent who was talking to him about the dis-
posal of his property after death, the millionaire
remarked : “ Leave it to the crows and let them
fight it out.” Explained that when the dogs had
brought down a kangaroo the carcase was usually
left to the crows. Evidently the old man had a
high opinion of his heirs.
Till called to duty at Goulbnrn the other
day in the matter of murderer Sleigh, N.S.W.
hangman Howard has had no rope-work since 3rd
April, 1899. When there is no work on in his
main line he does-up the court garden and cleans
the knives and forks used—ugh ! —by Bench and
Bar. The last man he butchered before Sleigh
was Stuart Briggs, the murderer of Maggie Dutt
and her grandmother at Annandale.
A graceful compliment to W. R. Boothby,
S.A. Sheriff and Chief Returning-Officer, would
be an invitation to control the first Commonwealth
elections. Boothby, who originated voting by
ballot, organised the first election in S.A. in the
fifties, and has been Chief R.-O. for that State
for 46 years. He was a fine athlete in his day, and
played for Surrey County at cricket before leaving
for S.A. in 1853. He is now 71 years of age, but
stiff clear-brained and active.
Chaffey Baker annually banquets S.A. Leg.
Councillors and other politicians, and this year’s
dinner was attended —for the first time for years—-
by C. C. Kingston. The bitter feud that so long
existed between them happily ended some time
ago. Similarly, Kingston and Symon, who quar-
relled over the Adelaide Hospital, have been hist
friends ever since C. C. K. so loyally backed up
the Federal delegates in England.

The kindly Bundey is the most humane and
popular Judge who ever sat on the S.A. Bench.
Insolvency Commissioner Russell has the strongest
claims to the succession, but it is freely rumored
that a prominent lawyer-politician may jump the
billet in the same manner as Way, when Attorney-
General in ’76, commandeered the C. J.-ship.

If Mr. Terry and one or two others already on the

A pleasing incident in the winding-up of
N.S.W. Parliament last week was the presenta-
tion of a watch and chain to Henry Clarke, mem-
ber for Bega, and father of the House. Premier
Lyne, making the presentation, healed the wounds
of a year ago, caused by Mr. Clarke’s disappoint-
ment at not having been included in the Minis-
try. Mr. Clarke is now verging on 80, and has
sat in the Assembly for about 35 years. He
intends to stick to the State Parliament and will
contest next general election. He and W.
MMillan are townies (Londonderry men), but
Clarke arrived in N.S.W. before M'Millan was
born. He was Postmaster-General in the Dibbs
Ministry of ’B9.

From a Maoriland paper :
Premier Seddon left Wellington on Wednesday after-
noon for Picton ; travelled to Blenheim by special train;
officiated at a banquet; left for Picton by special train ;
left for Wellington at 2 a.m. in the Tutanekai, reaching
port in time to catch the Manawatu train ; arrived at the
Palmerston Show at noon, and left for Wellington again
next morning.
293 miles odd under 24 hours, with a banquet and
half-a-dozen speeches thrown in, does not look as
if Seddon, P.C., were breaking-up. But how long
can it last ?
Printed recently in the Sydney dailies
that
The Premier yesterday received a cable message from
the High Commissioner for S’Africa stating that Col.
Maekay had been appointed staff officer for Australian
contingents at Capetown.
Now, be it noted that Mr. Maekay was sent to
S’Africa in command of a regiment of alleged
bushmen, which regiment is said to be still fight-
ing the Boers at “ the front ” while its commander
is at Capetown, 700 miles in the rear, in a snug
staff-billet. How on earth does this come about ?
No wonder Holder, Premier of S.A., is
gaunt. Though a physical wreck, he is always in
his place in the House, leaves no Bill wholly to a
colleague, and recuperates by conducting as many
as three church services on a Sunday. The last
fortnight of Parliament is generally crammed with
work ; in addition, Holder’s last fortnight was
full of anxiety, for a censure motion over his con-
cealed deficit was talked of. Yet he filled in the
Sunday between the two weeks by travelling to
and from Broken Hill (680 miles) and preaching
twice and delivering one address with the ther-
mometer at 105°.

“Jimmy Governor,” we read in the
Catholic Press, “ was once a contributor to The
Bulletin.” Well, we don’t remember it; but
perhaps the consecrated organ had the information
at first hand from Jimmy between drinks, when
the black gentleman called in to revise the proof
of that leading article. The Bulletin has had
all sorts of contributors. It once published an
alleged poem by a Chief Justice in the same issue
containing a real poem by a man who had just
come out of gaol after serving eight years to which
he had been sentenced by the Chief Justice afore*
said. The ex-prisoner’s poetry was about hunger,
and bread, and his children; and the Judge’s
verses—he was an old boy who, at over 80, wore a
ring on his accusing digit and twiddled an
exquisite brilliant on his little finger—concerned
a slender girl with violet eyes, and Blue Mountain
flowers.

Aboriginalities.
An old identity of the N.S.W. Far West
has just disappeared. There was a time when
there used to be water pretty often in the Darling
River and the Talyawalka Creek at Wilcannia;
and in those days, too, when there were no
bridges, the inlands fattened tens of thousands of
cattle for the Southern markets. To get a mob
over a flooded stream was beyond most drovers ;
for the brutes would let the stream turn their
heads, and then the chances were they would
begin circling—and that was Gehenna! So a
bushman, a splendid horseman, J. E. Burgess, set
to work to train a cow and a bullock for the work
of leading the mob across. He speyed a cow
which had a bull-calf at foot, and the latter was
encouraged to seek liquid comfort from his mother
whenever he was in trouble. First the cow, tied
to a boat, was got to the other side of the river ;
then the sucking bullock was placed at the head
of the moo and invited to get to the cow and the
liquid refreshment aforesaid. Later on the bul-
lock knew his work even when the cow was other-
wise engaged; yet for 15 years he remained a
sucking calf. Burgess, with the help of this one
bell-bullock, crossed over 50,000 head with hardly
a loss. There is a bridge now, though, and, even
if there wasn’t, there is only an occasional
river, so the owner decided to turn the animal
into pork sausages and prime veal before he died
of extreme old age.

“J.A.C.”: Re clay-eating in Northern
Queensland (B. 24/11/’00). About two years ago,
travelling from Brisbane to Townsville, there was
on board the steamer a boy who had become hope-
lessly addicted to the habit (which is only a
symptom of disease and not the disease itself).
His mother had taken him to Melbourne for
medical advice, but the doctors there knew abso-
lutely nothing of the disease. (Why she selected
Melbourne doesn’t appear.) There was no pos-
sible hope for the lad, who is probably since dead.
His face had a dull, pasty look, much like glazier s
putty. A N.Q. school-inspector, who was on
board, told me he had met with many similar
cases, both among aboriginals and Europeans,
but that, while the blacks restricted themselves,
with comparative impunity, to one particular
kind of clay, the whites would, when the craving
had got hold, eat even mud off the road.

In Nathalia (Vic.) district recently, sheep
and lambs have suffered severely from the sharp-
pointed grass and wild-oat seeds. These seeds,
about half-an-inch long, punctured the bodies of
the unfortunate animals like so many splinters.
The breasts and fore-legs were penetrated by hun-
dreds of seeds. In numbers of cases the eyes and
ears were pierced, scores of lambs being blinded
•in consequence. When the seeds entered the ears
they worked right into the brain in many instances.
Sheep and lambs which were slaughtered, upon
being skinned were found to have their carcases
punctured as though they had been tattooed. Ex-
perienced sheep-farmers say that they never
remember such an extraordinary thing occurring
in previons seasons.

“Jim”: Once saw two crows dealing
■with a lamb. One would peck it sharply behind,
and when it opened its mouth to bleat the other
would wrench at its tongue. Have often seen
lambs that had thus lost the tongue running
about.
Flying-foxes are numerous and destructive
on Clarence River this year. A destruction league
in Grafton recently offered Id. a claw, and paid
for 5000 the first fortnight. One man claimed to
have shot 250 with a Winchester rifle.

of forests, The dodge down River-
ina way is to take up a 40-acre-

thousands of posts and rails, logs
and poles, all (nominally) coining
from a patch of whipsticks. it
saves splitters’ and timber-getters

forest-rangers, t akes a long time
to clear 40 acres when there’s a saw-

and firewood wanted in the town.
“ Hiram Abiff” : Re the
bringing-down of rain by concus-
sion. Firing cannon is not the
trick. I explained my views to the
Murruinbidgee squatters, but they
would not run an experimental risk.
One gave £2OOO to the Presbel-
fastenan Ghurcli at Manly, which
ought to have included rain-fall,
but didn’t. My idea is to have
ready at different points (hill-tops
for choice) some Mongollier bal-
loons, which can be made for £1 to
£5 each, and let their whole freight
be a charge or two of dynamite or
melinite in the lightest possiblecase.
These must have fuses timed to end
at different heights, 1000 to 2000 ft.
Then they must be sent up on those
awful, exasperating, tantalising,
cloudy Riverina days when we see
the rain drifting away for want of
a shock. The whole experiment
could be made for £lOO, and I think
it is a fact that if one shower was
started the rest would follow. One
objection is that, if the rain came,
they’d say it would have come any-
way, and this prophet would be
without honor.
“ Mac.” : Here goes for a strong-woman
contest. At , N.S.W., in 189,5, there
was an innkeeper’s wife, an Englishwoman, whom
I saw take a 2001 b. sack of flour under each arm,
and another in her teeth (!), and carry them about
three chains and up four steps to finish with. This
woman could hold an average man down as if he
were a six-months-old infant. Also she could bite
pieces out of a glass tumbler and chew the mouth-
ful of glass into powder. Likewise she could get
under any ordinary saddle-horse and lift it and
rider clear up off the ground. I now claim the
strong-woman stakes, by virtue of a mere “walk-
over.”
Mr. F. A. Rochfort writes from Gira gold-
field, British Papua:—
Dear Bulletin. —Bishop Stone-Wigg’s recent sweeping
condemnation of the Papuan miners for “immorality”
embodies one of those mischievous half-truths that are
worse than a deliberate lie. I have been on the field for
over two years, and excepting two women whom I met on
the road near Tamata I have not seen a single female out-
side the Govt, stockade. I don’t hold the miners up as
an example of virtue ; they are neither better nor worse
than the usual run of men leading rough lives. But what
I do assert is that however “immoral,” in the Bishop’s
sense, they might desire to be, the absence of native
women would effectually bar that. A few miners may
from time to time have misconducted themselves accord-
ing to S.W.’s views, but to therefore condemn the whole
body of men is more in accordance with clericalism than
with Christianity. Also, from S.W.’s remarks, one would
suppose the Govt, officials were immaculate, but if he
wishes to improve matters let him be honest and begin at
the top 1 It is freely reported also that some of the
mission stations are not free from blame in this respect;
and however much we may be inclined to discredit the
statement, we cannot be blind to the fact that half-caste
children don’t grow on trees in the neighbourhood of
such establishments. — Fras. A. Rochfort. Gira Gold-
field, British N. Guinea.

A remarkable sheep was lately found in
the mountains near Horsham (Vic.) The animal
has four hind and two fore legs; all its legs are
serviceable. It has also a number of stomachs.
The other organs are well developed, and in some
instances duplicated. The sheep is in good health,
and carries a growth of about 4m. of wool*
A CRY OF WOE.
THE PEB .: “ Blime, if I on'y 'ad me best clothes
on!"
Not long ago three Afghans, en route from
Murchison to nor’-west, met an old prospector.
They thrashed him within an inch of his life, took
his two camels, and left him 75 miles from any-
where. He walked that distance to Peak Hill,
from which place Trooper M‘Donald was sent
after the Afghans, and after four days ran them
down (270 miles away), riding the same horse
throughout!

Go on the land, young man ! A Richmond
River (N.S.W.) youth travelled south to Bellinger
River, applied to the Land Board, and unwound
all the red-tape that he was told was necessary in
order that he might obey the above command.
The board, when considering the case, looked hard
for a flaw—and found it. “ One of the letters was
not registered ! ” Application dismissed.

NOT THERE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
When all Australia gathers for her festival of glee,
And the wide world rocks the cradle of the Common-
wealth to be,
When pride and youth and beauty look down from roof
and rail,
And all the sunny harbor is a gleam of snow-white sail 1
Before the cheering crowd goes by, before the rockets
flare,
Let us drink one toast in silence to “ The men who won’t
be there ! ”
They are sleeping on the sandhills with the brown grass
overhead ;
They blazed the dim tracks, living, and they mark the
milestones, dead !
They opened up the goldfields, and they broke the '
virgin soil,
And they took their share of danger and a
double share of toil—
By wind-swept graves and shallow where
their whitened bones lie bare,
The men who made the nation are the men
who won’t be there !
They are sleeping in the graveyards in their
silent graves apart,
With empty arms and eager that would hold
her to their heart,
Those statesmen of the buried years, those loyal men
long dead ;
Are they turning in their dreaming to the dull tramp
overhead?
When they pin the stars and garters, when they write
the titles rare,
The men who earned the honors are the men who won’t
be there 1

A Married Couple’s Trials.
Mr. R. Spinks, of Bulli, timber contractor to
the old Bulli Colliery Co., recently gave a
reporter an interesting resume of certain episodes
in his life. “ I have had my share of trials and
privations,” said he, “but ray experience of
fifteen yearn ago, when I was working in the
mine, was worse than any. I was then attacked
in the hip by the hot, shooting pains of sciatica.
I could not sleep at night and had to lay up.
This attack eased off, but my right leg was often
numb and it pained me intensely to stretch it. I
was a victim to indigestion, which so weakened
me that I was obliged to leave the colliery.
After meals I was quite weak from pain and
oppression on the chest. I was also afflicted with
sick headaches, biliousness, and heart palpitation.
Later on I became a timber contractor, and this
exposed work caused the sciatica to spread to my
wrist joint and right knee, and sometimes I was
unable to work for days. Four years ago I read
how beneficial Dr. Williams’ pink pills were in
such cases, and I began them. I soon improved,
the pain grew less, and I ate and slept better.
By continuing with them the sciatica, indigestion,
and heart palpitation all left me, and I can now
work as vigorously at felling trees and carting
timber as 1 could twenty years ago. Dr. Wil-
liams’ pink pills have also benefited my wife
greatly.”
Mrs. Spinks, when questioned, kindly said: —
“Eight years ago I became a victim to heart
palpitation, weakness, and dizziness. Sometimes
I swooned away. My heart gave two or three
sudden jumps and seemed to stop beating. I was
always weak and tired. My husband advised me
to try Dr. Williams’ pink pills. After using one
box I felt improved, and when I had taken three
more boxes the attacks ceased. Since then I have
gained in health and strength, and can undertake
household work which was previously too much
for me. My daughter has found these pills un-
equalled for curing neuralgia.”
Mr. and Mrs. Spinks’ experiences with Dr.
Williams’ pink pills prove how beneficial they are
to both men and women. By their action on the
blood and nerves they cure anaemia, consumption,
debility, sciatica, scrofula, erysipelas, chest com-
plaints, ladies’ ailments, loss of manly strength,
dysentery, paralysis, etc. Sold by chemists and
storekeepers, and by the Dr. Wiliiams’ Medicine
Co., Queen’s Place, Sydney, two and nine per
box, six boxes fifteen and three, post free. Send
description of your ailment and receive instruction
free.*

SCHWEPPE’S DERATED
SARSAPARILLA
The rifle-shooting enthusiasm turns up in
unexpected quarters. Abdul Wade, the Bourke
(Afghan) camel-carrying boss, becoming afflicted,
has erected butts outside Bourke, and practises
assiduously. Wade, too, by the way, maintains
in his private service a Mahommedan priest, who,
mounted on the most gaily-decked camel in the
country, visits the various camps.

capacity. Once when m3 7 kangaroo slut’s six
puppies were about a fortnight old, I went out
one morning and found that a huge carpet-snake
had swallowed three of them, the remaining three
being crushed flat as pancakes.

“ Ne’er-do-well ” on a phase of the rabbit
question :
What is “ Chas. F.” (8., l/9/’OO) giving us ? No rabbits
on a border-station in Queensland in the end of 'BB !
Why, rabbits were firmly established all along the Q.
border in ’BB, and long before the border-fence was com-
pleted. In ’B6 rabbits were seen near Mungindi and Worn-
pah. In Jan., ’B7, they were found 30 miles below Eulo
on the Paroo. In Sept, same year 17 were killed on
Yeranbah. In Jan., ’BB, they were killed on Bundaleer,
and in Feb. same year the Q. marsupial boards on the
border were authorised to expend funds in rabbit-destruc-
tion. During ’BB, rabbits were killed on stations right
along the border, east to Mungindi (I can give the num-
bers), and at the end of ’B7 so many rabbits were re-
ported in Q. that complaints were made that the border-
fence was not being pushed on fast enough. As to gaps
in long stretches —as the fence was completed, boundary-
riders (30-mile sections) and inspectors were put on and
the slightest break in the fence at once repaired. This
arrangement is still in force. “Chas. F.” says “Young
rabbits will squeeze through the mesh.” The border
fence netting is of 1 jin. mesh, and experiment has proved
that young rabbits cannot get through any mesh of less
than lfin. Also, rabbits rarely burrow under a fence
when the netting is properly sunk, though they may take
advantage of holes made by porcupines, bilbies, &c. In
short, the correctness of my contention (8., 2/6/’00) that a
properly-constructed and properly-looked-after fence is
effective, has been proved by the fact that the border-
fence has effectually prevented the rabbit-wave from
devastating Queensland as it did N.S.W.

Talk about the siege of Ladysmith ! The
backblox settlers in Whangamomoana, Northern
Maoriland, have never during four months been
three days’ food ahead. One camp lived for a
week on sow-thistles and butter, and another
party of five subsisted for four days on 12 biscuits.
Yet M.L. Govt, sent £5OOO for the sufferers by the
Ottawa fire. Once again—“ Go on the land ! ”

“M‘Cooey”: Be the prevalent notion
that we have in Australia only four or five kinds
of venomous snakes. Fact is, we have 68 venom-
ous kinds (six being aquatic). Of non-venomous
kinds we have 38—making 106 species in all. We
have 180 described lizards —none of them fanged,
though several species are legless and very snake-
like. All are carnivorous and insectivorous.
CHANGE THE MEDICINE, STEP-MAMMY.
“ The trade-unions of Victoria have lately made
creditable efforts to raise money for the sufferers by the
Indian famine. The people of Hindustan form a
terrible example of the effects of Freetrade. The Indian
peninsula should be the Utopia of the Freetrade Demo-
crat. A large portion of the economic rent of the
country passes into the State Treasury. British manu-
facturers have taken good care that native industries
should not be fostered. In consequence, it is said that
80 per cent, of the population of India are dependent
upon agriculture for their livelihood. In such a case
the slightest failure of the crops means destitution for
thousands, and a bad season signifies actual famine
for millions. Were Australia turned into a sheep-run,
a wheat-farm or a vineyard, her people, like the
Hindus, would be at the mercy of the elements. All
their eggs would be in the one basket of rural industry,
and when the bottom fell out of that they would be
absolutely ruined.”—Nov,.
“ Kihikatea ” : “ Fancy Free ”
(8., 22/9/’00) is misleading about Maori-
land saw-mills. The sawmill-hand’s curse
is bio’cen time. My working hours per
month for 12 months, from Oct., 99, to
Sept , ’OO :-12Bi, 1461, 941,1531,. 183
1531, 1091,170,174,152 f, 162,131. Highest
number of 8-liour days in any one month,
19 ; lowest, 7. And this mill is not the
worst of the dozen or so round about here.
You’re promised so much a day, but in
reality it is so much an hour, as you only
get paid for the time the saw spins.
Causes of broken time—breakages, wet
weather, wind, fire, rough timber, scarcity
of orders, football, drunk, and so on.
Risks are few enough, and the pay is
good enough, but the hours are too short.
Wages, 7s. to 125., an occasional fast and
skilful benchman getting up to 145., with
a premium for a certain tally. But
cheques are not payable till the 20th ot
the month following—3o days wages
being thus kept in hand.
“ Snell ” : Luck counts for a great
deal in mining, so much indeed that the
man who has no faith in his luck h&d.
better take to soldiering, or some other
comparatively safe means of earning a
livelihood. I once worked on the same
shift, and in the same drive, with two
facemen at Smeaton (Vie.), one of whom
had twice been under falls that should
have crushed every spark- of vitality out of
him. In the first, which happened at
Clunes, instead of being squeezed to a
jelly, lie was dug out afflicted with noth-
ing more serious than a broken leg. Hie
second accident came about in a Ballarat
East mine, when about half-an-aore of
mullock came away, killing one man and
burying the other in a comfortable little
den formed between a sloping cap-piece
and the side of the drive, from which he
was extracted without having suffered a
scratch. The second of my two mates at
Smeaton was killed a few months back
by the fall of a piece of reef m a stope.
That bit of reef weighed 19ozs. 1

C.M.P. : The sugar used in beer-making is turned into
glucose by means of an acid, generally sulphuric acid.
Now, nearly all sulphur contains arsenic, and there is
precious little of the sulphuric acid of commerce in which
you cannot detect at least traces of that mineral poison,
which, like all other mineral poisons, is strongly accumu-
lative in its effects. (2) Peripheral neuritis is inflamma-
tion of the nerves of the extremities. It is an exquisitely-
atronising malady, and is the trouble from which, it will
be remembered, Mrs. Geo. Dean suffered after she had
been loaded up with rat-poison by her loving husband....

POLITICAL
POINTS
Some humorist suggests “ Lyneton ” as a
name for the Federal city. Why not ‘ ‘ Dry Dog ? ”
The senatorial candidature of Mr. C. J.
Royle, of Sydney, is smiled upon by Calico Jimmy
and Co., and it is whispered that every drummer
on the road is to act as an electioneering agent of
his.
“Mr. Charles, M.L.C., speaking against
old-age pensions, says that when a man is helpless
he should be put in an asylum. It is, at least,
more sensible than putting him in the Upper
House.”— -Australian Star.
In the Federal pageant, everything is to
give way to the soldiery. The birth of the
Commonwealth is to be marked by a mere military
S’African triumphal procession, which might weil
stand for the Funeral of Liberty.
Queensland Works Royal Commission
report (scotched through the death of the Under-
secretary) fizzled out most miserably. It was
cheap, as commissions go, only costing the tax-
payer some £6OOO. It goes without saying that
nothing will be done.
Evangelist Yarley, lecturing in Sydney,
extols Britain for freeing the S’African blacks
from Boer thrall, but “admits it to be humiliating
that the British nation seems incompetent to raise
up the inferior races,” Foul slander! The
Manglo-Saxon race always “raises up” the in-
ferior races ! Raises ’em to Heaven ! Where, for
instance, are our poor nigs ?
R. T. Yale, of Ballarat, assails the doors
of the Federal Senate, backed by the Orange vote.
Yale is a dreary, weary, wooden bore, who should
be voted down by the Orangemen in their own
interests. The Protestants of Australia can suffi-
ciently repress Papal aggression without enlisting
the alleged aid of a bigot so uninteresting that
his talk would bring wrinkles to the brow of a
brass monkey.
The R.C. common-schools in Victoria are
a failure. This is palpable through the recent
speeches of Archbishop Carr. Archbishop Carr
and the priesthood have almost given up threaten-
ing parents who send their children to the State-
schools. A leading Melbourne Catholic lately
gave two flags to his local State-schools. The
Catholic religious book-shops, in Melb. suburbs,
all sell State-school books.
The Argus, in backing one Hamilton, a
Gippsland squatter, to beat M‘Culloch, Turner’s
Upper House representative, rested the former’s
claim on the fact that he offered to raise and
equip a corps of bushmen for the Transvaal. The
offer was not accepted at ’Ome, and Yic. Govt,
paid for the cables, so that squatter Hamilton’s
patriotism didn’t cost him much. But the Argus
is the sort of paper that would ask no higher
qualification in a legislator than that he had once
carried chops to some Royalty’s lap-dog.
OUR FOREIGN NATIONAL AUSTRALIAN IMPORTED DRUNK.
“ To the detriment of local industry, but for the glory of the Commonwealth.”
What promised to be the most interesting
liquor experiment of the age has apparently been
abandoned. It was reported that the Maoriland
Government was going to try National instead of
Local Option, so that if the popular vote decided
against drink, all Maoriland would become tee-
total at the same time. Seeing that there is a
uniform tariff, a uniform marriage law, divorce
law, criminal code, and all the rest, it seems only
fair and logical to have a uniform drunk or a uni-
form teetotalism. But the Seddon Government
weakened on it, and is inclined to divide the
country into nine local-option districts. There
isn’t even to be a uniform closing hour. The
nominal hour for shutting off the beer is to
be 10.30, but the local licensing committees are to
have power of extension.
SOME AUSTRALIAN POLITICAL DEFINITIONS.
[For The Bulletin.]
M.P. : One who earnestly strives to benefit humanity
—as represented by himself !
Premier: An editor’s errand boy.
Editor : The servant of a capitalist.
Capitalist: God’s own chosen.
Lobby: The prompter’s box of the Parliamentary
Theatre.
K.C.M.G. : The shed candle that lures the leading
sheep. The rest of the mob follow, and much driving is
thereby saved.
Cecil P.
The Queensland Govt., in order to make
the granting of scholarship concessions to R.C.
colleges look more plausible, has conferred a simi-
lar privilege to certain Brisbane private academies.
These private schools are at present competing
with the Brisbane Girls’ Grammar School, and
have almost reduced that institution to a state of
financial desolation. Wherefore the Philpocracy,
in order to complete the aforesaid desolation,
ukases that State scholarships may be enjoyed in
the very institutions that are ruining (with the aid
of a body of crazy trustees) the State-supported
and State-conducted academy.
Mr. Pearse, of the Australian Pastoral-
ists’ Review, describes a recent visit to a country
where they have a Government paper currency :
In the Argentine Republic you get lldol. 50 cents for a
sovereign. Across the river you get sdol. for a sovereign,
and one dollar goes as far as the other. That is one
reason why the Argentine is going ahead so. They are
selling everything for gold, while they pay everything in
paper. The result is that the people are so wealthy that
they don’t know what to do with their money. Their
opera-house, jockey club, and so on are dreams to us in
lavish expenditure. It has always seemed to me that
people who sell for gold and pay in paper have a big pull
over us.
N.S.W. ex-Treasurer M‘Millan, inaletter
to Sydney Telegraph dated 27/11/’OO :
While we recognise the necessity of a large volume of
income from the Customs, we must steadily set our faces
against any differential system which practically means
protection.
The last four words embody an immortal truth. The
proposal to give Britain the benefit of a differen-
tial tariff in Australia is giving protection to the
British manufacturer in Australia which he doesn’t
get in Britain, and giving protection to a man
who affects to despise such a thing, and The Bul-
letin is pleased to see that the M‘Billion recog-
nises to the full the absurdity of the idea.
The Lyne Government’s eminently sensi-
ble proposal to establish State-owned electric-
lighting and power works for Sydney has evoked
a protest from the City Council, which holds that
the State is interfering with what should be a
purely municipal work. So it is. But the only-
cheap and effective electric system is one which
covers all Sydney. The Government can supply
a system for all Sydney. If the same work was
done municipally there would have to be about 43
systems, with as many separate managements,
engineers, sources of supply, loans, &c., and all
this subdivision and foolery would make it a hope-
less loss. The system which divides a second-rate
city into over 40 local governments blocks all
municipal progress.
Judge Docker (N.S.W.) opposes old-age
pensions—is afraid that the system will
“not conduce to the growth of Thrift or
cause individuals to recognise their personal
responsibilities.” Docker is a man with a prospec-
tive old-age pension himself, so, if he really feels
like that, he had better give up the evil thing.
Any Government will gladly pass, at his request,
a short Act setting forth that the usual Judge’s
old-age pension shall not be paid to Mr. Docker,
lest it destroy his self-reliance. When a man
who has got his own old-age pension (not 10s. a
week, mind you, but £750 a year) provided, and
who hangs on to it like grim death, objects to his
fellow-man getting an allowance of a microscopic
fraction of the amount, it looks mean. And, when
he says he does it for his fellow-man’s good, it
looks worse than mean—it looks hypocritical.
The Agricultural Leases Bill of Bennett,
M.L.A., enthusiastically rejected by N.S.W.
Assembly, was chiefly intended to give fixity of
tenure and some kind of a fair rent to the tenants
on the absentee-owned estates in N.S.W. The
N.S.W. absentee who lives in England acts, in
many eases, just like the Irish absentee who lives
in the same place. His rents are exorbitant, he
grants no leases, reserves the right to eject ’his
tenants at short notice, and gives no compensa-
tion for improvements. Bennett should try again
in another direction. A graduated land-tax
ranging as high as 3d. in the £ on the very largest
estates, with 50 Or 100 per cent, increase on absen-
tees, would break ud the bis estates among small
settlers, and end the absentee difficulty and the
big estate difficulty together.
RETALIATION.
WIFE: “There's my fool of a husband flirting
with old Potter's wife again! Won't Potter
just be mad when I tell him about it!”
The Turner Govt, has introduced a Bill
providing for the establishment of penny postage
throughout Victoria after next April, if it passes
the most probable result is a universal Australian
penny postage under Federation, and as that
must needs involve some loss it is all the more
reason why there should be a universal half-penny
newspaper postage to make it good. Australia
can’t afford to hump bulky dailies and weeklies to
far ends nf this continent for nnthinc • in some
tar ends ot this continent tor nothing, m some
remote regions it costs about bd. to deliver a
paper. Apropos, a deputation of three sturdy
daily-paper beggars, all Freetraders, have already
been biting the ear of the Postal Conference, on
the subject of getting themselves protected and
subsidised and pauperised by the Commonwealth,
which, they demand, shall carry their wares at
public expense, on the ground that their bogus
fiscal statistics and their unsavoury divorce
reports are a valuable aid to education.
The action of the recent Conference of
Manufacturers in formulating a proposed tariff
for Australia, which tariff it has decided not to
divulge at present, seems to have created a most
unnecessary amount of frenzy. The manufac-
turers will, presumably, present their draft tariff
to the Commonwealth Parliament, which will
accept it or not as it pleases. The little knot of
Freetraders who follow George Reid will present
their tariff, which is also a secret at present, in like
manner. The Bulletin will present its own
tariff if it feels like it. So will almost everybody
who has a tariff, and many people who think they
have but haven’t. If the Freetrade party had
published the details of its own intended tariff
and shown exactly how it proposed to raise the
necessary coin (by an income-tax of 2s. 6d. in the
£ or otherwise) then it might complain with more
reason.
Launceston (Tas.) Examiner, a strongly
Freetrade organ
The Tasmanian Freetraders do not aim at carrying
their policy to its logical conclusion all at once.
None of them do, though Heaven only knows
why. If Freetrade is such a good thing, why not
have it ? If customs duties raise prices to the
extent of twice or thrice the amount of the duty,
why make the people pay two or three millions,
as the Freetraders allege they do, under a
revenue tariff, in order that the. Treasury
can get one million, when by making them pay
one million in direct taxation the Treasury
could get the same amount? That Free-
trader’s cry, “Freetrade is a good and beautiful
thing, and the short road to happiness and pro-
sperity, and you needn’t be in the least afraid to
elect us, for we won’t give it to you,” is calculated
to tire any logical community.

George Reid commenced it, and his Freetrade earn-
P v!?v. la v ‘°. t ® rla : an jf S-A. has resulted in a challenge
which the Victorian Protectionists are certain to accept,
This is much to be regretted. There should he no parties
in the initial House of Representatives if we are to have
the administrative machine made perfect. The first Aus-
tralian Ministry should contain nine or ten of the best
administrators in all Australia, chosen without consider-
ing their political views in the least.
Almost the first thing the Federal Parlia-
ment has to do is to fix up a Protective
Tariff, or Revenue Tariff, or else decide to
have Freetrade and no tariff at all. And
at least a dozen papers are imploring the pub-
lie not to have any Protectionist or Free-
trade or Revenue-Tariff party in the Parliament,
but just to draw its fiscal policy out of a hat by
accident. In fact the public is urged to have no
party that is for or against anything, but just to
draw everything out of a hat by accident.
Complaints about the draft-
ing of Bills presented to N.S.W.
Parliament are in many respects-
well grounded. In some Bills
the very English is bad. But it
does not follow that the blame
goes to the Parliamentary drafts-
man. That official is often passed
over. Even Ministers of the
Crown do one or other of the
unemployed juniors at the bar a
good turn by feeing him with
£lO or £2O (in one case lately £5O)
for drafting an occasional Bill.
That is a sort of patronage Par-
liament is justified in inquiring
into. A badly - drafted Bill
causes the loss of whole sittings
and often prevents legislation on
the matter concerned for the ses-
sion. If drafting outside the
Govt. Department be continued
Government Bills at least should
be made pass through the De-
partment. By the way, N.S.W.
Parliamentary draftsman gets
£B5O, whereas ditto
draws £l3OO.
There is so much provin-
cial jealousy among the various
parts of Maoriland itself that it
is small wonder so many Maori-
landers take a provincial view of
Federation. The North Island
is frightfully jealous of the South
Island, while in the North Island
itself Auckland grinds her teeth
at the attention shown to Wel-
lington, and in the South Otago’s
standing grievance is that West-
land gets too much perishable
loot out of the Treasury.

a question arose whether Tadbury wasn’t the-

The Kail of Wasted Things.
At the Castle of Men’s Sorrows, in the Hall of Wasted
Things,
Are broken old betrothals, and old betrothal rings,
And long-forgotten kisses, and old letters never sent,
And heartstrings of young lovers that faithless ones have
rent,
And long-since-burnt-out passions, and the fires of wasted
T - loves, _ ' ,
And cast-off maidens’ ringlets, and pairs of maidens
gloves,
And smiles that men have treasured and sweet glances
gone astray,
And broken words of lovers, and hours of many a day.
Now with these I’d fain deposit some few things of my
own—
Some paltry, wasted trifles that someone has outgrown ;
This tiny, battered locket, and this bit of gem-set go.d,
And the love I’ve left unspoken, and the love I may have
told ;
May they He and be forgotten, where the grey-robed
Angel sings—
The Angel of Oblivion, in the Hall of Wasted Things.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Quarter-mile swimming premiership of N.S.W.
at Sydney this (Saturday) afternoon looks like supplying
a hot tussle between boy Dick Cavill and Bob Craig!
Lane and Hellings (just home from the old country) are
unlikely starters, and may not be “fit ” at all this season.
Kumor mentions a “ dark ’un ” in the person of one Read
(who has never competed before) as likely to upset cal-
dilations, but writer won’t stand rumors—especially
swimming- rumors. Reid was a pro. until a couple of
weeks since, when the A.S.A. dropped the lily-white
mantle of amateurism over him—which recalls that both
the swimming and athletic governing bodies of the
Mother-State have done a heap of off-hand whitewashing
t Probabl y it’s their idea of celebrating the advent
of the Commonwealth.
* • * *
World’s furlong premier swimmer Freddy Lane
will be seen “on the job” this afternoon at E. Sydney

W. B. Coates (plunging premier of Australia)
essayed, at Coogee (Sydney), Saturday, to eclipse the
record (61 ft. 6£in.) —his own—for a plunge from a “ take-
-H- with a time limit of 60sec. He covered
hut exceeded the minute by ssec. It’s odds
on that, had he been stopped when the 60sec. elapsed,
his object would have been achieved. The performance
won t count, though ’fcwas manifest that the judges were
to blame. Pierre Stapleton, of Bathurst (N.S.W.), some
days before, was credited with having done better than
Coates’ record by putting up 67ft. 2in. in 54see. This in
fresh water, too. As there were no Swimming Associa-
tion officials present the performance will not be allowed,
but Stapleton intends having another cut at it on Boxing
Day—the occasion of Bathurst swimming gala—when all
necessary arrangements will be made by the swimming
governing body. Writer feels convinced that Stapleton
will repeat the feat and, perhaps, do a bit better. He is
built for the game—over 6ft. high and weighing in the
neighbourhood of 17 or 18st.

First leg of Sydney Amateur Sailing Club’s
supremacy was decided Saturday under “ mixed ” con-
ditions—a stiff nor-easter followed by a strong southerly.
Only three—Gloria, Mr. A. E. Cutler’s Bunyip and Dr.
Elliott’s Meteor—finished, and in that order. Winner
was well nursed by Fred Doran. Mercia turned “ turtle ”
off Shark Island and Yankee carried her rudder away at
the lightship.
* * * »
Sydney P.J.Y.C. “outside” race didn’t come
off on Saturday. Course was to have been five miles to
windward of South Reef. ’Twas found impossible to get
a steamer to follow, and arrangements were made with a
fishing party to moor the rounding flag, but subsequently
no one seemed to know whether it was placed due north,
north-east, or east. Isea and Bona opened North Head
and gave it up : none of the others got even that far. To
writer’s view, Saturday’s occurrences demonstrate that
our 30-footers were never designed for “ outside” work-
smooth water is more in their line. A mild north-easter
early, hardened considerably later, and then came a stiff
southerly just as the last two yachts got in—which was
very fortunate for them.

The extent to which Victorian property has
deteriorated was evidenced by the recent sale of the late
W. R. Wilson’s estate, St. Albans. It went for £14,000
and will probably be cut up into allotments. Had that
alleged “moral” (Kinglike) won Caulfield Cup, his
owner would have been the possessor of St. Albans now,
and it would have remained a first-class breeding estab-
lishment, with Bill of Portland still bringing in £lOOO a
year as interest on that IOOOgs. he cost. W.R. bought
St. Albans from Mr. J. Crozier for £75,000, and subse-
quently sold it per medium of a “ Tatt’s.” sweep, after-
wards re-buying at £23,000.
* * * *
As showing what a profitable venture the St.
Albans estate might have been for a syndicate, ’tis said
that the late W. R. Wilson, in stakes alone, cleared
£38,209 in five seasons, while fees from mares during the
same term topped £4OOO. Then there were the many
thousands realised by stock-sales.
* * * *
The evidence of Kensington Racing Club
Secretary O’Mara before a Parliamentary select com-
mittee t’other day, that alleged Sydney Turf Club is no
club at all, surprised nobody. Most people suspected it.
All the public could ever find out about this mysterious
thing was that Mr. T. S. Clibborn filled the office of secre-
tary and meetings were held at Randwick four days in
each year—two of them being Anniversary Day and
Queen’s Birthday. What connection there is ’tween the
S.T.C. and A.J.C., and what arrangements the former
makes with the latter for the use of the leading N.S.W.
course will probably always remain a close secret, for no
one ever vouchsafes information on the subject. If, as
has been hinted, the S.T.O. is run by a syndicate of pro-
minent A.J.O. people, then why it is more favored in the
way of dates than most other clubs would be interesting
information. ’Twas this sort of thing that caused trouble
in Q. recently, and brought the objection that in the
allotment of dates ordinary clubs should not have to con-
tend against a syndicate composed of men sitting on the
committee of the body which apportions those favors.
Goldsmith (winner of Q. Cup on Monday) is a
gelding by The Jeweller. Mr. P. Talty gave 300 gs. for
him in Sydney eight months ago. Last year’s winner
(Amor) humped 9st. 21b. Goldsmith had Bst. 31b up.
Divy paid was £5. Owner’s share of the stake (500 sovs.)
is £350.

“As the best of good and the worst of bad,”
writes Thb Bulletin's London correspondent, “ reaches
Australia with the scar of the English trade-mark still
r a T.,. u P°? * presume ‘doping’ is just about to
fertilise at Randwick and Flemington. As this artistic
turf accompaniment—Yankee of course—has just
excoriated the track here, disturbing the Prince, Lord
Durham, and Rothschild, this is what you may expect.
Doping is simply pumping or capsuling arsenic,
strychnine, or sulphuric ether into a ragged outsider
before he goes to the post so that at the ‘ go ’ he goeth
like greased lightning. Formerly the hypodermic
syringe was used for doping, but as the slight puncture
on the horse was traced by keen vets, the drug or tonic is
now given in a capsule. The American turf warns off
dopers’ just as it bars the electrical spur or whip.
Doping does not improve the breed of the horse —the cant
excuse for racing and absolutely ‘ footle-dozes ’ the
handicapper."
“ Rothschild says (continues our correspondent)
that at present there is no positive proof that there are
dopers here, but the “ extraordinary improvement made
by horses trained by Americans has led to the supposition
that some unknown and mysterious treatment must have
been adopted to effect this rapid change in the form of
certain horses.” At Gatwick I heard one of the talent
say, concerning a fairly well-supported “ no-possible-
hope, when the start was delayed, “It’ll all have
fizzled out if they don’t hurry off.” Meaning the drug
would have evanesced. So it did, and the doped nag ran
to his true form—an emphatic last.

* * * *
Tasmania has a coming Samson in young Stag-
pole, who lately coached himself up to attack Sali’s record
of elevating a 1041 b. bell from shoulder to arm’s-length
above 15 times. Sali accomplished this in Victoria 13
years ago, but Stagpole failed in the 14th hoist. All
things considered, especially the youngster’s age (16) the
performance was a great one. As ’twas Stagpole’s first
public show, nervousness probably affected him ; he’ll do
better next time—if he doesn’t burst a heart-valve. Stag-
pole pere is an instructor in gymnastics, and trains his
boy on the Sandow system.

Yankee files tell of marvellous performances by
U.S. flier, M. W. Long. At New York A.O. Games (Sept.
29) he won the J-mile supremacy on a circular track from
100 others, in 47 4-sths—a small fraction under world’s
record. Five days subsequently, at New Jersey, Long
improved on this and set up new world’s figures (36 2-sth
42 l-sth and 47sec. respectively) for 350yds., 400yds. and
440yds. When ’tis considered that the 440yds. feat repre-
sents little more than i 0.1 sec. for each 100, the extraor-
dinary nature of the achievement will be better under-
stood. The late Lou. Myers covered 300yds. in 36 4-sth
sec. 19 years ago, and this stood intact (though assailed
often) till Long’s great run. Previous best for 400 was
Downes’ ,43sec., accomplished in ’9O ; and for the quarter
straight Wendell Baker’s 47|sec., at Boston in ’B6

Clever Australian bantam top-nofccher Bert
Weekes—who on Saturday night at Golden Gate A.O.
(Sydney met Billy M‘Kell—paid the penalty of gofng out
of his class. M‘Kell was stronger, could punch harder,
and carried close on a stone more brawn—judging bv ap-
pearances, though he only confessed to 51bs. The battle was
hot and fast. Billy made the pace throughout, but Weekes
evaded well, and frequently gave as good as he got, till
a heavy bash on the point in the ninth round made him
easy game ; he went clean out in the tenth 3min. Excite-
ment ran high from end to end, and the place was so
packed that many spectators sat on the floor immediately
under the ropes. Just after that jolt in the ninth round,
a general metee looked imminent for a while. Jack M'Kell
(Billy’s brother) intruded, and was, ’tis said, struck by Jim
Barron, one of Billy’s seconds. Then ex-novice Jim
Farrell moved as if to give M'Kell some advice, but the
latter evidently misunderstood the intention and shot out
the right, flooring Farrell. Weekes showed marvellous
grit and took a heap of stoush without wincing. Two or
three times he had M‘Kell sick but weight and power beat
him. Match was billed as for bantam supremacy, but
winner owned up to 41bs. over that class limit, hence the
title remains with the vanquished one.
* * * *
The recent very one-sided glove battle at Gaiety
A.O. (Sydney), between the miner-bruiser Dan O’Connell
and one-time acrobat Snowy Sturgeon, provided the un-
usual spectacle of the skilful man (Sturgeon) fighting foul
right through, and the rough ’un behaving with scru-
pulous fairness in the tightest situations. O’Connell
should have got the money in the sixth round, when
Sturgeon threw him savagely and heavily. The thing
was so plain that the timekeeper ceased counting, but
referee Seale said “fight on.” O’Connell got an awful
battering, and stood it till the knock-out punch settled
him completely. Sturgeon went to bed without a mark.
Tis to be hoped O’Connell will turn the game up now,
and follow some more legitimate occupation. The mines
or anything else would surely be better than playing the
part of a chopping-block, which seems to be Dan’s posi-
tion, for he can get none but the best to take him on.
Third and fourth raters are not having any of the sturdy
but slow-moving Irishman.

Heavy-weight pug-mill at Melb. Democratic
Club this Saturday night between Sydney Tim Nolan and
Melb, Tut Ryan looks like proving desperate. Both
favor close quarters, hence they’ll be at it from the jump.
Tim says, “ I’ll bate 'im, shure ; lave it to me;” but
writer entertains a different view. Tut is an older
general and a lot cleverer than Nolan, whose methods are
crude in that he depends mostly on his capacity for
taking punishment and his ability to land effectively
when a good opening offers. A fight between Nolan and
Peter Fehx is on Sydney Gaiety A.O.’s list for nextmonth.
lhe brawny Milesian has his hands full. Should he get
past Ryan his chance with the lathy black (if the latter
fights at all) would not be bad.

“ Old Timer ” : Re Bulletin’s recent suggestion
that Australian bruiser Billy M'Carthy might have been
among the thousands of victims of the Galveston (Texas)
calamity. News comes now that, though they lived in a
street fronting the wharves, he, his wife, and four young-
sters were all saved. Writer remembers M‘Garthy work-
ing" for Peter Jackson at the old U.S. Hotel, Kent-street,
Sydney, so as to learn the game, and also recollects him
being matched with Peter’s pet pupil, Bill Smith (now in
America), when the clever black thought it a “moral”
for the last-named, yet Mac. won in 11 rounds. M‘Carthy
was the gamest pug. Sydney ever produced—he’d tackle
anything. He had mills with Paddy Slavin, “ Buff ”
Costello, and Mick Dooley in their best days, was the first
to take Fitzsimmons on in America, and is the only man
who can say he knocked out old “ Ironbark ” Jimmy
Burge—which he did per pivot (“ La Blanche ”) blow at
Broken Hill.

, P ictu re is presented (through challenges in
late U.S. papers) of a fighter, just 21bs. over the light-
weight limit (lOst.) and sft. lin. high, challenging for
money any one of the heavyweight top-notchers (Jeffries,
Sharkey, Ruhlin, Corbett, or Peter Maher) without re-
sponse. The defiant one is the “ Barbadoes Demon ” and
stunted giant—gorilla-like Joe Walcott. Reason of the
gage not being taken up is that Walcott owns abnormal
physical advantages in reach, strength and stamina, and
so would always have a winning chance until completely
knocked out. Then there’s the ridiculous look of a
heavy weight meeting a lightweight, and the always-possi-
ble likelihood of the former going under to the ourang-
outang and being crushed completely out of existence by
the load of ignominy such a happening would heap upon
his reputation.
