Another round of Sydney electorate cricket
competitions ended Saturday. Bilowell (of international
fame), for Central Cumberland against Glebe, was in fine
bowling fix. In the opening innings he did the “hat
trick before one run went down against him, and
finished with 6 wickets for 20 to his credit. The second
effort saw him secure another half-dozen wickets at a cost
of 23 runs. Twelve wickets for 43 will probably top
everything likely to be recorded this season. It took
only four balls to get his first three and eight the next
Veterans Bannerman and Sweetman came to the rescue
for N. Sydney against Paddington when the first-named
was m senous trouble, and played out time, with the
neifcher Bi<l e could claim any great advantage
N. Sydney now leads with 6 points, Paddington (6) comes
next, while Waverley, Sydney, Redfern and Central Cum-
berland (2 each) are bracketed together.
* * * *
This year’s Austral is being run on a new plan.
Of the 10 initial heats the winners go into the final
at once. The 10 “ seconds ” then run, the first three
getting into the final; then the 10 “ thirds,” the first two
also having a further chance ; thus leaving 15 in the
final. This is a scientific way of obviating the throwing-
out of two out of three men with good chances who may
happen to be drawn in the same heat—besides making
the final more open.

ing. Worse still, Middleton’s nearest man is Macgibbon
on 150, so, barring accidents, all cycling experience indi-
cates that from Middleton back the competitors might as
well retire. The race would seem to be between Ohamp-
ness on 210 and O’Callaghan and Stapleton on 220

In reference to the £2O offered by the Dunlop
Tyre Company to the person who sends in to the com-
pany the most suitable name for the second grade tyre
they intend placing on the market, to meet the existing
demand for a good wearing tyre at a low figure. The
company states that competitors can send in as many
names as they choose, the only stipulation being that the
names are to be sent in on separate slips of paper.
A pitiful thing is the compulsory sealings
down of two shafts at the Greta (N.S.W.) Col-
liery, on account of the fire raging below—
although it is known that five men—two of them
married and two others brothers —are entombed
below. Of course there is not considered to be
the remotest chance of the unfortunate fellows
being still alive.
Judged on the principle that a newspaper
gets the sort of letters it wants, Sydney Daily
-Telegraph is doing its “darnedest” to advocate
the claims of N.S.W. Premier Lyne to be called
on to form the first Federal Ministry. In one
letter it is argued that the appointment of (anti-
rederalist) Lyne is the only “constitutional”
course, and that Barton was only appointed
Federal delegate to England through the grace of
Lyne aforesaid. Another letter (from a pushing
attorney) says:—
In the honest and able work to uphold the rights and
prestige of N.S. Wales Premier Lyne has merited the con-
ndence of its people, and there is no hesitancy when I say
that were a poll taken of the electors to-morrow the Pre-
mier would be considered the fittest of all those sug-
gested as head of the Federal Ministry, both on Consti-
tutional grounds and those which show that by his merits
he deserves the honor.
Then we are to assume that it is “ the electors ”
(of N.S.W., not of Australia) who are to be con-
sidered re the appointment of the Federal Pre-
mier Were the selection left to the electors of
the Commonwealth there are at least a dozen men
who would come before the present N.S.W
Premier.
In the great Coningham-O’Haran divorce-
has been so violently exciting Sydney,
the (Protestant) petitioner—a one-time well-known
cricketer, formerly noted for his good bowling
and the queer pranks he used to play in the field
—sued for divorce from his 32-year-old R.C. wife,
Alice Stanford Coningham, and claimed £5OOO
damages from the co-respondent, Very Rev. Dean
Denis Francis O’Haran, a 40-year-old R.C. clergy-
man, who for years has been the secretary and
right-hand-man of Cardinal Moran. The alleged
adultery was averred to have been committed in
various semi-public apartments within the pre-
cincts of St. Mary’s Cathedral, Sydney, of which
co-respondent has long been administrator.
VV hen the case commenced petitioner seemed an
utter wreck ; the loud, nerve-destroying talk of
Jack Want crumpled him all up, and he shook
like a reed in the wind when that Q.C. glared at
i m ' r w , as not , under cross-examination very
long’ before he admitted that he had occupied the
Sal ? e i r( j 0 ™ as his wife ’ and P aid that lady’s board
and lodging, after her alleged misconduct was
known to him. The immediate result of this was
to leave him without a solicitor, and the bottom
seemed to have dropped out of his case. Then
deserted by his counsel, the nervous wreck of five
minutes before pulled himself together, and set
himself resolutely to battle with the redoubtable
lawyer on the other side. Petitioner called Mrs.
Oonmgham. Mrs. Coningham came, tall, wrapped
in the memory of something that had once been
very alluring, and appearing as if she had just come
through a long illness. She proved to be a
witness of phenomenal nerve and memory. She
looked round the court with large, dark eyes that
glittered with excitement, and told her “tale of
shame in a simple, straightforward - seemii g
manner. Jack Want long and searchingly cross-
examined her, but failed to disclose any serious
holes m her symmetrical story. She admitted
that she had borne an illegitimate baby at
Brisbane when she was 17 years old, but
declared that to be her only fall up to the lime
at which she alleged she succumbed to the impor-
tunities of the co-respondent. Mrs. Coningham’s
examination-in-chief was a curious sight, and
one the like of which has rarely been seen
m a court of justice—the nervous, broken-
down-looking husband questioning the faded,
hollow-eyed wife who swore so circumstantially to
her own downfall. She was practically her hus-
band s only witness. Dr. O’Haran gave an em-
phatic denial to all Mrs. Coningham’s allegations,
and with respect to several far-back days named
by her as dates of clandestine meetings between
called evidence tending to show that
at the times in question he was otherwise engaged
at places far removed from the cathedral. He
was cross-examined at great length by petitioner,
who disclosed considerable ability as an amateur
counsel and put a variety of much-resented ques-
tions apparently designed to elicit the admission
that a Roman Catholic could sin at random so
long as he went to confession and procured
absolution. The case, which abounded in details
calculated to absolutely enthrall cooks and their

All Advertisements
For the cure of kidney disease bring to mind
Warner’s Safe Cure, whether mentioned or not.
We have often read, “ Watch the kidneys,” and
these words naturally bring vividly before us the
only world-wide famous specific that has stood the
test for twenty-one years—Warner’s Safe Cure—
although the advertisement may refer to another
remedy. The public knows there is but one
remedy upon which to rely when attacked by
kidney disease, and all these advertisements of
others but call Warner’s Safe Cure to mind.
Mr. E. T. Jones, who is an agent for the Rover
bicycle, writing from Sydney on 14th August,
1900, says : “ 1 have much pleasure in testifying
to the excellent qualities of Warner’s Safe Cure.
I have, until quite recently, been a resident of
Western Australia, my occupation being that of a
bicycle agent, but, owing to my health giving
way completely, I was compelled to leave that
colony and take a sea trip. I came to Sydney,
but felt no better, and was instructed by a doctor
to give up cycling, which I reluctantly did, but
felt just as bad as ever. One day I was asked by
a friend if I ever took medicine for my liver and
kidneys. I said ‘ No,’ and did not think the liver
and kidneys could prostrate a man as I was
prostrated. Anyway, after a great amount of
gersuasion I took one bottle of Warner’s Safe
■ure; and it worked wonders. I then purchased
six bottles, the last of which lam now taking. I
can, and do, honestly recommend this wonderful
Warner’s Safe Cure to anybody suffering from
liver or kidney trouble as my case was considered
to be incurable; and lam thankful to say that I
am stronger and feel better than ever I did before
in my life, and am riding my bicycle with
marvellous energy.”*

At Poverty Point.
Your mummer is ever the prince of egoists. Tenor
Kenninghamcontributes to the Argus some reminiscences,
interesting enough, of Arthur Sullivan, who lately died.
“ He wrote the tenor part in ‘ Ivanhoe ’ for me, in which
I made my first appearance in opera,” says the flutey
Charles. He wrote it “ for me,” observe. If there had
been no Kenningham, there had been no tenor part in
Ivanhofe, and Maurice de Bracy might have been a basso,
or a baritone, or a fat contralto in ironmongery tights.
“S.”: D’Albert, Rickards’s latest importation from
’Ome, is a young man with a giggle, who works the
“patriotic” gag to death. His picture of the lemon-
colored-kid-gloved Bwitish club Johnnie, the tired officah
fellah, dontcherknow, who hangs about his club.doocedly
bored by the devilish difficulty of—haw—living, when a
fellah has to breathe with such demned regularity, but
who, in the face of the enemy, becomes a lion of valor
and a worker of horrid prodigies, spreading dead and
desolate Boers over kopjes and veldts, is intended to
arouse all our —haw —bettah feelings, and amaze us all
over again with the phenomenal resource of the British
character. But we have just been reading “Smiler”
Hales (and a few hundred private letters from the front)
on the same awful officer, and know him for the ape and
imbecile he so often is ; so Mr. D’Albert’s efforts in the
cause of Imperial expansion fall like a cwt. of dough on
to the floor of the floorless pit.
“ The Bohemian Girl” affords an admirable illustration
of the efficacy of prayer, which should commend it to the
clergy. When the Count hears his beloved child scream,
with the fatuity of fathers in opera he starts singing
frantically about the circumstance, and then invites his
friends and followers to flop whilst he warbles a long and
laborious baritone prayer. While he is busy with his
melodious supplication of Providence to look after the
blessed “ cheild,” Devilshoof sneaks through the praying
crowd, bearing the heiress off to his woody fastnesses.
That is about the only gleam of humor the opera displays,
but there is just a suspicion that it is not intended to be
taken hilariously. Which is a pity.
Arthur Sullivan was an effusively-loyal snob and per-
sonally most generous. Gilbert is a ferocious democrat
and careful of the bawbees. Sullivan knocked down
hundreds of thousands of pounds, the fruits of his in-
spired labor. Inspired—for such music as “The Lost
Chord ” can never die.
Freely stated in London that Oscar Wilde was the real
author of “ Mr. and Mrs. Daventry,” a very near-the-
knuckle piece first produced and fathered by Frank Harris,
of Saturday Review fame.

dent, “ goes southward shortly with a great cinemato-
graph show of a 1 Life on the Ocean Wave,’ picturing
the land-lubber youth’s first intro, to the Navee and his

Nellie Stewart undoubtedly owes most of her good
looks to her dad. Dick Stewart is one of the handsomest
old men in Melbourne. His white hair and regular classic
features would make him a good model for any artist.
S.S.—“Zero” (P. P., Dec. 1) talks of Geo. Rowley
coming out to Williamson as “ Mr. Hilda Spong.” There’s
a mistake hereabouts. Rowley married Australian Ethel
Haydon. Hilda has not yet bestowed her name upon a
man creature.
In a recent leader about Musgrove’s Opera Co., now
performing in Sydney, S.M. Herald harps absurdly on
the phrase “high opera”—a perfectly idiotic variation
upon the customary designation of “ grand opera.” In-
deed, considering the pitch adopted, “ Muzzy ” is giving
us, if anything, “ low ” opera.
Nance O’Neil has favored Scotch ears by shading away
the “i” in “ Glamis,” but M'Kee Rankin and Jimmy
Atholwood “split the ears of the groundling,” with a
loud “ iss ” for second syllable.
Antoinette Trebelli (now Mdlle. Dolores) starts her next
Australian tour in Sydney about the middle of April.
When she last wrote to manager Tait, the bewitching
songstress was holiday-making in her native France.
Nellie Stewart first appeared on the stage at Melb.
Royal as a boy in the comedy of “ Saratoga.”
Musgrove’s revival of “ Bohemian Girl,” with a dis-
tinctly feeble Devilshoof in Gilbert King, set the|old-
timers thinking of Farley, who seems to hold the Aus-
tralian record for a fine basso rendering of the character.
Devilshoof degenerates from picturesque melodrama to
puerile low comedy, and only a tall man of good voice
and striking personality can make the part go for much.
The Carl Rosa co. in England has never had another
basso to equal Snazelle as Devilshoof. Snazzy, 25 years
ago, played the fiery young gipsy to the life, and was
perhaps a little too imposing. He bossed the show.
Bland Holt has put some paint on the frontage of
Melb Royal, and added three electric lamps to the out-
side illuminations. And the auditorium, it is said, is to
be made trim and flealess.
+,
What an amazing thing is the assurance of
Dry Dog Reid. Last Monday night the man who
kept heavy protective duties on a multitude of the
largest N.S.W. industries (on sugar, tobacco, wine,
beer, biscuits, confectionery, jams, jellies, preserved
fruits, newspapers, &c.) got up on his two ridicul-
ous brief legs at Sydney Town Hall and told the
people all over again how he had had the
courage to introduce Freetrade in N.S.W. and
how N.S.W. had prospered under it. Reid never
had any courage worth mentioning. He is the
Man who Ran Away from his convictions as soon
as he was put to the test. He is the boss back-
down man of cornstalk politics. It is a case of the
person who bolted for his life at the beginning
of the battle coming back and presenting himself
with the Victoria Cross when the shindy is over,
and the motto on the cross is : “ Presented to Me
by Myself for the Valour which I said I displayed. ”
The Bulletin is still waiting to find the Aus-
tralian Freetrade statesman who believes suffi-
ciently in his own doctrine to try it. When it
meets that man it will be so badly astonished that
it may even turn Freetrader itself.

A big sensation was recently created in
England by the court-martialling of Artillery
Lieut. A’Beckett, on the extraordinary charge of
having stolen ss. (two marked half-crowns) of
canteen money, while his accuser, a master-
gunner, was looking at him from about a yard
away. Accused, a son of Punch A’Beckett, was
honorably acquitted ; but the matter won’t end
there, as the accuser has demanded a court-martial
in turn.
BACK FROM THE WARS AGAIN!
Back from the wars they come,
With trumpet-sound and drum,
Across the rolling main.
Australia’s heroes true,
Australia welcomes you
Back from the wars again.
Back from the wars at last 1
Safe from the cannons’ blast,
Safe from the leaden rain.
For these their country’s praise ;
For these the victors’ bays,—
Back from the wars again.
But some may fight no more,
For they are wounded sore
Or racked by fever’s pain.
For these their country’s care ;
Honor the scars they bear
Back from the wars again 1
And hearts are aching here,
And falls the widow’s tears,
Who waits and weeps in vain ;
For in a distant grave
Sleeps many a soldier brave
Who comes not back again.
Sleep well! their country’s pride,
Who for the Empire died
On Afric hill and plain.
Australians, honor those
Who fell with face to foes,
And come not back again.
But welcome those who come
With trumpet-sound and drum
Across the rolling main,
Australia’s patriot sons,
Safe from the Boer guns,
Back from the wars again !
E. BROUGHAM DOCKER.


In Westralia the ticket-of-leave system is
»till in full bloom. This is something like the life
•f the released prisoner in W.A. :
Every ticket-of-leave holder is obliged to exhibit his
ticket to ‘ any person ” who may require it. The comp-
troller can assign a ticket-of-leave man to a certain dis-
trict, and he cannot leave it without permission. The
ticket-of-leave man when travelling is required to report
himself at every police station by the way; he is not
allowed to be out after 10 o’clock at night; he cannot
obtain employment without supplying to his employer
his register-number as a prisoner, and the resident
magistrate is_ to confirm the engagement and its terms.
Further, he is not to leave or change his employment
without the consent of the R.M. ; and he is not allowed
to work on his own account without permission. An
engagement for employment cannot be terminated by the
ticket-of-leave man, but the employer is allowed to termi-
nate the engagement, and “ return the ticket-of-leave
holder to the service of Govt., by forwarding him to the
nearest depot or police station.”"
These are 10 of the regulations, and there are 50
more of the same kind. Yet the old Westralian
objects to being called a “groper.”
Concerning a queer street-episode :
Dear Bulletin.— Waiting for a friend the other day in
Bundle-street, Adelaide, outside a jeweller’s shop, I saw
a ten-seconds’ tragedy. It was the most compressed
tragedy in my experience. A young and extremely
pretty woman entered, and presently I saw the shop-
assistant lift from the window a tray of scarf-pins. When
he replaced it I saw that the handsomest had been taken,
and presently the fair purchaser came out looking in-
tensely happy. “ A newly-made bride !” I reflected, but
all at once the girl’s face turned ashy white, and she
stared with horror and incredulity at a passing cab, in
which were seated a man and a woman—the latter hand-
some in a big, bold type. He was gazing into her face
with a look of fatuous admiration. When they had
passed the other drew herself up with a shiver, and —
seeming to become suddenly aware of the little parcel in
her hand—with a gesture of despair and misery threw it
into the gutter. As she turned away I was curious
enough to follow her, and was not surprised to see her
turn into a chemist’s shop. They sell things there be-
sides golden hair-wash—things that are good for the
young woman who has just seen her husband worship-
ping a bulky yellow-haired woman in a cab.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
One “ Lux,'’ writing to Sydney Tele-
graph, proposes that all hotel bars should be
without fronts —should be wide open to the street
like banana-barrows or coffee-stalls—so that the
public can see .people drinking. This publicity
would, it is thought, be a great safeguard against
drunkenness. But why not also compel clubs to
have no front to the premises, so that the Fat
Man can be seen drinking, as well as the Lean
Man and the Ordinary Man? And as this kind
of publicity might lead people to carry the liquor
home and absorb it secretly in their own
dwellings, it becomes obvious that the family
which proposes to drink a jug of beer
on its own premises should go out into
the street with the liquor in its hand,
and ring the dinner-bell hard to draw
attention, and then consume its unpreten-
tious refreshment in public. If publicity
will dimini-h drunkenness and other sins, by
all means let us have publicity. Some good
might even be done by a law whereby a man twice
convicted of wife-beating should have the front of
his house taken out, so that thereafter he must beat
his wife publicly or not at all. But after all it is
just possible that the bar front isn’t so much for
the sake of seclusion as to keep the dust out of the
beer, and if it was done away with the thirsty one
would drink behind a mosquito-curtain quite
unashamedly. The curtain wouldn’t hide the
view much, and it would keep the bricks and
brown paper out of the liquor when a southerly is
blowing, and probably that is all that the drinking
public wants.
Died recently, in Fulham workhouse,
London—of previous starvation, drink, and gene-
ral wretchedness the late Madame Sainton-
Dolby’s brother, George, once intimate friend and
factotum of Dickens—author of the breezy book,
“Charles Dickens as I Knew Him”—and fre-
quently referred to by the novelist in the most •
affectionate terms. It is said that there remains
a valid insurance of £3OOO on the unfortunate
man’s life, and people are asking how it was that,
while the premiums were kept duly paid, the
miserable insured was allowed to sink into such
abject poverty.

“ Gunner ” : Re N.S. W. military examina-
tions :
Before the current N.S.W. Military inquiry, General
French said—with regard to the fact that certain officers
had not passed exam, for the rank they hold—that he
thought “ there was a little margin left to the Comman-
dant.” Presumably a “ margin ” that allows the General
to favor certain persons. That is not discipline, however,
and every soldier, from the General to the youngest re-
cruit, is or should be equally subject to the discipline. In
the produced list of unqualified officers there are some
curious specimens of favoritism. A Major and Brevet-
Lieut. appointed in ’9l, held his rank 6 years and hasn’t
had time to qualify by exam. A Major appointed 27/4/’9B
—2years—not qualified. A Major appointed 1/1/’94, 6
years 10 months —not qualified. That’s a pretty good
margin. What is the exam, worth anyhow ? Has anyone
ever seen a copy of the papers set at former exams. ? Copies
of papers for ’Varsity, Public Instruction, Public Service,
and all other exams, are to be had, but the military keep
theirs secret. Why ? There are some queer stories about
the questions sometimes asked. One of those at the last
exam, (in Oct.) was —“ What are the duties of a sergeant-
cook of a battalion ? ” There are no “ sergeant-cooks ”in
Australia. Now, the questions for Gen. French to answer
are: 1. If these exams, are anything but a sham, why
doesn’t he compel all, without distinction, to pass
them before getting commission ? 2. Why does he not
follow the example of every other examining-body and
make the papers public, so that candidates could see
what sort of questions they are expected to face?
Till these queries are publicly answered, it is reasonable
to infer that this secret exam, is a howling sham.

The attention of our readers is drawn to
the advertisement on page 21 of this issue
announcing a new volume of popular and
humorous verses by Henry Lawson. A glance at
the contents of the volume there given reminds us
that many of his best pieces were not included in
his first volume, “In the Days When the World
Was Wide.”*
N.Z. Loan and Mercantile Agency Co. reports
that the new grain is now arriving in considerable
quantities and that many lots which the owners desired
to dispose of “ ex truck ” have been sold on arrival at full
current rates, Numerous consignments on which owners
wished to have the advantage of three months free storage
have been housed, the owners hoping that at the end of
that time, values will be more tempting than at present.
Millers are still operating with considerable discretion,
while shippers are desirous of doing business at rates
quite on a relative parity with London values. Japan is
just now a buyer, and the leading representative here
is prepared to do business at ruling rates. Quota-
tions : Last season’s grain, 2s. 6d. to 2s. 7d. for prime.
This season’s prime, 2s. 6d., pinched, 2s. 4d. to 2s. 6d.,
and chickwheat 2s. to 2s. 2d. per bushel.*

Unconscious on the Floor.
I purpose to write, or rather edit, a short chap-
ter in the history of a woman’s life. There is no
romance about it, yet those of her sex who fail to
read this plain tale will miss something which
may easily be of more value to them than all the
novels that have been published in a twelvemonth.
Most of the story will be in her own words, the
present writer merely adding a line or so of
comment, for the sake of clearness, should it be
needed. The whole is the substance of a state-
ment made by her at her home at Bank, Barnard
Castle, about a year ago, and originally intended
for public use. The paper having been for some
time accidentally mislaid, it could not sooner
appear in print. The woman referred to is Mrs.
Mary Jane Canham, well known in Barnard
Castle.
It seems that one day, nearly or quite ten years
ago, a friend entered the house and found Mrs.
Canham, lying on the floor, perfectly unconscious,
and entirely alone. What had struck her down ?
“The little I have to tell”—so Mrs. Canham
says—“ begins in the Autumn of 1889. It was
then I commenced to feel weak and poorly. What
ailed me I didn’t know. The strength went out
of me, and I felt heavy and tired. I took no
interest in my work, or in anything else; all
things, no matter how trifling, were an anxiety
and a worry to me ; my mind had lost its tone.
“ I had always been a good eater, but now I
had no wish or desire for food. My family would
say, ‘ Oh, do try to eat something; you will feel
better.’ To please them I did try, but it was no
use. After eating there would be a feeling of
weight and pain, and then it all came up again.
No matter how simple and plain the food might
be, the result was the same; I could not keep it
down.
“Sometimes the pain in the pit of my stomach
and in my back was so great I could neither sit
nor lie still; I rolled on the floor in a distress I
have no power to make you understand.
“After a while my kidneys became affected,
and the secretion from them was thick and often
mixed with blood. I was then attacked with
gravel, and suffered horribly from it. One day,
when I was in the house alone, I had a dreadful
spell with this complaint, and for a long time I lay
unconscious on the floor. Once I passed a large
stone in such agony that I thought I should die.
“By this time I was so weak and run down
that my limbs trembled and yielded under me
when I tried to walk. When out of doors I was
in fear of falling prostrate in the road.
“Then, occasionally, I would be seized with
dizziness, and things appeared to swim around
me; and often, in crossing a room, or even in
standing, I was obliged to hold on by some article
of furniture. All the details of my sad experi-
ence, if I could remember them, would make my
story too long; and you would weary of them
anyway.
“ It will be enough to say that I lingered along
in misery for nearly three years. Neither medical
treatment, nor the medicines I heard of, and used
of my own accord, did me any good.
“It was in August, 1892, that my niece said
she knew of remarkable cures that had been done
by Mother Seigel’s Syrup, and strongly advised
me to try it. I answered that after the medicines
I had already taken, to no purpose, it wasn’t likely
this one would help me. And I think it was a
reasonable conclusion.
“ Nevertheless there was a spark of hope in it,
and my husband got me a bottle from the Co-
operative stores in Bridlegate, and after I had
taken the Syrup only a short time I felt better—
yes, very much better. I could eat something
without pain or sickness, and began to believe I
might yet get well
“I was very badly off, remember, and when I
say that even Mother Seigel’s Syrup, took nearly
or quite two months to cure me, you will admit it
to be a wonderful remedy. But it did cure me—
which is the point I want others to know of.
Since that time I have been well and hearty.”
This case was a combination of chronic and pro-
found indigestion, with kidney and bladder
troubles, such as are the despair of the best phy-
sicians, and form the hopeless processions to the
hospitals.
Yet Mother Seigel’s Syrup soon overcame it,
and gave this woman back to the world of healthy
and happy people. Recollect it when the dark
days come to you or yours.
A successful surgical treatment of con-
sumption is reported from Madrid, where a lady,
it is said, had her chest and left lung opened by
Prof. Molle, who treated the tuberculous portions
of the lung with antiseptics and cautery—the
result, to date, being a marked progress towards
recovery.
At South Melbourne, t’other day, a man
was fined 10s. or 24 hours for indecent behaviour.
It was his 61st conviction ! Probably the 62nd
sentence will be two years and a flogging. And
the law calls this sort of happy-go-lucky arrange-
ment a scientific system.
A Melbourne constable was, t’other day,
told by Chief Commissioner Chomley to resign or
take the sack. Of course, he resigned. He had
merely written the name of a J.P. at foot of a
summons, apparently to save himself the trouble of
hunting up a “beak” in the flesh. Now, the
common civilian person who commits forgery (this
was certainly “wilful,” if not “ corrupt ”) gener-
ally gets more than the sack.
And now we get from England some more
irritating blather about “ Sir Langdon Bonython”
of S.A. Self-Advertiser and his dreary doings,
how he has been inspecting certain schools—and
so on. Why, simply because this Bonython man
happens to be one of the press-cable ring, should
his dull, drab commonplaces be inflicted upon us
at breakfast ? To the devil, says the public, with
self-puffing nonentities who inspect schools at the
other end of the earth, and then seek to bore Aus-
tralia about it at ham-and-eggs time.
Six thousand copies of Henry Lawson’s

The “hat” suicide is spreading. Mel-
bourne had a short and exciting run of pre-
meditated corpses who left notes in the lining of
their hats stating the reason why they did it.
Then Sydney had the hat without the corpse.
Now it has the hat with the corpse. The latest
case drowned himself at Coogee, leaving the usual
statement in his hat to the effect that he was dead,
and that the remains were there or thereabouts.
Then he left the head-garment on the rocks and
dropped himself in. He was a friendless bailiff,
aged 64, and given to drink, and when a bailiff
reaches that age he has mostly outlived human
sympathy.
Looks as though Port Fairy (Yic.) is a
somewhat Irish electorate. The four candidates
at the recent election were William o‘Brien,
Bryan o‘Loghlen, Martin Bourke, and James
Duffus.
“ The Presbyterian Church of Australia ”
—first fruits of the Federal spirit. Under the
new caption go the Presbyterian churches of
N.S.W., Vic., Q,, S.A., and Tasmania. So runs
the official record. But where are the Westralian
Presbyterians ?
“Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“ Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
the author’s “absolute genius.” The S.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
The denizens of Melbourne slums do not
get many chances of enjoying the beauties of
nature. The other day, however, 55 covered
waggons conveyed 1200 men, women, and children
to the annual picnic of the Latrobe-street Ragged-
school, held near Sandringham. Liberal amuse-
ments were provided and the crowd was as orderly
in its way as a fashionable garden-party. As
Walter Besant would have said, the scene was a
more cheering sight than any results of the teles-
copic philanthropy which expends itself on un-
sympathetic Chinamen or inhabitants of savage
lands who enjoy life best when left to their own
devices.
Harland and Wolff, of Belfast, are building
for the White Star Line a steamer of the enor-
mous capacity of 20,000 tons—3ooo tons bigger
than even the huge Oceanic. Apropos, the big
Atlantic liner, Campania, which lately ran into
the 1200-ton iron barque Embleton, in a fog off
the Irish coast, cut the unfortunate “wind-
jammer” so clean in two that the halves sank on
each side of the steam-monster. And this at nine
knots, or much less than half-speed !
S.A. Register thus seeks to prepare Ade-
laide for Mayor-elect and publican Ware’s Com-
monwealth-celebration title. The thing is evi-
dently printed to show that Ware has been used
to titles, and is warranted to wear his as if he had
grown up in it:—
THE MAYOR’S ANCESTORS.
James Ware (author of the history of the Ware family)
was descended from a noble and ancient family in York-
shire, which flourished in the name of Roger de Ware,
Lord of Isefleld, and a baron of Parliament in the reign of
King Edward I. Many of that family under the name of
de Warr, de Warre, and le Ware, were in succeeding
reigns summoned to Parliament as lords thereof. James,
the younger son of Christopher Ware and father to our
author, was liberally educated, and when he came to
maturity was sent to push his fortunes in the court of
Queen Elizabeth, where he soon distinguished himself in
such manner as to obtain the favor of the great men then
at the head of affairs. He accompanied Sir William Fitz
Williams, who was sent over to Ireland as lord deputy, as
his secretary. In a journey to England he was honored
with knighthood by King James I. Sir James Ware was
born in Castle-street, on November 26, 1594. And so
on, &c.
The childishness of it all !
One of Lord Hopetoun’s postillions has
been injured. “ Postillions ” in Australia I Ye
gods !
Printed that Toby Barton has promised
to stand for the Federal constituency which in-
cludes Orange town and district, the site already
selected by Sydney Calico Jimmydom as the
Federal capital. The successful candidate for
this electorate will not necessarily be a Protec-
tionist, or a Freetrader, nor will he be a guardian
of the Constitution. He will be simply and solely
a barracker for the capital, first and last, and
when returned will be expected to advance the
“claims” of Orange in a loud voice and to
advance them often. As Barton aims at a high
position in the Commonwealth Parliament, and is
more than a mere possibility for the very highest,
the squabble for the capital should be beyond him,
and it is satisfactory to have his assurance that he
has not yet selected a constituency.
Intending visitors to Sydney during the
coming celebrations are referred to Alexander and
Rochets advt. on page 19.
The Melbourne Herald prize design
Federal Flag will be flown from the Massey-
Harris Bicycle Co., 365 George-street, on Satur-
day, the 15th instant. It was designed by Mr.
F. Thompson, of Melbourne, and selected by a
committee of judges out of about 8000 designs sent
by competitors from all parts of Australasia. The
flag has the “Jack” in the corner, representative
of the British Empire. The six red bars beneath
the “Jack” show the six federated colonies, and
the Southern Cross (on the Red field for Govern-
ment or Military, and Blue for Naval) the Aus-
tralian Emblem.

Great Weakness and Anaemia
An Interesting Case
And a Perfect Recovery.
The Case of
MRS. SARAH DILLON.
(by oor own reporter.)
As our reporter was one day pursuing his
usual mission, involving the collection of
items of interest to the reading section of
the community, he happened to hear of the
remarkable recovery from weakness and
continuous headaches of Mrs. Sarah Dillon,
who was then residing at 5 Norton-street,
Surry Hills. He accordingly called upon
that lady, who cheerfully offered to give
him all the information he desired.
“ My case must have been a particularly
interesting one to the doctors who attended
upon me,” began Mrs. Dillon, “for the very
simple reason that they were of the opinion
that I was beyond the scope of medical
treatment. I can assure you it is exceed-
ingly dispiriting to be told by your physician
that your condition is the natural result of
old age creeping on, and anything he
prescribed for you could not possibly do any
good. And it is especially so for a woman
like myself, who had previously enjoyed the
best of health. I had never before that
time known what it was to have a day’s
sickness, excepting as a child, when I met
with an accident to my hand, which soon
got all right again ; but I do not call that
sickness. And then to be suddenly
conscious of an ailment which was gradually
drawing all the vitality from me, and to be
told that nothing could be recommended by
my doctor to cure me, you can easily
imagine how despondent I became.”
“What were the symptcu&s of your
malady, Mrs. Dillon?”
“Well, I cannot very easily describe them
at the commencement of my illness, because
they were altogether too complicated. I
knew that my blood was not in good order
on account of the alteration in the colour of
my complexion. My lips assumed an un-
healthy paleness, and my eyes became dull
and heavy. Icannotsay that Isuffered a great
amount of pain, although very frequently I
would become possessed of a sickly feeling
which left me languid and extremely weak.
It would have given me a great amount of
relief if I could have slept when I retired to
bed at night; but even that luxury was
denied me, for I would turn in feeling so
jaded and worn out that I felt quite sure of
getting a little repose; but I was invariably
disappointed. As a result, it was almost an
impossibility to get up in the mornings, and
when I did manage to do so, after much
perseverance, I was too weak and exhausted
to stand. During the day I would be
afflicted with a feeling of a drowsy languor,
as though everything taking place around
me was no interest or importance; and the
next development in my failing health was
something entirely new 1 had never
before known what it was to faint; but now
it was a frequent occurrence for me to go
right off without any apparent cause, and
when I regained consciousness I would be in
a depressed state of mind for a long time.
It was enough to frighten any woman at my
time of life to experience such threatening
indications of a general break-up of the
system; and when I tell you that my
appetite had abandoned me altogether, you
can understand how dejected I was. The
most tempting food failed to make me eat,
and the robust appearance I had so long
been proud of gave. way to a condition of
emaciation J The sight of fodd was a source
of trouble to myself, as my inability to eat
was an additional reminder that I was
gradually wasting away. And the hopeless-
ness with which I thought of the future was
brought about through the doctors’ opinion
that there was nothing in the way of
medicine which could possibly restore me to
health. The idea of being in a state of such
utter abandonment occasioned me no end
of worry and anxiety, which, I’m sure you will
agree, was quite natural. No matter how ill
a person may be, I think they can always
keep up their spirits to a certain extent if
they are following out a course of treatment
which will encourage them to believe that
their health may ultimately be regained.
But in my case I was apparently left without
a chance. However, I made my mind up
that it would be foolish to continue in such
a state of misery without making an effort
in some direction, because I did not think
anything in the way of physic could have the
effect of making me worse, my condition
then being so forlorn. By a stroke of good
luck I secured the right remedy at the first
attempt.”
“How were you so fortunate?” enquired
the reporter.
“ It was through listening to the advice of
a lady friend of mine, who had benefited
through the same medium, that I was so
favoured by Dame Fortune. She strongly
recommended me to try Clements Tonic,
assuring me that if I did so there would be a
decided change for the better in a very short
space of time. It was with a certain
amount of doubt and misgiving that I con-
sented to give it a trial, although it was a
pleasure to think that there was something
which might have the effect of recuperating
my wasted system. I was really too
dejected to place confidence in any medicine;
but I sent for a bottle of Clements Tonic by
way of experiment, and, would you believe
me ? I felt myself improving after the first
few dose*. Day by day my appetite gained
ground, and I was soon able to sit down
and enjoy a comfortable meal. The old,
worn out, despondent feeling of weakness
entirely left me, and the vigorous blood
that now ran through my veins had such a
stimulating effect upon my spirits that I
soon became quite cheerful again. The
racking headaches from which I had
suffered were but a memory of the past; and
I can tell you they used to be very severe
indeed. They never attack me now,
however, for I continued taking Clements
Tonic till iny vitality was thoroughly
restored, and my health is now as good as
ever. I think in a country like this, where
the heat is so oppressive in summer,
Clements Tonic is a grand thing to have in
the house, and I always have it on hand
during the hot weather to take as a stimu-
lant. I believe that is why my strength
keeps up so well.”
“Do you give your consent to the
publication of this interview ? ”
“With the utmost pleasure. I owe my
recovery exclusively to Clements Tonic, and
now I always recommend it to my friends.”

The Rhyme of Sudden Jerk.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Editor the Emperor is of cable, comp, and
proof—
The architect creates the house from damp-course
unto roof—■
The Czar he is the Lord Most High of Cossack
and of Russ —
And the gripman of the dummy-car—he hath his
will of us !
But none have such authority—strong, stern, and
absolute—
Authentic and immutable o’er man, and mine, and
brute,
As he who sits in majesty to hear the miner
sue—-
The Warden of the sultry mining - field of
Girofloo.
Now, when you want to lease a claim (the regula-
tions say)
Thou shalt peg ’twixt dawn and sundown, and
identify the day
With a statutory statement that you’ve carried
out the Act—
(And the statement may be fiction—but some-
times it is fact).
Thou shalt mark thy datum “Number One,” and
run each boundary line—
Thou shalt dig at every corner peg “ L ” trenches
as a sign—
Thou shalt post thy written notice of intention to
apply,
And sit down in exemption till the boom comes
by-and-bye.
The land was full of weariness, for the mining
“experts” came;
They talked of dykes of elvan, and of diorite the
same,
They laughed at garnet rock, and they on stock-
werks put a slight;
They wept because there were no schists—they
sneered at eclogyte.
There was one who’d been a draughtsman, and
such pretty plans he drew !
With the crosscuts all in carmine, and the drives
all tinted blue,
And the shafts outlined in yellow and the stopes
in Indian ink —
But all he knew of mining would have driven
a man to drink.
Yet he’d drawn some plans in London, and some
Hooleys saw them there ;
And they said “What pretty plans they are—
how well he draws a square !
We’ll appoint him as our expert, and when we get
a mine
He’ll make such lovely plans of it in tint and
black and line.”
So they sent him to Australia with geranium-lake
for freight,
And he came to represent the Anglo - Yiddish
Syndicate.
(And he saw one mine to please him and he swore
by it—and God;
And now that great mine’s twenty stamps are
singing “Icliabod.”)
There was an hydraulic crank who called red
oxides “ paying-dirt ”
And who sneered at any mining not transacted
with a squirt—
And a third, who disbelieving good news could
not resist;
And who got uncertain tucker as a profess’nal
pessimist.
’Twas optimism made the world all fit for man to
live—
But this “ expert ” just existed by indiscriminate
negative—
And ; ’f ™ ore s P ecs are wrong than right—his
Ao seemed not a sin—
An< l£ 'w/T “ N °”
man wasn 1 ln ’
So these “experts” sampled Garnet, and they
. turned down Chillagoe;
And they said Ihese shows are surface blows;
And-“ the abandoned ’cause there
An dTt * Collins found the
The^udde/Jerk was big and rich-blue and
green carbonates-
And fibrous malachite by tons—the country rock
was slates—
(And the “experts” loved slate country—near as
much as they loved schist—
The sluicing crank—the draughtsman—the pro-
fessional pessimist.
Ban Collins had his one man’s ground—’twas
cheaper than a lease ;
(If a n engine cost a bob Dan hadn’t money for the
grease)—
But he’d got a fortnight’s tucker and he’d done a
little work,
When the “experts” came from Herberton to
criticise the Jerk.
His crazy old prospecting shaft some twenty feet
he’d sunk;
And the “experts” opened whisky, and they
tried to make him drunk,
While the Indian-inking artist brought his know-
ledge into sight,
And said “ That ore that’s in the sink is called
chalcotrichite—
Capillary crystallization of red oxide,” and much
more—
Dan Collins said he’d keep the claim, and they
could name the ore.
They offered him a tenner, and sprang four times
the price,
And waved a note for fifty pounds (their manners
weren’t nice).
But Collins drank their whisky, and refused to
sell his claim,
So they pegged him in on every side and beat him
at his game ;
And, when they’d gone he lost his nerve, and
wished that he had sold ;
For his bullion was his energy—their brains were
just their gold.
He cursed his luck until he knew the curse’s text
by rote ;
And in his curse he stopped and bent—he’d found
the “experts’” note !
The wording of the note was terse and unto the
effect
That the Q.N. Bank had fifty pounds the holder
could collect.
And Dan was flushing hot and cold and trembling
in the legs—
But the trembling didn’t stop him shifting all the
“experts’ ” pegs.
He left the range next sunrise, and by curlew-
time that night
He had walked to Buffing Billy’s, and he circled
from the light,
For in Puffing Billy’s shanty, talking wildly of the
pegs,
Was the draughtsman, badly needing a search-
warrant for his legs.
And while the sluicing crank talked schemes to
dredge the Sudden Jerk;
And the professional pessimist rehearsed his holy
work
Of keeping the investor out of mines of eclogyte—
Dan walked sturdily unto the Warden’s office in
the night.
The sheoaks in the gullies struck him gently as he
strode,
Grey shadows of the night-birds came upon the
ghostly road ;
The night—o’erfilled with pity—draped the shafts
that looked like scars,
And through it went Dan Collins up the range
and in the stars.
For miles the track sped upward and the air was
soft as moss;
And through and up sped Daniel till his head was
next the Cross ;
And over and down the range’s scarp, by springs
that night new-born,
Until the town roofs whitened to the lifting of the
morn.
He made his application for the “ experts’ ” lease
that day—
And their fifty paid the charges, and the balance
went astray ;
(For Dan was able-bodied at the rum when it was
red,
And for seven nights thereafter he was carried to
his bed).
And when the experts came to town, and found
Dan had applied;
Thej' sneered some more and then began the
country to deride;
They objected to the Warden, but “ the weight of
evidence ”
Was on the side of Daniel, and the experts’ “lame
pretence
At objecting ” met dismissal —for stern and abso-
lute,
Authentic and immutable, o’er man, and mine,
and brute,
Who in equity and majesty doth hear the miner
sue —
Is the Warden of the sultry mining field of
Girofloo.
The experts wept their weep for schists—they
sneered at eclogyte,
And left the town disgusted in the Southern
coach —by night.
But Daniel “ optioned out ” his show and three
shifts started work—-
Now ! four one-hundred tonner smelters flame at
Sudden Jerk,
And open-cuts three hundred deep show oxides
red as blood—
And two chains wide they tear her tripes while
glows the smelter rud.
“A conscience grows from great success”—all
men for long have known—
And Mister Daniel Collins reaped what plain old
Dan had sown,
And his conscience being uneasy he confessed him
to the priest
Expecting, for a penance, half a little hell at
least.
But the Father spoke him soothingly, and said,
‘ The note to find
Was very wrong—but ’twould be worse to leave
the coin behind ! ’
And the Sudden Jerk’s in better hands than if
they’d held the mine ;
And the way ye walked the long day through and
the long day’s night was fine !
And then they stopped half-way up on the track,
though they were horsed aright,
And they sneered at the blessed country-side, and
they scoffed at eclogyte.
No man to leave three ‘ experts ’ in the mud
could well resist
The sluicing crank, the draughtsman, and the
professional pessimist.”
“And now” (said the man who told me this)
“ it’s time I went to work—
I keep the time for the surface-hands and the
slagmen at the Jerk.”
RANDOLPH BEDFORD.
One member of Vic. Bushmen’s corps in
S’Africa got hold, for a little over face-value, of a
remarkable curio in the shape of an old Boer Re-
public £l-note, which, as bearing the signatures
of Pretorius, Joubert and Yiljoen, will be sale-
able in London at a very high price.
Concerning London larrikinism :
The “Hooligans” are on the war-trail, writes The
Bulletin’s London correspondent, and in ten days have
killed and maimed more persons than the Governors from
and including their initial murderous carnival. Yet the
doings of the two blacks down-under have been pointed
at and emphasised as sky-signs of Australian lawlessness.
Here, practically in London’s heart, not a mile from St.
Paul’s, boys, men and girls are shot, mutilated, or kicked
to death by gangs of boys of 17 to 20 years. Tire
“ Hooligans” go about in mobs, armed with cheap revol-
vers, knives, and weapons equal in effect to “ slung-shot.”
The “ Rocks Push,” the King-st. (Melb.) “ Forty Thieves ”
under Tippoo Hayes, and the “ Carlton Rorties,” com-
pared to the “ Hooligans,” appear merely romping lambs.
The “ Hoolies,” revolver-armed, kick men to death, brass-
buokle-belt the life out of some youngster—or shoot him
as they did a boy this week, stoush the teeth into the
face of a young girl, and stab indiscriminately. Sydney
ex-Police-Supt. W. E. Lenthall, in an interview, advises
arming the London police with revolvers, but I am afraid
that wouldn’t stop it. Too many woman-hearted P.M.’s
here ; and though the tactful and good-tempered London
police are the finest street police in the world, they’re too
well off to go looking for “ scraps.”
A petitioner for divorce told Judge Hood,
t’other day, in Melbourne, that he had been mar-
ried in ’63 and had lived happily with his wife for
31 years, but one morning in ’94, on returning
home for lunch, he found her removing the
last of the furniture. “ What,” said he, “ are you
shifting?” “Yes,” said she, and that was all
that passed. She went away then and remained
away permanently, leaving him nothing but the
keyhole. He was perfectly friendly with his wife
on the morning she went off, and could give no
reason for her abrupt departure. Apparently she
just hired a van and packed up the furniture —his
furniture —and passed away, and he was too tired
to ask her where she was going, or whether her
departure was permanent, or anything. He wrote
to her once or twice, but she took no notice. He
knew of no cause to keep them apart; it
was just a case of aimless, negligent drifting,
and unless some contrary current set in and forced
them together again, they would probably con-
tinue to drift through the next century. But
Judge Hood declined to swallow the simple,
casual tale, and this derelict of a side-wind,
so to speak, will have to wander alone for the rest
of his days unless he can persuade the old lady to
drift back again.

Heard in Trains, 'Buses—Everywhere and Always DOAN’S.
When incidents like the following occur here in Sydney, and
Sydney men and Sydney women relate their experiences in a
Sydney paper for the benefit of Sydney people, the genuineness
of the statements cannot be doubted. They have the ring of truth
about them that there is no getting away from.
Here is the case of Mrs. Fred. Russell, 7 St.
Andrews-street, Balmain: —“My husband has
been bad three or four years with kidney trouble.
He was such a sufferer that he could not stand up
straight, and all his movements were fraught with
great pain. Unable to sleep at night, he used to
try all manner of positions, and would get up next
morning thoroughly done up and unrefreshed.
The best position for him to lie in was face down-
ward, but he would soon have to change that.
Sometimes the pains were a lot worse than others.
To stand up straight was worse than hard work.
When well (before he got bad I mean) he used to
do heavy work, but he had to give up, his back
having well-nigh crippled him. He went to hos-
pitals one after another; and when they failed to
give him ease from his terrible pain he tried all
manner of remedies advertised, excepting one.
We spent a lot of money in plasters and liniments
and other things for outward applications
but he was still as bad as ever after using
them—aye, worse. It is now over 12 months
since that lucky day when we got Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills at Newman’s Pharmacy,
King-street. At that time he was unable
to sit down or lie down, and when he walked he
held his back all the time. His pains were fright-
ful. Well, the first few does of Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills rid him of those terrible pains, and
after a time he became much improved. In all
he took six boxes. Then he left off the medicine,
and has taken none since, and that is over six
months ago. A few days ago he said to me, ‘ You
never hear me complain of pains now; Doan’s
Backache Kidney Pills are simply grand.’ ”
Then there is the case of Mrs. Spear, of 32
Goodsell-street, St. Peters, Sydney, who suffered
for a good while with her back, a constant dull
ache about the kidneys, who was at last cured by
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills.
And there are the cases of Mrs. Gregg, 9 En-
more-street, Newtown ; Joseph Grieve, 18
Albion-street, Surry Hills ; Mr. E.. Hansen, 41
Rosser-street, Balmain ; Mrs. F. May, Excelsior-
street, Woollahra; and hundreds of others, all
cured by Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills, of illness,
the full facts of their cases have already been
published in the Sydney papers.

Quatrains.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“Rest.
When, spent with fight, the swordsman drops his
brand
And his green laurel-wreath grows wan
How good it is to clasp with pallid hand
Thy poppy red—Oblivion !
Philosophy.
Life is a web with many broken ends;
Then why, 0 friend, be sad ?
Good is not near so good as it pretends }
Bad is not half so bad.
The Others.
The Coming Men —in shoals come on,
The Men Arrived—I know them well.
But what of Those who’ve Come —and Gone ?
Let publicans and sextons tell.
Death.
Pale lies the Bridegroom, pining sore,
Then, in that hour of hope and fear,
There comes a knocking at the door,
A voice that cries—“ The Bride is here ! ’
Finis.
Faith shuts her eyes,
Poor self-deceiver !
The Last God dies
With the Last Believer.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
Our New Selection.
II.— STARTING AT SADDLETOR.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At Saddletop. Dad looking older; Mother ditto.
Hal tall and womanly; Dave a big, bony man,
bearded and more reserved than ever. Joe ap-
proaching manhood; broad-shouldered, sturdy,
full-faced and irresistibly droll; he was our own
comedian, entertained us often, and gave us pains
in the side by “taking people off”—frequently
Dad and Dave, in their absence. The younger
ones all “stretching out,”—and—no more babies.
Our new selection was a piece of magnificent
country ; twelve hundred acres, all rich black soil
10 to 15 feet deep; permanent water and a
Government well outside the fence; a wide fringe
of heavy timber at the back ; plain in front, and
grass ! —we used to lose the horses in it.
The house was new—a stately palace, after
Shingle Hut; five rooms in it; weather-boarded ;
floor-boarded ; iron-roofed ; lock and key to every
door ; kitchen, and a thousand-gallon tank. And
such water ! we never knew the taste of real
water before. No charcoal; no ashes ; no traces
of a drought; no doubtful flavor of any kind
about it, and no having to scrape a scum off when-
ever we boiled some.
And Dad was proud of the selection. He was
pleased with our prospects altogether, and talked
of the progress the district was making.
* * * *
Saddletop was a rising place. A branch rail-
way-line was coming to it—it had been coming for
twenty-two years. Farmers from South Australia
were there—men who, ’twas said, knew how to
farm ; who understood all about soils and silos,
and worked on scientific principles. They were
settled along a deserted gully where nothing but
burr and thistle and nut-grass grew, and whenever
it rained hard they would retreat in disorder to
highland, with their wives in barrows and their
beds and things on their backs.
There was one farmer from Victoria ; he knew
something about selecting. He came and rooted
the trees out of his land and put in wheat, and
watched it grow for a while; then went away one
morning and never came back.
A number of old pioneers were there, just be-
ginning to do well after 30 years of toil and
struggle—the difficulties and dangers and innu-
merable hardships encountered by them but
faintly comprehended.
Up dry gullies and blind gullies, hidden away
behind ranges and rocks, were selectors who hoed
holes in the long grass and dropped pumpkin-seed
into them, then went away somewhere and came
back with a horse at the end of a long rope, which
they handled and broke for somebody while the
crop was growing.
There were a few of a species who, in wet
weather, stood all day long in their doorway with
a wet bag on their shoulders, gazing out philo-
sophically on the pelting rain, and when it
wasn’t raining they went away with a blanket and
looked for shearing.
A weird, silent ‘ ‘ hatter ” was there, whose hair
penetrated his hat and who stitched patches on
his pants with a packing-needle and string—the
strange man who lived under the range away from
everybody, in a bark hut propped upon the down-
hill side by a stick—the man who avoided all
human society—whose hut contained harness,
flung on the floor, and scraps of greenhide and a
greasy table with sapling legs driven into the
ground ; who sat anywhere on the floor and took
his dinner off a tin plate placed on his knees, and
asked questions of his watchful dog, and told it
things while the birds of the bush came round the
open door and hopped in and out unmolested ; the
man who never grumbled, whose happinesses were
the silences and solitudes of his surroundings,
whose God was Nature and whose only hell the
toothache.
The selector of regular movements was there —
the methodical man who caught the same old
horse each morning by gladdening the steed’s
heart with the sound of the same few handfuls of
corn rattled in a dish, and always went some-
where. His form was known in the district miles
off. He was a clock to those working in the
fields. They told the time of day by him as he
passed along,
There were people there, too, from the town ;
broken-down “swells” who professed hatred for
the bush and were always going back to the city
—but never started ; polite people who wouldn’t
drink tea out of a saucer; who loved flower
gardens—and grew one geranium in a tin ; whose
daughters declared all the young men around
rouuh because they neglected to lift their hats,
and who came out in holland riding-habits be-
cause they were cheap, and said they were all the
fashion in town.
There was one man who regularly employed
men —who possessed everything necessary about a
farm and 'stored his hay in sheds, and drew
cheaues. He was the squire of the district. He
always travelled to town by train, first-class, and
never said “Good-day” to the neighbors, except
in moments of absent-mindedness. He might
have been worth a thousand pounds ; people often
called him a millionaire and it never worried him.
It made him less absent-minded. He met Minis-
terial parties and influential visitors to the district
at the railway-station, and drove them in a buggy
to his house, and entertained them, and showed
them over the farm, and filled the “special cor-
respondent ’ with inspirations of his (the squire’s)
own enterprise, and ideas about “prosperous
selectors,” and a “district with a future.”
Scattered about in remote places were average
selectors—the plodders; men who poked along
leisurely and reared large families and took re-
verses as cheerfully as they took pills —men who
placed large stones and heavy fencing-material on
the roof to keep it from flying off when there
was any wind (the roof kept the stones from fall-
ing through and shattering the dinner-table when
there was no wind); men who reckoned they could
make money and save, if only the seasons could be
relied on and they had no interest to meet.
And, some distance from each other, a small
school, surrounded by gum-trees, and two un-
painted churches of battered, warlike appearances
raised their heads. The “heathen” school-
children battered them. They frequently quar-
relled over religion on the way from school, and,
as there was no one to read the Riot Act to them,
wrecked each other’s churches with stones.
* * * •
We were not long at Saddletop when we became
acquainted with these people. Many of them
came to welcome us to the district —some to borrow
things.
Miss Wilkins and Miss Mulrooney were among
the first to come. They came one bright afternoon
when the air was fresh and the sky was blue—
when the birds were singing and the butterflies
fluttering—when Dad and Cranky Jack were
trenching for fruit-trees, and Dave and Joe plough-
ing on the plain.
They slid off their horses at the fence surround-
ing the house, and laughed. The laugh was to
attract attention. They stepped on to the veran-
dah and curtseyed, and said it was a “lovely
day.” Mother invited them in. They hesitated
as though time were precious, and Miss Wilkins
said, “We can only stay just a minute, Mrs.
Rudd. ” Then they sat down and poked out the
toes of their boots from under their habits, and
looked through the corners of their eyes at every-
thing in the room, and didn’t go away till dusk.
Miss Wilkins was a stout person, fat and flabby,
and owned to being five-and-twenty. She had
owned to five-and-twenty for sixteen years. She
laced tight, too, and fancied herself thin and
shapely, and Polly Palethorpe and Annie Hayes
(both jammed into one wouldn’t equal her fat)
were objects of astonishment to her.
Miss Mulrooney had a figure, and was only 28.
She had seen some town life, too, and was one of
those who wore a holland habit. There were
hints of vanished finery about her—she was hard-
up, in fact, and strove to conceal her poverty by
putting on airs.
She sat and drank tea and ate scones and fresh
butter with much gusto; praised the provender
every time she reached for more; asked mother
if she made the scones herself —as if she would
call in a traveller or get Dad to bake them !
Miss Wilkins talked to Sal about a dance
that was at the Rise, and Jim Murphy
courting Nor ah Fahey, and old Fahey chasing
Jim with a gun, punctuating her conversation
with loud, cheerful shrieks ; and Miss Mulrooney
told mother in tired tones how hard it was on
them all to have to rough it in the bush after being
so well off in town, used to their carriage and ser-
vants. (Her father, the story went, had been
employed by a lawyer in Brisbane as confidential
clerk. He used to open the “ private ” door when-
ever a client came, which was nearly once a
month, and inquire in a respectful tone of the
empty chair if he was engaged; then hand the
client another chair without making a noise and
steal out into the street to find the lawyer and
drag him in by a back way. She assured mother
(and mother, good soul, believed her and felt
deeply for the family) that she didn’t know how
they would live at all only for the little money
papa was still receiving from the business in town,
as there was nothing at all to be got out of farm-
ing.
And as they talked and drank more tea the
schoolmaster’s wife and Mary O’Reilly and Miss
Perkins approached the house. Miss Wilkins saw
them through the window and whispered to Miss
Mulrooney. Miss Mulrooney fidgeted and was
uneasy. But, recognising the horses fastened to
the fence, the schoolmaster’s wife and Mary
O’Reilly and Miss Perkins turned their heads
away and rode past. They weren’t speaking to
Miss Wilkins or Miss Mulrooney.
» * * *
Gray (the “squire” of the district) came one
morning; he merely looked-in because he hap-
pened to be passing. But he wouldn’t come
inside—that would be making himself “ too
cheap.” Ho stayed in the yard and talked with
an air of superior knowledge to Dad.
“What do you want with a thing like that?”
he said, pointing to a new three-furrow plough.
(He was a stale Conservative who wouldn't see,
and regarded everything new with contempt.)
“To plough with, and save time and labour,”
Dad answered.
“ Save your grandmother ! ” Gray said ; “ I’ve a
couple of single-furrows over there,” he went on,
indicating his farm, “ and I guarantee any of my
men ’ll turn over as much land in a day with any
one of them as you will with that.”
“ Well,” Dad said, “ send a man along with one,
an’ we’ll see.”
“And plough your land for you ?—Yah ! ’’—and
Gray went away.
And Sam Evans came, and stood on the
verandah, bashfully turning an old hat round and
round in his hands, and wouldn’t step inside
because the place was clean. But Barney
Ballantyne would. He said it was “cheaper
sittin’ than stand in’,” and sat down on Sal’s hat
that had been left on a chair, and told Dad lies
about wheat-crops, and he chewed tobacco, and spat
squares and circles till they evolved into carpet
patterns on the cleanly-scrubbed floor.
Many others called, and in due course we
returned their visits; we mixed with them in
their homes, mingled with them abroad, shared
their successes, their prejudices and sympathies,
joined with them in song and sorrow in these new
surroundings, and among new friends and true
friends we faced the vagaries of fortune afresh and
commenced a new life.
STEELE RUDD.
According to a Sydneyite on the staff of
the British general in China, only the Japanese
and the British are treating the natives with any
consideration. The cruelty of the Russians he
describes as fiendish. “Villages are destroyed,
and I cannot write about the fate of the poor
inhabitants. These foreign ruffians think of
nothing but loot, and sable rugs worth probably £5O
are often sold for a few bottles of beer.” The
Russian, be it remembered, is at bottom an Asiatic
himself!

03XTES THOUSAND PEOPLE ....
died from Consumption in Sydney last year, and this dire disease is spreading day by day. And no wonder. If citizens are so careless about their supply of Milk, the wonder is that there
are not more deaths. There will be ! !
HERE’S A. SHOlntT STORY. .... * •
A milk supplier to Sydney had three cows suffering from Tuberculosis, and as he was afraid his healthy cattle would become contaminated, he sold them at less than £1 each to another
supplier who sent his milk to the same distributors. The seller now feels annoyed to think that the Sydney purchaser had been paying for all the milk from the diseased animals for some
months past. THIS MILK IS BEING SOLD IN SYDNEY TO-DAY ! This is no fairy tale, but an absolute fact, capable of being easily proven. This is only an individual case;
there are many, alas ! too many of a similar nature.
IIffSFBOTIOsir OJj’ I>A.IRIEI3 J&.TSTT2 OA.TTLB ....
The N.S.W. Fresh Food and Ice Coy., Ltd., have every cow in every dairy thoroughly inspected, as well as the premises and utensils used. They have their own railway trucks. All milk
arriving under standard quality is condemned. They laugii at local inspection. They lose suppliers because of their strict methods of inspection.
The Company sells milk in bulk or in bottle that is PASTEURISED. If delicate people use other milk, they may meet a premature death. Use only the milk supplied by the
F.F. &I. Coy., and they will get healthy and strong. Strong robust people will grow the stronger. If, after this NOTE OF WARNING, you continue to use milk from cows suffering
from Tuberculosis—or, in plain words, Consumption—you deserve to die, for the fault rests with yourself. , , , • „ ,• ..,
The F.F. and I. Coy., Ltd., is the only Company who has an Inspector in its regular employ. If you want to rear a healthy family, deal ONLY with us ; but if you want to rear
weaklings, or be weak, then Don’t.
